THE RED PAGE.
Robert Bridges.
ROBERT BRIDGES, the feeble-pulsed
poet of faint airs, delicate odours,
and soft sylvan sweetness, was born
in English Kent in ’44 ; and to-day,
at 55, is settled in Berkshire. A retiring
physician (now retired), he waited till he
was forty ere marrying (with many pre-
liminary courtship tremors suggested in his
verse) Miss Mary Monica Waterhouse. For
long a dainty dish in privately-printed
pamphlets, his poems were collected last
year in two volumes—“ The Poetical Works
of Robert Bridges.”
They are two volumes of sighs and amorous
breathings for the Muse, who never fully em-
braced her timid worshipper. Always a little
too calm, perhaps a little too cultured, the poet
never gains or gives the highest ecstasy. He
is not Beauty’s master, but a page in Beauty’s
train, touching now her rustling skirt-hem,
and anon strewing flowers for her path—
lilies, and white rosebuds, and fragrant
woodland daisies. Decidedly a poet for the
few who deem themselves elect. And his
Australian analogue—almost homologue —is
Hubert Church, of Maoriland.
Bridges begins his “ Works ” by decking
Greek legends with English rhyme. He
relates the myth of “Prometheus the Fire-
Giver,” and tenderly tells the story of
“Er;>s and Psyche.” In the collection of
sonnets, regular and irregular, which he calls
“ The Growth of Love ” —Though, as in
Rossetti’s “ House of Life,” not all are ger-
mane with the title—isfound his most mettled,
most pithy and pqignant verse ; though not,
maybe, his most poetical. But Bridges’
poetry is not compound of pith and sun-
: strong mettle, but of sensitive fancy, delicate
allusion, and the moonbeam-stuff of Arcady.
He has wandered over Helicon without
quaffing Aganippe, and might say, in his
medical tongue, that his verse is a cooling
and healing lotion, not a fiery and inspiring
draught. Yet his shorter poems are truly,
if faintly, lyric, and most melancholy musical.
To a breath and a blossom he rightly likens
his song, and featly, and sweetly—
I have loved flowers that fade,
Within whose magic tents
Rich hues have marriage made
With sweet unmemoried scents:
A honeymoon delight,—
A joy of love at sight,
That ages in an hour: —
My song be like a flower !
I have loved airs, that die
Before their charm is writ
Along a liquid sky
Trembling to welcome it.
Notes, that with pulse of fire
Proclaim the spirit’s desire,
Then die, and are nowhere:—
My song be like an air !
Die, song, die like a breath,
And wither as a bloom:
Fear not a flowery death,
Dread not an airy tomb !
Fly with delight, fly hence !
’Twas thine love’s tender sens®
To feast; now on thy bier
Beauty shall shed a tear.
Or he will mirror Nature—
. . . these sweet-springing meads and bursting
boughs of May—
or the October landscape when—
Out of the golden green and white
Of the brake the fir-trees stand upright
In the forest of flame, and wave aloft
To tho blue of heaven their blue-green tuftings
soft.
But always you must read him aloud; for he
is an ear-poet, and learned in the English
music, and for music or to music many of
his shorter poems hare been written. And,
as his manner holds much of the best that is
called classic, it may be that by-and-by, like
the Elizabethans, he will be treasured by
the finer spirits of a far generation, though
he have only the post-Elizabethan sweet-
ness, and nothing of the Elizabethan might.
Yet the “Elegy on a Lady, whom Grief for the
Death of Her Betrothed Killed,” is a stately
measure not unworthy to move beside the
lamentation for Lycidas. And “ Love ! and
Be Joyous ! and Love ! ” is the essence of
all Bridges’ preachment—
Think not of what misfeaturas
Or cares the year may bring ;
But unto love
Resign your simple nature*.
To tender love.

Here « English Cobbett redivivus in S. A. Rosa,
Australian demagogue and grammarian ! With
Rosa, the revolutionary-orator, Southern-Australia
is more or less acquainted ; but the Rosauthor erf
■*“ Ungrammatical Statesmen : an Exposure of the
Bad English contained in the Australian Federal
Constitution,” is nova avis in terris austrulibus.
While other eyes have been fixed on the
spirit*of the Commonwealth Constitution, Rosa’s
have scanned its dry bones—and now he
rattles ’"'them with a> antriFederal ven-
geance ! And the naisehief of it is that
Rosa, with his eighteen Authorities Lom Chureh-
ill to Mason behind him — ,r An Austr[al]ian Army
Awfully Arrayed ’* —is right erften. Not always ;
for he has not yet risen to the eoneeption the*
Language is “ a living entity, no* a dead aggee-
gate,” and that last yeaA» grammar may be as
shabby in the seams as las* year’s elothes. And
by the might of Euphony he is unmoved. Stall —
bis pen touches, it pierces !
Saith Constitution § 71 —
The High Court shall consist of a Chief Justice, and ao
many other justices, not less than two, as the Parliament
prescribes.
So is wrong, says Rosa. It is at least awkward.
Not less should be no fewer, says Rosa. It should
—or not fewer. Prescribes should be may pre-
scribe, says Rosa. It should. Then the clause
would read—
The High Court shall consist of a Chief Justice and M
many other justices, not fewer than two, as the Parlia-
ment may prescribe.
Constitution §5l says Parliament shall have
power to make laws with respect to “ Invalid and
Old-Age Pensions.” This is ambiguous, says
Rosa. It is. It should be “ Pensions to Aged
Invalids” or “Pensions to Invalids and to Aged
Persons ” —whichever is meant.
But the critic fetters relative pronouns too
closely. “I heard it from the boy who takes
round the beer ” is better, not worse, than “ I
heard it from the boy that takes round the beer”;
because the first sentence is more perspicuous and
more euphonious. That is an indefinite word as
compared with the precise who and which, and
nowadays is used too frequently in their places.
The Constitution-framers frequently use in
respect of for in respect to or with respect to —mean-
ing “ with reference to.” Rosa says they are
wrong. And they are—though their fashion is
extending.
The Constitution says “the first five years”
(§ 93). Rosa says “ the five first years.” Dis-
honours are easy. “ During a period of five
years,” etc., evades the hypercritic.
§ 100. “ The Commonwealth shall not abridge
the right of a State to the reasonable use of the
waters of rivers for conservation or irrigation.”
Read, says Rosa, “to the reasonable use, for
conservation or irrigation, of the waters of rivers.”
Right again.
§ll4. “A State shall not ... or .. ." .§ll5.
“ A State shall not . . . nor ...” “The Conven-
tion spurns idle consistency,” says the Rosatirist.
And so on. “In conclusion,” ceclares Cobbett
11., “ there are very many more ” errors. Gene-
rally speaking, his indictment of the Constitution’s
English is sustained. And unless the Constitution
is to be a butt for schoolboys’ ridicule, the flaws
must be mended.

A recent reference to Kendall’s uncollected
verses has brought these two interesting and (said
to be) unpublished poems, with a letter to the
poet’s patron, R. D. Stenhouse.
Survey Offices,
Feb. 7th, 1866.
My dear Mr. Stenhouse,
I cannot manage much with “ Kathleen Aroon.”
The metre is perplexingly short, and moreover it
flies off, at right angles, from trochaic to iambic
feet. I must add also that the rhymes are as
legions. However, I send you two attempts in
the way of new words for a really lovely air. In
case you are at all pleased with them will you
kindly let me know. But do not pass judgment
till the lady of whom you were speaking has sung
them for you.
I am, my dear Mr. Stenhouse,
Faithfully yours,
Henry Khndall.
R. D. Stenhouse, Esq.
I have had a letter from one of the editors of
Fraser. He tells me I can write for that maga-
zine. This is very encouraging in the face of my
local disappointments.
Somebody in search of the longest word in Eng-
lish has found, in the “ Life of Archbishop Ben-
son” (p. 441), certain people alluded to as “Anti-
disestablishmentarians.” “ Would n’t ‘ mules ’ be
shorter (says somebody) and equally expressive ? ”
Robert Richardson adds a “Russell of the
Scotsman ” story—
Towards the close of Russell’s life, there was a scheme
among the Liberal party to present him with a substan-
tial sum of money in recognition of his great services to
the Liberal cause. Russell was in doubt as to whether he
should accept this form of reward for his work, and for
advice sought an old Highland friend, in whose judgment
he placed great reliance. The Highlander heard Russell’s
scruples, and then tendered him this advice: “Sandy,
my mon, gin they offer ye ten thousand, tak’ it, but gin
it’s less than ten thousand dinna’ tak’ it, and say ye
wouldna hae taken it gin it had been twenty thousand.”
Doggerel printed in Sydney—regarding which
the aut .or declares that “His Excellency Earl
Beauchamp has honoured me by transmitting
some copies (in gold type) to Mr. Chamberlain,
the Right Honourable the Secretary of State for
the Colonies, for presentation toller Most Gracious
Majesty.”
Gov. Beauchamp is said to have recently spoken
praise of The Bulletin for “ fostering Australian
literature.” But it remained for Gov. Beauchamp
to foster that.
Orthographical Evaoustes A. Phipson, from
London—
Taking “ ballerina ” as an Italian word, the plural is, of
course, “ ballerine," but it is surely sufficiently naturalised
to be treated as English with the plural “ ballerinas,’
otherwise you might equally urge that We should say
“ portict,” “ studl," etc. It is far better to give the
benefit of any doubt in favor of the Anglicised form,
especially in Australia, where fewer of the population
have the opportunity of talking French or Italian than
even in England. It is mere affectation to pronounce (or
rather attempt to pronounce) prestige, massage, envelope,
employee, baccarat, vase, renascent, fiance, accouchment,
basrelief, pomade, promenade, amateur, impass, restau-
rant, clientele, etc., as if they were French, and to write
others, where the sound is unaltered, in Italics or with
accents, as detour, role, hotel. As for dipdt, it is either
French, in which case it requires both accents, or elst
English, when it needs none ; and naive, naivety are just
as good English as gay and gaiety. You might also use
your influence to abolish the senseless and unpronounc-
able word vigneron, peculiar to Australia (how do the poor
people there try to say it ?), and reinstate the plain English
vintner. If you must have a foreign expression, much
better use the German winzer. There is no more reason
for saying tomahto than potahto, nor lahva than volcahno.
Wrongly spelt foreign words are cwragdo, mostaccio, mac-
cheroni, basso rilievo, guerrillero, schottisch, opera buffa,
while the meaningless phrase, “ It goes without saying,”
for “It needs no saying,” and “on the carpet ” for “ on
the (table) cloth,” are so idiotic that one would think even
the most hopeless snob would not use them.
Some correspondents—
Alone : Carlyle referred td conceptions, not facts. It
is quite possible to conceive “Infinity” in relation with
Space, and “ Eternity” in relation with Time ; though
we cannot conceive “ Eternity " and “ Infinity ” in them-
selves.
Syb. : Drink coffee as they used to drink it in the gay
French Ardennes—ten cups after dinner, each cup with
its name, to wit (1) Gafe, (2) Gloria, (3) Pousse Oaf 6, (4)
Goutte, (S) Re-goutte, (6) Sur-goutte, (7) Rinoette, (8)
Re-rincette, (9) Sur-rincette, (10) Coup de l’6trier. You
begin with pure coffee, and add a little more cognac to
every cup till you end with pure cognac. (This piece of
wisdom is not, as you might think, our own. It comes
from Brewer’s “ Dictionary of Phrase and Fable.”)
THE BOOKFELLOW.
DENIEHY'S LAMENT.
Air: “Kathleen Aroon."
Spirit of Loveliness ! Heart of my heart /
Flying so far from me, Heart of my heart /
Above the eastern hill, I know the red leaves thrill,
But though art distant still, Heart of my heart /
Sinning, I've searched for thee, Heart of my heart!
Sinning, I've dreamed of thee, Heart of my heart /
I know no end nor gain; amongst the paths of pain
I follow thee in vain, Heart of my heart 1
Much have I lost for thee, Heart of my heart /
Not counting the cost for thee, Heart of my heart /
Through all this year of years thy form as mist
appears,
So blind am I with tears, Heart of my heart /
Mighty and mournful now, Heart of my heart t
Cometh the Shadow-Face, Heart of my heart!
he friends I've left for thee, their sad eyes trouble
me —
I cannot bear to be, Heart of my heart!
* Intended to be a lament over his vain search after a
beautiful but impossible Ideal. How much his
Dream led him astray I need not say.
DENIEHY'S DREAM.
Air : “ Kathleen Aroon."
Just when the western light
Flickered out dim,
Flushing the mountain-side,
Summit and rim,
A last low lingering gleam
Fell on a yellow stream,
And then there cam « a dream,
Shining to him.
Splendours miraculous
Mixed with his pain
AU like a vision of
Radiance and rain !
He faced the sea, the skies ;
Old star-like thoughts did rise ;
But tears were in his eyes
Stilled in vain.
Infinite tokens */
Sorrows set free
Came in the dreaming wind
Far from the sea !
Fast years about him, trooped.
Fair phantoms round brim stopped,
Sweet faces o’er him drooped
Sad as could be/
“ This is our brother stow:
Sisters, deplore
Sian without purpose, hike
Ship without shore l
He tracks false fire,” one said,
“ j ßut weep you—he must tread
Whereto he may be led/
Lost evermorel”
“ Look,” said another,,
"Summit and slope
Bum, in the mountain-land—
Basement and cope /
Till daylight, dying dim,
Faints on the world's red rim,
We’ll tint this Dream for him
Even—with hope / ”
TOM DARING: A SOLDIER’S SONG.
Here but cold clay lies poor Tom Daring,
The bravest of our corps ;
We’ll miss his tall form and manly bearing
When peace shall reign once more.
True to his oath when he took the Queen’s shilling,
To serve, with devotion and love,
His Queen and his country, Tom ever was willing ;
And now he’s gone to serve above.
True to a fault, no care for to-morrow,
Honest, sober, and kind ;
Tom had a partner in joy and sorrow,
But now she’s left behind.
Tender and true she will always remember
Her Tom with his rough-hearted love ;
It may be in June, or it may be December,
One day she will join him above.
Like a brave soldier, Tom ever was ready
The bugle’s call to hear ;
Fearless of danger, in action steady,
The battle’s brunt to bear.
When the last bugle shall sound the great muster
To summon all ranks up above,
There we’ll find Tom with a crown of great lustre,
For bravery, devotion and love.
From "The Growth of Love.”
By HOBERT BRIDGES.
O flesh and blood, comrade to tragic pat.
And clownish merriment; whose sense could wait
Sermons in stones, and count death but an achat
All things as vanity, vet nothing vain :
The world, set in thy heart, thy passionate strain
Reveal’d anew; but thou for man didst make
Nature twice natural, only to shake
Her kingdom with the creatures of thy brain.
Lo, Shakespeare, since thy time nature is loti
To yield to art her fair supremacy ;
In conquering one thou hast so enriched both.
What shall I say ? for God—whose wise decree
Confirmetli all He did by all He doth—
Doubled His whole creation making thee.
My lady pleases me and I please her;
This know we both, and I besides know well
Wherefore I love her, and I love to tell
My love, as all my loving songs aver.
But what on her part could the passion stir,
Tho’ ’tis more difficult for love to spell,
Yet can I dare divine how this befel,
Nor will her lips deny it if I err.
She loves me first because I love her, then
Loves me for knowing why she should be loved,
And that I love to praise her, loves again.
So from her beauty both our loves are moved,
And by her beauty are sustain’d; nor when
The earth falls from the sun is this disproved.
In all things beautiful, I eanndt see
Her sit or stand, but love is stir’d anew:
’Tis joy to watch the folds fall as they do,
And all that comes is past expectancy.
If she be silent, silence let it be ;
He who would bid her speak might sit and sue
The deep-brow’d Phidian Jove to be untrue
To his two thousand years’ solemnity.
Ah, but her launched passion, when she sings,
Wins on the hearing like a sharpen prow
Borne by the mastery of its urgent wings :
Or if she deign her wisdom, she doth show
She hath the intelligence of heavenly tilings,
Unsullied by man’s mortal overthrow.
I care not if I live, tho ! life and breath
Have never been to me so dear and sweet.
I care not if I die, for .! could meet —
Being so happy—happily my death.
1 care not if 1 love ; to-day she saith
She loveth, and love’s history is complete.
Nor care I if she love me; at, her feet
My spirit bows entranced and worshippeth.
1 have no care for what was most my care,
But all around me see fresh beauty born,
And common sights grown lovelier than they
were:
I dream of love, and in the light of morn
Tremble, beholding all things very fair
And strong with strength that puts my strength
to scorn.
O Mil t mid ms divine sometimes I say :
Now let this word for ever and all suffice ;
Thou art insatiable, and yet not twice
Can even thy lover give his soul away :
And for my acts, that at thy feet I lay ;
For never any other, by device
Of wisdom, love or beauty, could entice
My homage to the measure of this day.
I have no more to give thee : 10, I have sold
My life, have emptied out my heart, and spent
Whate’er I had ; till like a beggar, bold
With nought to lose, I laugh and am content.
A beggar kisses thee ; nay love, behold, -
I fear not: thou too art in beggarment.
Since not the enamour’d sun with glance more fond
Kisses the foliage of his sacred tree,
Than doth my waking thought arise on thee,
Loving none near thee, like thee nor beyond ;
Nay since I am sworn thy slave, and in the bo*4
Is writ my promise of eternity,;
Since to such high hope thou’st encouraged me,
That if thou look but from me I despond ;
Sinoe tliou’rt my all in all, 0 think of this :
Think of the dedication of my youth :
Think of my loyalty, my joy, my bliss :
Think of my sorrow, my despair and ruth,
My sheer annihilation if I miss :
Think —if thou shouldst be false—think of Htj
truth.
Love that I know, love I am wise in, 1ot«,
My strength, my pride, my grace, my skill un-
taught,
My faith here upon earth, my hope above,
My contemplation and perpetual thought:
The pleasure of my fancy, my heart’s fire,
My joy, my peace, my praise, my happy theme,
The aim of all my doing, my desire
Of being, my life by day, by night my dream:
Love, my sweet melancholy, my distress,
My pain, my doubt, my trouble, my despair,
My only folly and unhappiness,
And in my careless moments still my care:
0 love, sweet love, earthly love, love divine,
Sayst thou to-day, O love, that thou art mine ?
Ah*tieavenly joy ! But who hath ever heard,
Who hath seen joy, or who shall ever find
Joy’s language ? There is neither speech nor
word;
Nought but itself to teach it to mankind.
Scarce in our twenty thousand painful days
We may touch something: but there live*—
beyond
The best of art, or nature’s kindest phase—
The hope whereof our spirit is fain and fond:
The cause of beauty given to man’s desires,
Writ in the expectancy of starry skies,
The faith which gloweth in our fleeting fires,
The aim of all the good that here we prize ;
Which but to love, pursue and pray for well
Maketh earth heaven, and to forget it, hell.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO WOOLGROWERS
111 New South Wales and Queensland.
The Dissolution of the Sydney Wool-selling Brokers’ Association by effluxion of time,
and our Refusal to join the Wool-selling Institute, enables this Company to Revise its
Charges on the Sale of Wool by Reducing same, and also Abandoning the CONSOLI-
DATED COMMISSION CHARGE, which, we have always contended, unnecessarily
Penalises the Woolgrower, as the BETTER the character of the Wool Grown and the
PRICE REALISED the HIGHER the CHARGE.
The following will be our Charges to the Grower :
COMMISSION I per Cent.
WAREHOUSING (including Receiving into Store, Fire Insur-
ance, Weighing, Lotting, Repacking Sample Bales, Adver-
tising, Cataloguing, etc.), on Wool realising over Gd. per lb.,
the charge will be ... ... ... ... ... 3s. 9d. per Bale.
On Wools realising Gd. and over 4d. per lb. ... ... ... 2s. Gd. per Bale.
On Wools realising 41b. per lb. and under ... ... ... Is. Gd. per Bale.
ON PRODUCE (other than Wool), viz., Skins, Hides, Tallow, etc., SPECIAL RATES will
be quoted on application.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An ingenuous Melbourne divorce-peti-
tioner said she didn’t object to keeping her hus-
band, but she kicked against paying him a salary
as well. Also, that she had always been so
anxious about him that she once took out a war-
rant for his arrest. Finally, she said she “ wanted
to get him out of her mind.” S’Onah obliged her.
Frederic Villiers’ sketch at the scene at
Paardeberg camp when Cronje surrendered to
Roberts, is an instance of the unreliability of
artists’ work as compared with the truthful camera.
Villiers draws the Lion of the Veldt as a giant
towering over his mannikin captor. Now, “Bobs,”
is small, but Cronje is only sft. high, though
broad enough for two men of his inches. *
The N.S.W. Unemployed Advisory Board,
after the manner of boards, has taken offices and
appointed a paid secretary. The Commission con-
sists of W. F. Schey, at £6 6s. a week ; Mr.
Brennan, representing the workers, at £4 4s. ; and
Rev. W. I. Carr-Smith, the Anglican rector of St.
James’, King-street, at a like figure. The fourth
member is the Acting-Director of N.S.W- Govt.
Charities, who, being a hard-worked official with
the responsibility of a large department on his
shoulders, is expected to do most of the work of
the Board in his spare time for nothing. «
Nemesis
Pm sick of the sight of a soldier;
I’m shamed in my country’s pride ;
The anger of Heaven is on her.
For her hands with blood are dyed.
The blood of brave men—women —
Who are fighting for hearth and home ;
Not all the seas can e’eanse her
Till a thousand years shill come.
This stain is on her honor,
This bane is in her blood;
The unseen Death shall haunt her
Till her pride has been subdued.
The weird things that in darkness
Wait on a people’s sin
Are not an old-world fable . . .
And the troops of God come in /
They into the city enter,
They move where none may see:
Woe, woe to priest and people
For this War’s iniquity !
For priest has joined with people
In the worship of this god,
And the Christ of Human Pity
They underfoot have trod.
Even in His holy places
They chant their heathen praise,
And pour their blood-libations
As in the ancient days.
• •
The Bethlehem fields are silent,
The Christmas angels gone:
The “ Christian ” bows to Dagon . . •
And the troops of God come on t
C.
Doubt.
Faintly I hear forgotten bells
Upon the mountain side, where dwells
The secret brook that poured for me
An oraele of days to be.
Ah ! bells that trembled holy joy
Into the soft heart of the boy—
Ah ! brook that whispered of a God,
Where’er you melted through the sod.
Come to me with your ancient dower,
Dew me with the mysterious power,
Restore the melody of faith
That once to me was more than death !
In vain I call—ye cannot tell,
And I no more from brook or bell
Take to my soul delicious rest,
And find a God within my breast!
M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.
There is a River.
There is a river—home of Moon,
And Sun, and Star, and Evening Mist;
Deep mirror of the Night and Noon,
A jasper pavement Ocean-kissed ;
The hand of Peace in silent rest
Light lieth on its moveless breast.
When Morning, like an infant smile,
Breaks on the dim face of the sky,
And walks with golden feet the aisle
Of jewell’d water, sob nor sigh
Comes with its breath ; but whirr of wing,
Bird-call, and wild-oak whispering.
And through the gasping Summer days
The river pool lies fresh and blue,
While hills are yellow in the haze
And smoke-banks hide the mountain view ;
The long days loiter sweet and cool
Along the river’s shining pool.
There winds a pathway on the shore
By glancing fern and swinging leaf,
Through incense sweet from forest store
Of musk, and moss, and deadwood sheaf ;
There Mavis pipes his joyous call,
And calm delight is over all.
Break, storms of Winter, gales of Spring !
Burn, Summer sun or Autumn fire !
For ever here the white-throats sing
Tlie sweetness of a world’s desire ;
Here Love a constant vigil keeps,
While the blue stream for ever sleeps.
Tas.
BARKIS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 9, 1900.
The New Sydney that is to be.
When New South Wales Parliament meets
a few days hence, and has sung “ God Save
the Queen ” and other irrelevancies, and
has shaken hands wildly with itself over the
Boer war, and has congratulated Premier
Lyne about his new bauble, and all the
necessary questions have been asked about
why something happened to Private Smith,
or how something didn’t happen to Private
Jones, and the usual vote of censure is
over, and George Reid has finished pointing
out for the 117th time that his deficits were
really surpluses, it is to be hoped that some-
thing sudden and strong will be done by way
of reforming the municipal government of
the N.S.W. metropolis. It will probably
need to be sudden if it is to be done at all.
The Plague appears to be passing away,
and the spasm of interest in municipal
affairs which was created by that bony
visitant seems to be passing also. If
Parliament had met two months ago
it would almost certainly have done
something that would have stood the awful
municipal system on its head in one act.
Now, the feeling that there is plenty of time
and the idea that old and mouldering insti-
tutions shouldn’t be lightly disturbed is
again beginning to assert itself. A lot of
valuable disrespect has evaporated within
the last month.
• *♦ * *
A little has been done meanwhile. A
municipal conference has sat, and has con-
sidered the question soberly and without
Billingsgate, in which respect it is quite un-
like some other municipal bodies, and has
drafted a highly-promising scheme for the
reformation of Sydney and its suburbs. The
principal features of the scheme (already
mentioned in this paper) are these :
(1) That Sydney, Alexandra, Annandale, Ashfield
Balmain, Botany, North Botany, Burwood, Canterbury,
Camperdown, Concord, Darlington, Drummoyne, Enfield,
Erskineville, Five Dock, Glebe, Hunter’s Hill, Hurstville,
Kogarah, Lane Cove, Leichhardt, Manly, Marrickville,
Marsneld, Mosman, Newtown, North Sydney, Paddington,
Petersham, Randwick, Redfern, Rockdale, Rookwood,
Ryde, St. Peters, Strathfield, Vaucluse, Waterloo,
Waverley, Willoughby and Woollahra (42 in all) shall be
absolutely amalgamated under one Council.
(?) Council to consist of 75 members. Area to be
divided according to population into 25 wards with three
aldermen apiece.
(3) Whole council to be re-elected every three vears,
every adult who has been for six months a tenant in the
m ™ l< = l P al ar ea to have one vote. No plural voting.
(4) Mayor to be elected annually in the same way as the
aldermen.
(5) Rates to be levied on land values only.
(6) Council to have power to seize upon and sell or let
property to recover arrears of rates.
(7) Railway Commissioners, Gas Co., Hydraulic Power
Lo-, telephone Department, electric lighting companies
like to pay rates for the use of the city streets.
(8) Council s accounts to be subject to annual Govern-
ment audit, and any deficiency certified to by the auditor
5’, overe . < ? nexfc year by a special deficiency rate.
(9) Council to solely control : Roads and streets, licens-
ing of vehicles and control of street traffic, water-works,
water-supply, sewerage and drainage, gas-supph r , supply
of electricity, hydraulic power, tramways, docks, ferries,
nre-stations, prevention of fires on land and in harbor,
maikets, parks and open spaces, cemeteries, free libraries,
common lodging-houses, night-shelters, public health,
destruction of garbage and house-refuse, abattoirs, public
baths and wash-houses, housing of the poor, public pawn-
offices, municipal art-galleries ; and have power to acquire
and carry on any of these services, and in addition have
power to resume slum areas, improve and widen streets,
dedicate parks, and acquire land for other public purposes,
to re-sell or lease any portion thereof ; to issue deben-
tures and negotiate loans for the purpose of defraying
expenses ; to have power to proclaim improvement
areas, and to impose betterment taxes within such
a ™ as ! t° establish labor bureaux and registration-
othces To establish a metropolitan statistical depart-
ment. To compel registration of ownership of land. To
license hotels, wine-shops, and places of public amuse-
ment. To regulate and test weights and measures. To
regulate and license noxious trades. To regulate and
license dairies and milk-supply. To license auctioneers and
a , trade licenses, and exercise all powers exercised
within the area by the Board of Health, City. Improve-
ment Board, Metropolitan Transit Commission, Tramway
Department, Trustees of Barks, such branches of the
Harbors and Rivers Department as control the foreshores
and docks, such branches of the Roads and Bridges
Department as control roads and bridges within the area,
such branches of the Public Works Department as control
the design and execution of works of water supply and
sewerage within the area, and all powers exercis-
able both within and without the area by the Water and
Sewerage Board and Fire Brigades Board.
(hl) AsH(‘t.s and liabilities of the whole 42 municipalities,
and the estimated deficiency of yearly revenue from trans-
services, to be determined by an independent
commission, and be taken over by the council; for a period
of ten years from the establishment of the council the
Government to grant it an annual subsidy equal to such
estimated deficiency.
(11) Council to have power to issue debentures for the
conversion of existing liabilities, and for the due exercise
of the various powers vested in it.
(12) All Crown lands within the area not Immediately
required for Government purposes to be vested in the
council.
* * * *
The scheme above outlined is possibly not
perfect—nothing is quite perfect—but it is,
by many thousand miles, the best that has
been offered to the misgoverned city for fifty
years past. Only three notable objections
present themselves. It is an open question,
in the first place, whether the aldermen
who are to govern Greater Sydney, when
there is such a place, shouldn’t be
fewer in number and whether they
shouldn’t be paid for their services.
If they numbered 25 instead of 75, then the
new city could give them £2OO a-year each
at a cost of only £5OOO per annum, and by
all past experience it would be money well
spent. The innumerable municipalities of
Sydney have had many years’ experience of
the gratis alderman, and the N.S.W. capital
and its environs are a huge monument to his
incapacity. As a rule the service which is
given for nothing is worth just that amount,
or perhaps a little less j in Sydney’s case it
has generally been worth a good deal less.
A city’s chance of being well-managed by
unpaid bosses is just about as great as a
bank s chance of being well-managed by un-
paid managers and directors. The gratis
alderman has failed miserably for something
like 50 years, and it would be a fair
thing now to give the other kind of
alderman a show. Further, though it is
proposed that Greater Sydney shall have
abundant borrowing powers, it doesn’t
seem to be specified that every loan
shall have a sinking fund of 1 per cent,
per annum attached—and it should be
so specified. Also, it is lamentable to
find that one of the first ideas of the reform
party is to bail up the Government for a
subsidy. Almost every good and bad and
indifferent public or semi-public movement
in Australia is disfigured by the pauper ten-
dency to cry to the Government for cash
assistance in the first place, and in the
second place, and in the third place, and in
every other possible place. Yet with all
drawbacks the scheme of the municipal con-
ference is an admirable one.
* • • *
And already there is a bitter howl against
it in the marble halls of Proputty, and un-
less the people wake to an intelligent in-
terest in the matter the dead-weight of Pro-
putty may yet flatten it out. The present
N.S.W. municipal franchise is mainly a pro-
perty franchise. The landlord has elected
the various councils and filled the seats
therein ever since municipal government
began. He is responsible for the serpentine
alleys which in Sydney are called main streets,
for the dirt, for the debt, for the deficits,
for the absence of enterprise, for the shore-
less wastes of Billingsgate which prevent
municipal business being done, and for
the shiftlessness and purposelessness of
things in general. He has had 50 years
trial and been found wanting—also dirty,
bankrupt, incapable, and coarse in his
language, likewise paralysed in the nose so
that he fails to smell his own property. And
he demands a continuance of the present
system on the ground that the proposal to
give every adult dweller in the city a vote is
a public wrong ; no one should have a vote
except the person “ responsible for the pay-
ment of the rates.” This good old lamenta-
tion was bound to crop up in any case—yet
the landlord doesn’t pay the rates, except
those on the house he occupies himself, and
then he only pays in his capacity as a tenant.
In all other cases he adds rates on to the
rent and passes them on to the tenant,
and the tenant passes part of them on to
the boarder or sub-tenant, if he has one.
In normal times rents are fixed at a figure
which will give the landlord a fair return for
his money, after paying rates, taxes, repairs,
and insurance, and making allowance for
depreciation. Sometimes he gets more than
a fair return ; sometimes less ; hut he gets a
fair return on the average. The landlord,
as a benevolent person vyho, with no hope or
prospect of return, pays the rates on whole
streets of houses out of his own pocket and
at his own loss, is an exhausted fiction. He
pays these rates out of his rents, and he
makes the rents high enough to cover the
rates and still leave him the current rate of
interest on his money. Therefore, the
tenant and sub-tenant and lodger really pay,
and they should have votes accordingly.
Only ratepayers should have votes, and they
are the ratepayers. The landlord is a rate-
payer for the house he personally inhabits,
and for it only. As regards the others, he
is an agent—and often a very dishonest one
—for the collection of municipal taxes.
* • * *
Moreover, the tenants have other claims.
They have to live in the landlord’s city
whether they like it or not; a man can’t
choose his own city, but must go where a
chance of victuals and a roof presents itself.
They have to look at his city, and to smell it
just as much as he does. They have to catch
diseases in it—the diseases the dirty land-
lord has left lying about. They stand the
mental shock of its uncomeliness ; they get
run over in its narrow streets because the
landlord didn’t make them wider ; they are
kept awake by its dogs, and they die of its
drains. As the people who indirectly pay
the rates, they pay the interest on the city
debts which the landlord has accumulated.
As subscribers to the general revenue, they
pay interest on the previous Sydney city
debt of £700,000 or so, which the landlords
accumulated many years ago, and which they
induced the central Government to take
over. And under all these conditions, the
absolute right of every habitual dweller in
the metropolis to have a vote at municipal
elections, whether he is a landlord, a tenant
householder, a person who rents an office, a
lodger in a private house, boarding-house,
hotel or coffee-palace, is indubitable. They
are all rate-payers even though they may
not hump the taxes personally round to the
Town Hall. For that matter even the land-
lord seldom does that, but leaves it to his
office-boy, and on the Fatman’s logic the
office-boy is the only properly-qualified voter
in the crowd.
9 • * *
All this is by way of leading up to the
question which really stands in the way of
radical reform. The Municipal Conference
has drawn up what, despite some small de-
fects, is really an admirable municipal cons-
titution. The Bulletin proposes that as
soon as Parliament meets it should be put
in the form of a Bill and submitted for
acceptance or rejection to the vote of the real
ratepayers of the 42 municipalities concerned
—that is, to the vote of all the adult resi-
dents of, say, six months’ standing, giving
them one vote each. The proposal most
commonly advanced is that it should be re-
ferred to the municipal councils, representing
only the handful of landlords who have
usurped the franchise to the exclusion of the
great mass of real ratepayers. And in re-
ferring it to the Councils to decide whether
a complete reversal of the existing system is
needed it is really proposed to ask these
bodies a series of questions about themselves
and their constituents, somewhat after this
fashion :
(1) Are you a complete failure ?
(2) Do you admit that it will be better for Sydney and
all connected therewith if you are abolished, and 41 out
of your 42 mayors and town-clerks get the “ sack ?”
(3) Do you consider that you and your handful of con-
stituents have usurped the franchise which really belongs
to the people at large ?
(4) Are you of opinion, as a body of landlords, repre-
senting landlords, that it is necessary in the public
interest that the control of building and sanitary laws
should be placed in the hands of the people at large as the
only means of making your properties reasonably clean
and not so unsightly from age, neglect and want of taste
as to be a curse and an eyesore ?
(5) Is it your conviction that the shortest way out of
the financial muddles you have created is to fire you
ignominiously into the back yard, and let a perfectly new
institution take the matter in hand ?
(6) Do you, in short, recognise your utter hopelessness
collectively and individually and the hopelessness of the
system which created you ?
» * » •
The opinion of this paper is that if muni-
cipal reform is to wait until all the 42
councils realise their own incapacity it will
wait a very long time. Also, there is a
grievous want of logic in the idea that,
because a few landlords have for many years
deprived the bulk of the ratepayers of the
franchise to which they are fairly entitled,
therefore the bulk of the ratepayers should
continue to be disfranchised unless the rob-
ber minority consents to surrender its privi-
leges. There may be circumstances in which
it is necessary to have the consent of the
robber minority, but this isn’t one of them.
Even assuming that the 42 councils will con-
sent in the end (they probably will in the end,
but the end is always the last thing arrived
at) the waiting is a foolish waste of
time. Dirt accumulates fast, and so do debt
and prejudice ; and the plague isn’t dead
yet; and the Lyne Government, to which
Sydney pins its hopes (having found by sad
experience that a Reid Government does
little save glare affably through its eye-glass)
isn’t immortal. A municipal referendum
offers a chance of getting a new municipal
constitution at work and a Greater Sydney
established this year, and a country which
only took one week to rush into a foolish
war can’t be blamed for too-great velocity
if it gets a wise reform started in seven
months.
The Public Interest and “Contempt
of Court.”
After all, the Public Interest comes first. Jus-
tice and the administration of justice are merely
means to the end of the Public Interest. And
that is itself a legal maxim which says that the
supreme law is the well-being of the people : a
‘"Supreme Court” yields to the supreme law.
All truisms ; but practice often makes light of
theoretical truisms. And in the contempt-of-court
rule recently laid down by Chief-Justice Darley in
the (N.S. W.) case of Kugelmann v. Norton, it ap-
pears to The Bulletin that “ the dignity of the
Court ” was upheld at the expense of the Public
Interest which the Court is established to serve.
• * • •
Kugelmann, “medicalherbalist,” sued Norton,
a Sydney journalist, for libel. After an excep-
tionally long trial, the jury disagreed. The Press
was prohibited by the contempt-of-court rule from
commenting on the case during its trial; though
the case held many points upon which Press com-
ment, in the Public Interest, was desirable—even
necessary. When the jury disagreed, Norton
considered the case closed, and published certain
comments. Kugelmann, having still the right
to ask for re-trial, protested; and N.S. Wales
Supreme Court decided that Norton was guilty
of “ contempt. It does not appear that
Kugelmann intends to press for re-trial. His
counsel, indeed, suggested that he has no in-
tention of asking for re-trial “ this year.” So
that for eight months or more the public Preas is
prevented from commenting in the Public Interest
upon a notorious suit which involves matters of
groat importance to the public. The result
is that the right of public comment on the
administration of justice in this case be-
comes practically worthless. By the time
Kugelmann lias decided whether or not he
will have a re-trial, the case and the evidence will
be forgotten ; the publio attention will lie directed
to current matters; and comment will lose its
public value. It is easy to see that this prevention
of public comment until publio curiosity has died
out may be the main object of a party to any such
case. If he be dependent on the public for a
living, and know that he has been shown guilty of
equivocal practices, it is of the highest moment to
him that the l’ress be hindered from pointing out
his fault until the matter is ancient history. Thus
advantage may be taken of Supreme Court rules
to shelter guilt from public reprobation ; and
crime may be shielded by the very institution of
justice, established, in the Public Interest to put
an end to crime.
• * *
It is difficult to legislate for “ of
court,” and on some grounds it is well that the
Judge’s jurisdiction in this matter should be sum-
mary, even arbitrary. But this arbitrary juris-
diction can be permitted only as long as a Judge
is able to show that it subserves the Public In-
terest. In The Bulletin’s opinion, the rule laid
down in the Kugelmann-Norton case was notin
the Public Interest. Probably, while the jury-
system prevails, it is better that Press
comment should not be permitted before
or during a trial; though in an ideal community
it would make no difference to an honest and in-
telligent jury. But the disagreement of a jury is
essentially that jury’s verdict just as much as a
verdict of Guilty or Not Guilty. The old Scottish
form of Not Proven praotically admits that dis-
agreement is a verdict. And the disagreement
should be taken as ending a trial for the purposes
of Press comment. It should not be permitted to
either side to prevent comment until comment is
fruitless. As between the Press and the Court,
the Court has taken its due precedence; but it
should not be permitted to exclude the Press from
entry to the case. If Justice have been delivered
of a still-bom child, it is nevertheless a birth, and
news for public advertisement and comment.
And if the suitors who begot the child are
fearful of the influence of comment upon the
mother, let them wait till the comment is for-
gotten. The public has given way to private in-
terests before and during the trial ; let private
interests give way to public after the trial—even
if the verdict is a verdict of Not Proven. It is
intolerable that, in a case like the Kugelmann-
Norton case, comment should be silenced for
half-a-year and more—until the case for comment
is lost to memory. And it is not in the Public
Interest.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Swollen Militarism in Australia.
The limitless “ glory ” lavished on an Australian
manifestation of “military spirit” lias quickly
brought an example of what is to be expected by
a nation which causelessly nourishes “military
spirit.” On Thursday, May 31, a “small army”
of N.S.W. artillerymen and Third Contingent
privates—as the reporter describes them—a “com-
pact band,” “ marching in excellent order,” came
through Sydney streets to avenge a quarrel
between an artilleryman and a larrikin, in which
the artilleryman got the worst of it. This new
contingent started from the military headquarters
at Victoria Barracks, just as previous contingents
had started ; but this time the force was directed
against the men’s own countrymen. They marched
out determined to break the peace by assailing the
members of the “ push” to which the victorious lar-
rikin belonged. And theymarched in uniform, with
side-arms ; though the weapons which they used
were luckily only belts, sticks, and whips. The
“push” declined encounter; but the city police,
in face of such an organised body of men, was
for a long time powerless. A riot followed, in
which several policemen and civilians un-
connected with the “push” were more or
less severely injured. For two hours and
more the central city was in tumult. At last
the “soldiers” were persuaded to return to bar-
racks. One of them was arrested, and—fined
£5 ! —as a ringleader in an organised riot ! The
military authorities apparently did nothin/ 1 ; with
the result that on Saturday, June 2, two new
forces of some “40 artillerymen, under a ser-
geant,” and “40 members of the Third Con-
tingent, similarly officered,” with other smaller
parties, marched again into the city and re-
peated the riot. This time the results were less
serious; but “bottles and stones flew,” a police-
man was injured, and “a band of artillerymen
and their allies ” chased a civilian into an hotel
and thrashed him with a belt. These edifying
performances are the first fruits of the swollen
“ military spirit ” in Australia, which needs small
encouragement to form an idle caste defying the
law, as elsewhere. Let the workers of Australia
take warning.


Victoria’s Lunacy Department.
A simple little remark by the Vic. Govt. Me lical
officeduring the recent trial of a woman ior the
rnurd jr of her child, whilst temporarily insane
some three years ago, was more severe upon the
methods of Vic. Governments than anything that
has been said since the days of the boom. The
witness’s observation was to the effect that, during
these thi'ee years, the woman had been kept, not
in a hospital for the insane, but in Pentridge and
Melbourne gaols. And the reason given for thus
gaoling an unconvicted and untried person itself
constitutes a most scathing indictment upon the
State —viz., that she would have a better chance
to recover in gaol than in the asylum. This was
possibly a good reason from the woman’s point of
view, but—what of the unfortunates in the asy-
lums? A glance at the statistical returns for the
best few years forces the conclusion that Victoria
has discovered a wondrously successful method of
manufacturing idiots wholesale. These returns
show that not only is insanity increasing to such
an extent that the province has now nearly the
largest percentage of cranks in the world, but,
more significant and mo e shameful, the per-
centage of recoveries is steadily decreasing—from
54 per cent, in ’B2 to 28 per cent, in ’97, and the
percentage of deaths is just as steadily increasing,
—from 6.92 in ’B9 to 8.84 in ’9B. Moreover it is
not that the increase is universal or even general
in Australasia ; N. S. Wales and Queensland have
a steady recovery-percentage and a decreasing
death-percentage, while other provinces show up
better still. The province of booms is now begin-
ning to reap as it has sown. For years it has
utilised its gaols as poor-houses and by its
solitary system turned many of its crimiry'ls into
lunatics, and apparently it is now making a study
of the simplest way of getting rid of these
by an asylum system which promptly hustles the
permanent lunatic into a grave. The charge
made by medical authorities against the Vic.
Lunacy Department is that it has practically given
up attempting to cure demented patients, and has
got back to the old, crude idea of just shutting up
the insane in a large building till they die or re-
cover of their own volition. As a casual piece of
evidence in support of this accusation Victoria, a
few weeks ago, learnt by accident that a perfectly
sane man had been locked up in one of its
asylums on the mere unchecked certificate
of two doctors. He only got out by acci-
dent, for the place was one for the deten-
tion, not the cure, of lunatics, and there was
apparently no expert on hand who managed
to recognise this sane man when he saw him. The
place was seemingly run on the broad principle
that the fact of a man being in sufficiently proved
that he ought to be in. Many ugly things wore
said then about the Victorian lunacy system, but
after all it is never likely to have any harder re-
mark made about it than that quoted above.
When a friendly Judge or magistrate sends a
lunatic to gaol in order that she may have a better
chance of reoovery than in the asylum, ho has
made about as violent a comment as the British
language is oapable of.

Law in a Hurry. Jaded Judges and
Bounding Jurymen.
The spurts of the law frequently produce as much
wrong as the law’s delays. The late-sitting J t;dge
and t ie fagged counsel and jury are responublo
for as many judicial murders as the reward-hunt-
ing detective, and Australia can cite numerous
cases of the kind in her own experience. But the
judge is not always at fault. We have sometimes
the case of the late-sitting juror and the fagged
judge and counsel. A ease in point, fortunately
not attended with the most serious consequences,
occurred at last Melb. criminal sittings during the
second trial of Mrs. Downey for “constructive”
murder. The court had been bard at work from
10 to 5 upon a most difficult case involving the
consideration of troublesome points of law—about
the reception of evidence and the hearing of
technical medical evidence and the stage
had been reached at which Purves, Q.C.,
had to begin his defence. The Judge was pro-
ceeding to adjourn the Court and mentioned the
arrangements for locking up the Jury for the
night, when one juryman got up and calling
attention to the fact that he had been down from
the country a week, asked the Court to finish the
case that night. Purves, of course, could only
consent with a sickly smile, for it wouldn’t have
done for him to endanger even hypothetic-
ally the chances of his client by holding out for an
adjournment. And when both counsel and jurymen
were enthusiastic for a night of it, the Judge was
also helpless, however jaded he might personally
feel. As a consjquence, a good half-day’s work,
consisting of addresses of counsel for defence and
prosecution, the reading of a long statement of the
accused and the Judge’s summing up, was crowded
into a little over an hour. The jurymen retired,
disagreed, and were discharged at 11.15 p.m., half
an-hour or more before they should have been
discharged according to law. The juror
from the country no doubt has his rights,
but the interests of his business are of
merely tinpot consequence besides those of a
person whose chance of life depends upon the
vigor and freshness of her advocate, the clearness
of the Judge in his summing up, and the wakeful-
ness of the other jurymen. In such cases as the
above, it might well happen that the desire to
catch the last suburban train would be the real
cause that would send a new victim to the gallows.
As it is, the accused woman, who had already
gone through the fearful ordeal of a trial which
had resulted in the judgment of death being pro-
nounced upon her, and who had to go to
great expense and trouble to get it quashed in
the Full Court, has endured a second ordeal
without the release from mental torture which
she might very conceivably have had if
counsel, Judge and juryman were not tired
and sleepy with overwork. Of course, it
might have been otherwise, but the probabilities
are that the hurry of that juryman was in some
measure responsible for the necessity that has
now arisen of sending a woman the third time
before a jury on the same charge, and that a capi-
tal one. Purves admitted, when starting his
address to the jury, that he hadn’t had time to
look at the notes that had been taken for him
during the trial, and would have to depend
on his memory. The case is a good one to
illustrate the need there is for a stringent applica-
tion of the Early Closing Act, not only to Judges,
but even to jurymen whose private interests are
more important than accused persons’ lives.
Brother Alick and Brother John.
Brother Alice Forrest (brother to Brother
John Forrest) is understood to be principal
owner of Perth (W.A.) Morning Herald, and
the language of Brother Alice’s paper about
Brother John, since the latter ceased to be an
incurable parochialist, is dreadful in the ears of
any right-minded person. Brother Alice’s paper
calls Brother John a .Judas, a “pitiable spec-
tacle,” a weak kind of man, and such-like, and
otherwise presents a painful example of dissension
in families. Still, Brother Alice’s paper has
been cabin" Brother John bad names in a slow,
purposeless sort of way for a long time, and hasn’t
broken anything. Possibly it is only meant to
show the noble independence of both brothers.
The Perth Morning Herald is still crying out
for that amended draft Federal Constitution which
was pitched up by a Westralian Parliamentary
committee some time ago, and which died young
in the Parliamentary waite-paper basket. “We
in Westrali i,” moans the Herald,' “know
what we want, and that, apparently, we can-
not get owing to the culpable weakness of
our delegates, past and present, and toe extraor-
dinary volte-face of a Premier who, to a 1 appear-
ance, is bent on wrecking the record and reputa-
tion of an honored lifetime.” “What we want,”
in this case, is, of course, the proposed amended
Bill which the We pnlian people never accepted,
which the Westralian Assembly merely accepted
so far as to agree that both Bills should go to the
people together, and which the Westralian Coun-
cil indignantly kicked out on a very cursory in-
spection. Brother Ala r’s paper always speaks
of this kicked and bruised and condemned measure
as something that is dear to the hearts of the
people, and of Westralia as a naked infant sitting
up in a tub of water that won’t be happy till it
gets it. And the “ extraordinary volte-face ”of
Brother John lies in the fact that he offers to let
the peo 1: say whether they want the existing
Federal Bill or not, and if, as Brother
Alice’s paper alleges, they don’t, and the only
Bill they want is their own amended one, they
will presumably at the polls that
they are not taking any of the present Bill, and
thereby end the whole matter. The wild cries of
Brother Alice’s paper (1) that the people are
dead against the Convention Bill, and (d) that
Brother John is a traitor for desiring to give
them a chance of proving that they are against it,
are two very interesting shrieks. If the Brother
Alice party had a shred of confidence in its own
assertions that Westralia is against the Conven-
tion Bill, it .might rather be expected to be glad
that Brother John is trying to give it a chance of
coming out into the open at the Referendum and
winning a great victory instead of skulking behind
a boulder and shooting adjectives. In one way and
another the row, or professed row, between
Brother Alice and Brother John is rathor
amusing.

Hogs in Council.
During the recent plague-spasm, hard-up citizens
were stopped from raking over the garbage in
Sydney streets. Now the aldermen rake it over
themselves in Sydney Council. But while the
deadbeats wore hogs by necessity, some of the
aldermen seem to be hogs from choice. From its
Civic Council Sydney expects incompetency. In
the management of civic affairs Sydney has grown
accustomed to corruption. Emboldened by im-
punity for blunders and worse, the Council does
not even take pains to decently cloak its disgraces.
A bear-garden is too complimentary a simile for
the Council meeting reported in Sydney Tele-
graph of May 31st. It was a blaokgarden. Here
are specimens of the language used in open
Council—
“ This man ought to be sandbagged ! ”
“ You are like an empty tin-can.”
“ That blasted ! ” (another alderman).
These choice expressions are given as uttered by
Aid. Norton in the printed report—
Aid. Norton remarked that Alderman Dymock was fast
justifying his appellation of “donkey Dymock.”
There were loud cries of “ Order, order,” and Alderman
Norton withdrew liis remauk.
Aid. Dymock made a reference to Alderman Norton’s
“ bombastic talk.” lie might as well, he said, call him
“ dirty, smutty Norton,” but he would not lower himself
to do it.
Worthy, dignified, representatives these of the
chief municipal body of the mother city of Aus-
tralia ! Presently there was a dispute as to the
Mayor’s ruling, and the Mayor left the chair.
Then the reporter—
Aids Small and Norton became involved in conflict.
The expression, “ J will give you a punch in the jaw,”
flhat d sweetly out from the din.
Later on, the position of City Organist Wikgand
—a man who, by testimonials and performance,
ranks among the first organists in the world—was
discussed. The discussion was chiefly conducted
in the spirit of the dunghill-rooting cock fabled to
have found a diamond, and to have spurned it with
the remark that all the diamonds in the world
were not worth one grain of corn—■
Aid. Norton confessed that he could not understand
the intricate symphonies and fantasies which M. Wieoand
played. They irritated him. He had seen “ a lot of those
nice, superfine, nasty people pretending to understand
this wonderful fantastic music, but they did not under-
stand it.”
Aid. Penny, butcher and Justice of the P@ace, ex-
pressed his opinion :
He contended that there was plenty of local talent. He
confided to the meeting that M. iViegand's high-clans
music “ nearly mal t me vomit,'' There were plenty of
local musicians to sat'sfy the musical taste o( the people,
without bringing foreigners into the country. He hoped
that the next organist would he an Australian—either
English, Irish, or Scotch—-not a foreigner.
The irony of it! The local butcher and J.P.
sitting in judgment on the merits of a musician
with a Euro >ean reputation, and telling how a
butcher’s gorge rises at “ M. Wiegand’s high-class
music.” And these are some of Sydney’s civic
governors—Sydney, “queen-city of the South!”
And the reporter gives only their “printable”
language, with the assurance that some of it was
unprintable ! The Bulletin is quite aware that
there are worse things than hog-language and a
hogmeat-tastc in music. Probably the city funds
are safer in the hands of adjectival Aid. Nor-
ton and adjectival Aid. Dymock and nearly-
vomited Aid. Penny than they would be in
the hands of Sydney Telegraph’s desired nominee-
commissioners. It is the publicity of the elected
City Council—the very publicity which enables
us to see how disgracefully its business is con-
ducted—which is the safeguard. Nominee com-
missioners may conduct their business with more
deference to courteous Chesterfield, but less to
arithmetical Cocker. And they will conduct
their business in private. No one knows to what
scheme they may commit the unwitting rate-
payers. And even if interregnum-commissioners
are paragons of honesty and wisdom, they will just
about learn their business by the time the Greater
Sydney Council is created —or their existence will
be made a reason for postponing the Greater'
Sydney Council.' No ; we do not want three sets
of policy clashing in the city. Is the Sydney
Council so bad that we cannot wait for Greater
Sydney ? Then do not wait; but hasten Greater
Sydney at once.
The per cent. Standard.
The inclination to spend loan money in building
small, unprofitable branch railways, which cursed
A ictoria in the Gillies days, is again asserting it-
self. The loudest discussion just at present is over
a proposed fragment to connect the Geelong-Port
Fairy railway with Beech Forest. There are
already a number of noil-paying little cul-de-sacs
branching out from this railway. The Wensley-
dale, Forrest, Timboon, Mortlake and Beaac
branches—all the live brandies from this luckless
line—fail to quite cover working expenses. As
for the interest on cost of construction, it is the
extra-deadest kind of a dead loss. And now the
Government is being urged to build another cul-
de-sac in the same locality, from Colac to Beech
Forest, and not being a very strong Government
its two bosses—the kindly man who is gone in the
feet and the shrill man who never quite arrived
in the head show signs of giving way.
The line would undoubtedly do good to
that part of the country, and it might be
made to pay expenses. On the other hand
there is no hope that it can be made to pay any
interest for a long, long time to come, and the 3
or 3| per cent, on borrowed money will be a dead
loss. The two-headed Government doesn’t want
to retard the progress of the country. Neither
does it want to add to the railway deficit. So the
Government hesitates and staggers from side to
side in its mind. There are many railways yet to
be built, even in much-railwayed Victoria, which
would do a lot of good to the country, but there
isn’t one of them that can be relied upon to pay
3} per cent, on borrowed money, so to build any
of them means making more difficulties for the
Treasury, and not to build them means retarding
the progress of the country. The visible remedy
is: Where a line will do material good to the
country, and is really wanted, but can’t bring
in enough direct income to pay interest on
borrowed money, to build it out of revenue.
T lare are scores of districts where lines could be
pro.itably built out of revenue to open up the
country, and increase its produce and its popula-
lation, and to find work for the unemployed while
the building operations were going on, and more
work for them afterwards on the land thus opened
up, and to make things boom generally through
the increased circulation of money—but as tiicse
lines won’t pay per cent., and •money can’t be
borrowed for less, and no-one dreams of doing
anything without borrowing, the boom never
corns; off. It is the borrowing principle that is
retar ling the progress of Australia. Everything
has to be measured by the 3| per cent, standard.
If a great idea can’t be dragged out by any pro-
cess of dislocation to the 3| per cent, length then
it ceases to be a groat idea.
“ The Crown’s ” Perjured Witnesses.
That charming Penny Novelette story of the
bad man and the will and the fortune, also the dead
girl who was to be personated, as told to the Mel-
bourne police the other day, fell to pieces sadly
when investigated in the cold light of the criminal
court. The girl, the chief Crown witness, ad-
mitted that she had gone with accused into
Carlton Gardens at night, and had allowed him —
a perfect stranger —to put a mark on her bare arm
and also on her leg ; also that she had walked out
of the courting ground with his arms round her
neck, passing a policeman just outside the gate.
It didn’t strike her that it would be as well to
mention the alleged assault to that gentleman, nor
did she oven toll her elder sister when she got
home. She first mentioned the matter to a cer-
tain Jimmy presumably her best boy. Judge
1 tool) told the jurymen that they had either to be-
lieve the girl’s story or the man’s. And the twelve,
without much consideration, acquitted the man.
Truly, the damsel with the indecent story,
and, in fact, the Crown witness generally,
is getting into bad repute in Victoria.
Only a few days previous to this, in a
similar case at Geelong, the Crown Prosecutor
had expressed a strong disinclination to goon with
the charge, as prosecutrix had proved to be a
deliberate and daring liar. Of course, the jury
promptly acquitted. But the C.L. authorities
still adhere to their old rule of never prosecuting
their own perjured witness—even when her case is
so bad that they have to publicly admit that she is
a hopeless liar, who is trying to swear away the
life or liberty of an innocent man. Therefore, the
Crown’s perjured witness still flourishes, and the
Crown is responsible. In fact, the Crown, by
practically annulling the perjury laws in her
favor, is her accomplice—the worst kind of accom-
plice.

“ We are Broke.”
Sydney Telegraph, in a recent leading article,
thus implored Australia not to think of becoming
a self-supporting community, or of paying its own
way, or keeping decently solvent and self-reliant:
The Times is perfectly right when it says that the flow
of .British capital would he far less free if this right
(of appeal to the Privy Council) ceased to exist; ana if
that how were arrested, Australian progress would, to a
far greater extent than we may recognise, be arrested with
it. Mr. Lyne may plan the resumption and reconstruction
of the wharves of Sydney, and city railways, a North
Shore bridge, and the Federal Parliament may design a
handsome capital at Bombala, or elsewhere, but without
the co-operation of the British capitalist such enterprises
would become veritable chimeras.
Thgn what is the good of all the brag about the
wealth and prosperity of Australia if it can’t even
raise half-a-million of its own to build a bridge
with, and must either borrow the money from
England or do without the bridge ? What also is
the good of boasting that these provinces have the
biggest revenue per head in the world, that they
have 23 £ millions of gold in their banks, that there
is a vast amount of capital here “seeking an out-
let,” that Australia is solvent and progressive, and
that its mines turn out £14,000,000 worth of gold
annually, and all the rest ? Also what on earth is
tlm use of telling England that this country can be
relied upon to pay annually £8,000,000 or so of
interest on its present public debt, and much more
on its future public debt, if it is alleged at the
same time that it is so stone-broke that it can’t
find £500,000 of its own for the North Shore
bridge ? And what kind of a lie is the yarn about
the enterprise and energy and self-dependence of
this great rich country, when a prominent paper
states in its biggest type that it is impossible to
build a few wharves unless England lends us the
cash ? The daily press of Australia talks much in
a general way about our wealth, progress, energy,
enterprise, resources, and so on, but when it comes
down to a concrete proposal to build even a bridge
out of our own resources its cry of “stinking fish ”
rends the balmy air, and it moans that we are
broke, helpless, penniless, useless, that we haven’t
the price of a bridge about us, and that we can’t
do even what Dutchmen and Swedes are capable
of—rely on our own pluck and our own resources.
It is a pitiable spectacle, and unless this country
learns to do something for itself and not rely on
the British capitalist for' quite everything, the
inevitable end is insolvency.
Our Local Savage.
The investigation of the recent Kyneton (Vic.)
tragedy revealed the existence of a family of
actual white savages within a few 'miles of Mel-
bourne. The victim, _ sister of the accused
Donald Kennedy, was ferociously assaulted on
Feb. 26, but managed to drag herself to an out-
house at her parents’ home, and there remained
for nearly a fortnight in unheeded agony (although
father, mother, brother and some nephews were in
and about the house all the time), except that the
mother occasionally gave her a little water or sour
milk. A married sister and an aunt who happened
along observed that the sufferer was in a fearful
state from her injuries, but presumably they
knew nought of doctors and hospitals, and it was
not till 12 days after the outrage that the unfortu-
nate was lumbered to the hospital in a dray,
dying two hours after admission. There was little
evidence as to the author of the crime, and though
there were many witnesses, of sorts, they were
either afflicted with painful lapses of memory or
were wilfully screening somebody. The Crown,
acting on the latter supposition, 'charged the
brother Donald with the offence; but the jury
(doubtless remembering “ what happened to
Jones,” the alleged Broadford murderer) acquitted
the prisoner—adding to their verdict, however, an
expression of horror at the existence of such
brutal savagery in one of the longest-settled
districts of the province. The case raises the
curtain upon a hideous picture. One of tire
witnesses—a cousin of the dead woman —is serving
a long term for an assault on his cousin, a child of
six; another relative stands at present charged
with a similar offence; a third is insane ; and the
whole connection appears to be hovering between
lunatic and beast. And, once again ! —we keep
sending missionaries to China.

In Sydney Banco Court last week a serious charge
involving hundreds of pounds, made by a client
against a solicitor, came before Chief-Justice
Darley and Justices Stephen and Owen. The
charge was made on affidavit and the client gave
testimony purporting to support. The Court
determined the case in the solicitor’s favor, but the
Chief-Justice, adverting to the solicitor’s action in
one phase of the case, “ hoped it would not occur
again.” Mr. Pilcher, Q.C., who appeared for
the solicitor, hereupon remarked that his client
would prefer that his name should not be men-
tioned in the newspapers, and reminded their
Honors that in the case of another solicitor a few
days before the name had been suppressed. The
Chief-Justice: “The name need not be mentioned.”
This was an open court. Parties to other actions
tried before and after were not protected from
publicity. Whether the decision of the Court was
favorable or unfavorable the names of the litigants
went to the Press. Can it be said the law is alike
for all, the courts are alike open for all, while one
class is shielded with privacy and all others are
exposed to public gaze ? The judicial “need not ”
is of course interpreted by the Press as “must
not,” and so a solicitor whose character has been
under fire may straddle about as if he alone were
a white man.
O-
R. B. Haldane, Q.C., hangs a locally-reprinted
eulogy of Privy Council judgments on this story :
It is told of a traveller who had penetrated into a re-
mote part of India that he found the natives offering up
a sacrifice to a far-off but all-powerful god, who had just
restored to the tribe the land which the Government or
the day had taken from it. He asked the name of the
god. The reply was, “We know nothing of him but that
he is a good god, and that his name is the Judicial Com-
mittee of the Privy Council.”
The name of the traveller, and the date, and the
place, are not given ; so that this particular myth
depends on Haldane’s say-so. The Bulletin
assures Mr. Haldane that he has totally misun-
derstood the myth, which really runs as follows
for purposes of argument :
It is related by G. H. Wilkinson, F.R.G.S., on page 128
of his book, “ Wanderings in the Himalayas ” (Harper’s,
1894), that arriving at Dhajawal, the chief town of the
Dheeli tribe, he found the natives offering up a sacrifice
to propitiate the wrath of a far-off demon, who had just
taken half the tribe’s land to give to the English. He
asked the name of the demon. The reply was, “We
know nothing of him but that he is the most malicious
demon existing, and that his name is the Judicial Com-
mittee of the Privy Council.
This lie, it will be observed, has names and dates
complete ; and if a man must lie he should lie
with circumstance.

One of the weirdest sights on Mafeking-drunk
night in Melbourne was a Westralian out-back
pastoralist and large slave-owner rending the air
with “Britons never, never,” &c., and holding
forth to all and sundry about the sin of the Boer
in “enslaving” the African nigger. What a
beautiful hypocrisy, in this country at least, is the
cry about how the blighted nigger is free when-
ever he touches British ground, while one-third of
Australia is a Slave State, and its slavery exists
under a law which Britain had the right to veto
but didn’t. The Boer and the Westralian are
much alike in some respects. They both call
their slavery “apprenticeship.” They both flog
their nigger more or less, though the Westralian
lies about it and says that he doesn’t. The Wes-
tralian, however, gets ahead in the matter of
hypocrisy, for he (or some of him) claims that he
makes the nigger work for him gratis out of his
pure and holy yearning to save the nigger’s
soul. Also, he professes that the nigger is a
voluntary slave. In Westralia you mustn’t own a
nigger without getting a policeman or J.P. or
other official to witness a document by which it is-
professed that the aboriginal binds himself to
drudge for you for a term of years out of sheer
desire for work. If you are a squatting J.P. and
your only convenient neighbor is also a squatting
J. P., you can t, without breaking the law, forcibly
retain any nigger you have captured, and judi-
ciously boot him into habits of industry, until your
obliging neighbor has signed the document which
sets forth that your nigger came to you quite
voluntarily. And your neighbor can’t legally
keep his fresh-caught nigger in slavery until you
have attested his document in like manner—so
you swap signatures, and all is well. As for the
nigger himself, if he tries to holler out that he is
no party to the agreement, it merely shows that
