(Saturday Afternoon.)
Williamson s new opera company opened
its Sydney season with good old “ Pinafore,”
and before James Cassius had got the lid more
than half off, Sydney had decided to receive the
visitors in a friendly spirit. The new combina-
tion may not be absolutely Al, but it certainly
doesnt get down anywhere near being Z-numer-
ous. It gives an excellent all-round perform-
ance, in which there are only two disappoint-
ments—or possibly I£. Lauri’s Sir Joseph Porter
seems to have been poured out the day before
yesterday ; there is a grievous lack of head on
it, and it is mostly flat and silent. It certainly
froths up to some extent in Act 11., but Lauri
doesn t take kindly to the part, and he ought
to be poured back into the bottle. Also, Doro-
thy Vane is only a mitigated success as Little
-Buttercup. Still, the rest of the co. is so good
that “ Pinafore ” could bear up against worse
troubles than these. Brownlow not only fills the
part of Captain Corcoran to overflowing, but he
has the further advantage of looking heavenly in
his blue uniform ; and Kenningham’s Ralph Rack-
straw cal Is for more kind remarks than there is space
for. Both these notables fill their parts for the first
time in Sydney. So does everybody else; even
the midshipmite. Miss Carrie Moore is the new
Josephine, and the fact that that dainty little
actress is making great strides in the profession
again asserts itself. There have been many
Josephines, including one dressed from her top-
feather to her_ boot-soles in bright, shrieksome
blue ; and if Miss Moore isn’t at the top of the list
she is still a very long way from the bottom.
Harry ohm© s bos’n is about the best bos’ii Sydney
h,os seen so long as he sticks to the book, and the
Dick Deadeye of Hugh Ward is also excellent,
though the angularness of that worthy and ill-
meaning mariner doesn’t show up very distinctly.
The last Deadeye this writer remembers was
Parley, and in a purely trigonometrical sense he
prefers I arlev. Ward is mostly a plain, equilateral
triangle, while Farley was isosceles, scalene and
various, and sometimes rushed out of his triangle
altogether and threatened to become a parallelo-
gram or a cone or polygon or even a rhombohedron.
* * * *
Sundry interpolated songs at the begin-
ning of Act IL and a species of midnight concert
on the deck of the Pinafore are passed over in
comparative silence. There are people who would
put a song and dance into Ephesians, so Gilbert’s
opera could hardly hope to escape. Pat Bathurst’s
fine voice gives a lift to a dismally commonplace
song in this interpolation. As for the war pic-
tures, some of them are excellent, and most of
them have got the insane joggle of the kinemato-
graph in their souls. The programme alleges that
they include “marvellous pictures of actual fight-
mg. This scribe didn’t see these marvellous
pictures. The firing of the disappearing gun is a
fine piece of work that has fewer crazy jumps
tlmn most biographs, but the gun isn’t fired at a
visible enemy, and a certain scurrying of troops
on a plain lacks any perceptible Boers or any
visible killed or wounded to distinguish it from a
mere peaceful review proceeding. An attempt is
made to appeal to the public sentiment of the
moment by showing Paul Kruger upside-down,
but on Monday night only half-a-dozen people
had energy enough to hoot at the big man with
the barn-door countenance.
** * *
A good deal more of Sydney than the
Theatre Royal could comfortably hold cast itself
headlong into that building on Saturday night to
witness its treasured and newly-returned Bland
Holt. It saw him in an Eton jacket and a pair of
short pants as a youth of 24, whom his egg-bald
father insists on passing off as 16 because the
glabrous progenitor is trying to pass himself off
as 35 in order to mash Mrs. Holt. She, in her
turn, is already married to Bland in his Eton suit,
and they have been guilty of a baby between them’
but Bland is wholly dependent on his parent, and
they daren’t revolt lest the old man should stop
their allowance, so Mrs. Bland holds a candle to
the progenitor and makes the best of things. Bland
and his wife and the baby and progenitor Brown are
the comedy elements of “ Women and Wine,’’and
they have no connection whatever with the tragedy
—run by Baker, Norman, and Misses Frances Ross,
Ireland, and Fitzmaurice Gill—save and except
that Bland tries to carry Baker home when he
is ruined and drunk, and later on falls into a tank
while looking for the woman who can clear Baker’s
character. Baker is the same old hero, and his
ringing voice as he declares that he didn’t in-
tentionally kill the woman whose body is found in
a basket in the flower-market can be heard down
in the threepenny bars in York-street. Baker’s
tather is blind and ruined, and Baker himself
tads to pass his barrister’s examination because he
lias spent his time breathing into the back hair of
the siren—who is Miss Harrie Ireland. He casts
ort his own sweetheart because he finds her under
suspicious circumstances in a lawyer’s office where
she lias gone to secretly pay Baker’s debts with
her own money. Then the siren leads him to
Bans where he bums the candle at both ends and
leaves it on the hob in between times, and the
siren throws him over for Norman when he is
cleaned out; and Norman throws his previous
ffirl over for the siren ; and later the siren throws
Norman over for a duke ; and then Norman pro-
m?®e® .*° S° back to his first girl if she
w ill kill his second girl. Wherefore Norman’s
previous girl kills Baker’s previous girl in a duel
with knives m the flower market, and Baker, who
13 u i is too drunk to remember
whether he did it or not. Anyhow, he is found
guilty, and is being sentenced when Norman’s
previous girl comes in and says, for no visible
reason, that she did it. Whereupon Baker acquits
himself without waiting for the court to do it
• * * *
The P lot is largely a mixture of “ Silver
jg. , an< i “ New Boy,” but a good company,
and rich scenery, and Bland’s good stagecraft cast
a glamour over it that isn’t its own. The only
character who has a flavour of originality is
Arthur tetyan’s Calibap, the red-headed hunch-
back, who rushes to grind two big carving-knives
*»■«»* ff hears that the girls are going to
light to the death. Caliban’s entreaties that they
won t start till he has got a good edge on the
knives are pathetic. Caliban also proposes hav-
ing Baker arrested for the mur lor because it
appeals to his sense of humour, and the find-
ing of Miss. Ireland’s body in the basket
makes him laugh till he nearly falls down
eight times. The four ladies already men-
tioned do much good work ; and Baker, Norman,
Bland Holt, Charles Brown, and plump Napo-
leon Boffara (who sings in a pleasing tenor voice
just when Baker is ruined) are all persons that it
is a pleasure to meet. But their parts are more or
less conventional. Caliban, with his refreshing
desire to see somebody or anybody or everybody
killed, not that he may make money out of it,
but merely that the sight of the body may com-
fort his declining years, and make him laugh
when he wakes up in the night, is bright and
original. And originality is scarce.

Alfred Dampier produces “ Briton and
Criterion on Saturday, until
which date Miss Lily Dampier will soothe the
last hours of little Willie as usual. M iss Dampier
is a better actress than of yore, and the only
senous drawback to her performance of the lady
of Last Lynne is her comfortable plumpness.
I Ins is all right m the earlier stages of the drama,
but the lady who dies in the last act of a bad
consumptive korf and a broken heart shouldn’t be
plump and healthy-looking. She should point a
transparent hand up Above, and her weight
shouldn t be more than six-stone —everything
above that is an outrage. Therefore plump stage
heroines should perish of plague or dog-bite or
some other complaint which rains on the thin and
the stout alike ; or else the lady who is strong
and healthy at 8 p.m. should have a thin duplicate
to die for her at 11.
* * **
At Sydney Tivoli, where the virtues of
whisky and bile remedies and various sorts of
tobacco glare from a curtain of many colors
things are much as usual. Jimmy Bain’s cry of
Ile ! Pie ! Pie ! a parody of the Abstracted
Petitioner or Meditative Abstractor—will cease
to flow on Friday night owing to the departure
of the tap to Yankeeland. The Martinetti
dumb-shows are apparently popular, probably
because there is so much of them for the money.
.Robert Macaire ” is the silent drama of the
ma,n who cuts his finger while slicing the bread,
and the individual who puts his stick down
heavily on the other fellow’s toe, and gets in the
way of everything that falls from an elevated
position, and goes on to die tragically while
turning a somersault. There isn’t very much in
it save quantity, but about that there is no mis-
take whatever.
# m *
“The Professor's Love Story,” which
struck this country at Melb. Princess’s last Satur-
is just what one expects from the author of
Ihe Little Minister.” It has a simple, fragile
plot, worked out in quaint style to a neat finish.
Ihe sentunent of the story is tender all the time,
and the humor never descends to “ comic relief”—
m fact, “The Professor’s Love Story” will suit
the palate of all people who don’t want to feed
their intellects with pork and apple-sauce. There
are only eleven characters, all told, in Barrie’s
gentle comedy, but most of them are interesting
individuals. Love takes the Professor by surprise
just when he is in danger of getting hopelessly
scientific. Much learning has made him so old
and absent-minded that the sister who safeguards
his bachelor state permits a very marriageable
young woman to be his private secre-
tary and in due course the said sister is
shocked to find her relative playing bo-
peep with his amanuensis in a large, yellow
cornfield. Up to this time the Professor doesn’t
know what is the matter with him. He is merely
conscious of a desire to frisk. But it happens that
an interloping widow notifies her intention to
taint on a corn-sheaf and get restored to conscious-
ness by the erudite lambkin, after which she will
consider themselves engaged. Then the right
girl decides to euchre the widow, anyhow, by
being the first one to lose consciousness on the
straw, and when the Prof, has carried the artful
angel to the nearest river in his arms he is a lost
scientist. The serious maiden sister makes trouble
for a while, on the ground that her own deceased
lover was a fraud, but subsequently discovers he
was not false at all, therefore she allows the
Professor to take Lucy into the firm and the
baffled widow retires for good. Apart from the
central narrative there are two sad agriculturists
who cherish a slow, lumbering affection for the
same Effie, and the chump with fewest cobwebs
in his hair secures the prize.
*
Charles Arnold, in his representation of
the professor, takes no risks. He represents
Arnold a few years older and* quieter than usual,
and as he is always an amiable Arnold, and no-
body can show cause why lie shouldn’t pass for a
bookworm occasionally, the performance is a nega-
tive success. It doesn’t suggest any depth of
character in the alleged professor, but for that
matter, the comedy doesn’t seem to have provided
much character for the actor to work upon. Miss
Hot Frederic, as the secretary, is all the better
for being in a part where her favourite manner-
isms are under judicious restraint. When she
grows accustomed to her new mood the little lady
will be better than on Saturday. Miss Agnes
Knight is a very sympathetic sister, also the two
yokels—Fred Sharp and .Frank Denton—are im-
portant considerations, ancLfhe other people are
well placed with the excepnon of Miss Inez Ben-
susan, who is not yet a sufficiently practised
actress to play the rather difficult and unthankful
part of a giddy old hen.
*# * *
“ Magda ” and Miss Nance O’Neil came
along to Her Majesty’s (Melb.) last Saturday. A
crowded house and a flashlight photo, of the
audience were features of the occasion, and his
Waxellency was present, and one way and
another the occasion tried its hardest to make
Miss O’N. look as large as she had been painted in
many preliminary paragraphs. Physically the
massive visitor is about the size of her advance
notices, though, by-the-way, she is not quite
another Maxine Elliott, Artistically well,
Nance O’Neil is not a great actress, or
even a finished actress, but she makes an
imposing show on the stage and turns her
back upon her admirers as though her left
shoulder-blade were—-in a Gilbertian way of
sneaking—a miracle of histrionic effect. Magda—
Ihe Bulletin has previously remarked —is the
well-written tale of a broad-minded prodigal
daughter, who made the mistake of returning (on
a visit of uncertain duration) to a dull, narrow-
minded home. It is a play of small details and
long, eloquent dialogues, and the lady in the
name-part has the best of the conversation from
the moment she appears. She appears in a gor-
geous cloak, and, after an impressive removal of
the outer raiment, she scores a decided success
with her gorgeous arms, bust and back, especially
her back.
* * * *
It seems to the present scribe that Miss
Nance’s Magda is out of the picture. M'Kee
Rankin, as the old semi-fool of a parent, is a
convincing figure, likewise his docile daughter,
Marie. The priggish pastor (Thomas Kingston)
is a fair sample of the profession. Harry Plimmer,
as the dull, red-headed lawyer, plays a life-like
scene with Mr. Rankin aforesaid, and the minor
characters (save the amateurish Miss Allen) tend
to give Magda’s old home an air of reality, what
time the overwhelming daughter isn’t there. Miss
O’Neil’s large, loud performance lacks feeling, and
variety. Some of her movements aie ungraceful,
and the actress has but one effective facial expres-
sion, viz., a tragic scowl which soon palls on the
observer. At the times when her face would be
expected to express strong emotions other than
the monotonous anger of the lady, Nance gets
over the difficulty by hanging her head or turning
her back to the audience. No matter, “Magda”
is a powerful play, and Nance O’Neil’s name pro-
mises to be a powerful attraction.
#* * *
At Melbourne Royal, the girl who loves
M'Dougall is still wiping her eye on the corner
of his kilt, and the two villains persist in their
strange endeavor to demolish Miss Eugenie
Duggan with a steam hammer. The sixth week
of “The Ladder of Life” finds that wild, irre-
sponsible melodrama fighting hard against its
fate. Another drama, “On Active Service,” is
ready to jump the Royal stage, but, meanwhile,
“The Ladder,” it appears, is stayed in the bones of
British heroes or something, and it will probably
refuse to be shifted until next week.

Amann, of the elastic countenance, is
closing his long season at the Bijou on Friday of
this week ; so is the somewhat cerulean Seel, who
is too muqh like a nightmare to please nervous
people. Arrangements for filling these vacancies
were not announced at time of writing. The
Ilaytors are getting along cheerfully with their
new turn, which bears a strong family resemblance
to one of the turns they gave us years ago, but is
just as laughable all the same. On Saturday
afternoon a special matinee in .aid of certain
orphanage funds is to happen at the Bijou, and
the companies from the Princess’s and the Royal
will assist the cause.
** * *
The new Biograph, before which all pre-
vious biographs must pale their flickering fires,
has scored about as great a draw as the size of the
Athemeum Ilall will permit. The pictures and
incidental orchestra give two performances a day,
and more often than not all seats are taken. Last
time the writer called in to see Lord Roberts,
Spion Kop and bugler boy Dunn (who shot three
Boers with his dear little revolver), the cleverest
picture in the series showed a shortish, stoutish,
goodish-looking man—the Prince of Wales, in fact
—walking past a regiment of soldiers at six o’clock
a.m. The foreground figures were so sharp and
true that one could almost see Albert Edward
earning an appetite for breakfast by the dew on
his whiskers.
* * * *-
At the Scherek Orchestral Concert, last
Saturday afternoon, an enthusiastic audience
filled the shilling parts, whereas the evidences of
taste for music in the two and three “bob’sec-
tions were not very numerous. Alberto Zelman
tackled his long solo in the Mendelssohn concerto
with a brave heart, and Mrs. Grace Ward’s pretty
soprano voice sounded stronger than it was a
month ago. The principal item in next Saturday’s
concert will have clever young Una Bourne at the
piano.
** * *
Donald Macdonald, the bright, able-minded
journalist who lived for many weeks in Ladysmith
upon the lean of the land, and who learned the
flavor of mule’s hoof from bitter experience, is
about to start upon a lecturing tour. The first
appearance of Mac. in his new character is fixed
for to-night (Thursday) at Melbourne Town Hall,
where he will lecture some more on Saturday (9th.)
and Wednesday next (13th.) Success is only a
matter of health and strength with the Aucius
man from Ladysmith. He has many tales to tell
—sad tales, funny tales, tales of blood and thun-
der, and tales of simple human nature in a state
of suffering from rats inside —and he tells them
for all they are worth.
The WILD CAT
COUMN
The gigantic A.M.P. Society’s policy-holders
are to meet and vote on 20th July to decide whether the
directors shall be Riven power to extend the business
beyond Australasia, to any part of the British Empire.
Australasia is almost the healthiest pit c ■ in the
the Empire, so the extension is almost certain to mean a
higher mortality-rate, and consequently lower profits
Also, an extension to England means the necessary in-
vestment of part of the funds in a country where interest-
rates are less than they are here—which also means lower
profits. And the A.M.P. Society is doing well in Austra-
lasia ; it is one of the half-dozen or so most progressive
offices in the world ; and the directors have never offered
any tangible reason why this amazingly-prosperous office
shouldn't leave well alone. Yet they are in such deadly
earnest over their fad that they have descended to
a very questionable dodge for cooking the vo.e, and
stuffing the ballot-box, and faking the result. They don't
issue a voting-paper to each policy-holder, and let him
send in or bring in his “ Yes ” or “ No,” and decide accor-
ding to whether the “Yes” or the “No" predominates,
leaving out of consideration the great mass of policy-
holders who record no opinion either way. Instead they
have struck a great idea for counting those who record
no opinion either way as voting for tiie extension.
Every policy-holder receives a form to fill in appointing
Speaker Abbott his proxy at the meeting (of course not
one policy-holder in 100 can attend a meeting held in
Sydney). A stamped envelope is enclosed for return
There is no voting-paper—only a proxy-form, and the
proxy-form doesn’t even contain a blank space in which
the policy-holder can express his views. And it is intimated
in a bright, off-hand fashion that Speaker Abbott will use
the proxy .as a vote for the extension 1! !! Those who are
strongly and intelligently for or against the extension
will probably be unaffected by this dodge, but the great
mass of policy-holders have no fixed opinion, and don’t
know what it is all about. And (especially as Speaker
Abbott is understood to be a very respectable personage
indeed) very many of them will sign their proxies and
send them in merely because it looks dishonest not to do
so when the society has wasted the policy-holders’ money
on sending a stamp. And all these absolutely indifferent
people are to be counted as strong and intelligent sup-
porters of the dangerous policy of extension. It is a
mean and shabby dodge for getting a bogus majority,
and every intelligent, member should object. A numbei
intend objecting at a public meeting on 11th June

Mr. J. A. Wallace, the pioneer of the gold-
dredging industry in Australia, holds a long stretch of the
Sandy Creek, near Yackandandah, and intends installing
a very large electrical plant to operate several dredges.
An order has just been placed with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., of South Melbourne, to supply high-class
compound condensing engines, with Corliss valve gear, of
400 h.p., together with boilers and fittings to operate the
first section of the plant. A duplicate plant to this will
be installed later and will work three electric dredges on
a stretch of about two miles of the creek. Each dredge
will have a capacity of about 150 cubic yards per hour.*

* # # *
Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, Mining Machinery
Merchants, 506 Little Gollins-strcet, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest Luhrig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, &c., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Melb.
and prompt delivery is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available near Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Iron founder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sortsof iron-work.*

Gibraltar Consolidated, Adelong, N.S.W. :
latest about this mine from London shows
a loss of about £ i 000 from one month’s
run. Three or four years ago it started with
some excellent crushings, paid one or two divs.,
which had the effect of sending shares in a i 300,000 co. to
a big premium ; they are now a less number of shillings
than they were then pounds, and abandonment seems
within measurable distance. The erection of such an
enormous and costly plant to work so small a vein in such
hard country was as unwarranted as the capitalisation
was excessive.
* * * *
United Exploration Co. has gone the way of most
others—into voluntary liquidation. At its final meeting
in London recently a shareholder declared that £371,683
had been spent uselessly—all that the shareholders had to
show for it were worthless shares in (with one exception)
worthless mines—name of exception not disclosed. Surely
not the Paddy Lackev Deep Levels ? Or was it the mine
this co. handled at Hillgrove ? Altogether it had a bad
time in Australia, especially in N.S.W., where it bonded
many mines, worked a few, got little or no gold in any,
and, at the same time, missed two or three excellent op-
portunities, the Paddy Lackey itself, to wit.

As regards the Citizens’, the difficulty last year
was partly owing’ to the fact that a large proportion of trie
funds were represented by a huge, unfinished and unpro-
ductive building. But the Colonial Mutual s trouble
appears to be chronic. The situation is this : Comparing
it with other offices of about its own size and standing it
appears a fair assumption that the funds, with all assets
written down to actual present value, should yield at
least £4 10s. per cent, all round, and to bring them to
that point it would be necessary to write off the existing
depreciation fund and about £240,000 in addition. And
then, so far as the old business (which is still the great
bulk of the business) was concerned the Colonial Mutual
would still have a margin of safety, as between interest
estimated and interest actually earned, of only 10s. per
cent, and (so far as the old business was concerned) it
would still look the least safe and the least promising
of all the large life offices of Australasia.
To give as large a margin as that of the National Mutual
it would he necess iry, on present appearances, to write
down the assets by more than £361,000. On the outward
appearance of things, the present writer, at all events,
can come to no other conclusion than that the Colonial
Mutual hasn’t been making sufficient provision for depre-
ciation in the past, and that its assets are at present very
much over-valued, and that there is a great difficulty to
be faced. The quinquennial investigation takes place
this year, and it will he interesting to note how the board
decides to deal with the situation.
* * * *
The Bankers’ Magazine (published in Mel-
bourne) remarks in its issue of 25th May that the writing-
down of capital by the National Bank (Melb.) was “ a
surprise to everyone.” If it was a surprise to the
Bankers’ Magazine that paper didn’t see so far ahead as
The Bulletin’s “ Wild Cat Column ” did. On 29th Oct.,
’9B, and again on Gth May, ’99, and a third time oil 4th
Nov. of same year it foretold that the National would
have to write down its capital. The 18 months’ warning
whicli this paper gave should have guarded against the
surprise above-mentioned. Yet apparently the public
was surprised, for it bought National ordinary shares in
the first half of April at from 82s. to 88s., and then they
dropped as low as 755.

In nine years, therefore, the Australian Mutual Fire Co.
has paid £34,918 in bonuses to policy-holders, added
£25,000 to its paid-up capital out of profits, added £32,500
to its reserves, and increased the balance at credit of
profit and loss by £1652. The shareholders are certainly
having a great time. They originally put up £6250. It
was increased to £12,500 by the re-valuation of their free-
hold property about 14 years ago, in the days when free-
hold property did occasionally increase ill value, and, later,
25,000 more was added out of profits, so last year the
■original holders received over 144 per cent, on the capital
which they actually put up. In the last nine years they
have got their capital back eight times over. The Aus-
tralian Mutual Fire has now a paid-up capital of £37,500
and £109,638 of reserves, the latter being a record with
which no other purely Australian fire office can compare.
And these results have been achieved despite many years
of steady adherence to the policy of doing business in one
province only—the policy which, according to the blatant
“expert,” is so utterly ruinous and disastrous as to make
State fire insurance (which would be necessarily confined
to one province only) quite impossible.
After which eulogium The Bulletin rises to
remark that the Australian Mutual, Fire Co. s balance-
rwrr™ntains not half so much information as it should
to vvhere theca’s big pile is invested. “Investments,
freehold property and mortgages £109,584 ” is hardly
worth calling information. An institution whose assets
cari stand the glare of public inspection should be more
liberal
The Equitable Permanent Benefit Building
Land and Savings Institution (Sydney) issues its balance-
sheet for year ended 31/3/1900. The document leaves a
vat ue impression of prosperity, but it is so mysterious
that few- details can be dug out of it. This institution
never states what its profits are. The year began
with a profit balance of £4946. The gross receipts were
£l5 200 • office expenses were £lBl7, and interest accrued
£2496 Also an 8 per cent div. on investing 1 snares
absorbed £30.. These things are clear enough. Then it
is stated thafc interest paid on deposits and bank over-
draft (which are items of working expenses) and 9 per
cent div. to shareholders (which is an item that should
come out of profits and be shown separately)—these three
lumped together come to £9699. A'so a bonus ab-
sorbs £2113, and another bonus runs to £282. An insti-
tution whose accounts were kept on sane principles
would show what balance remained as net profit after
meeting all working expenses, interest on deposits of all
kinds, and interest to bank. Out of this balance it would
pay its div. and bonus to ordinary and investing share-
holders. Then its report would show what the profits
really are. Some things that are certain, however, are
these : The co., in its wild desire to pay a large div
(whatever it came to), and a bonus as well, spent £123/
more than its earnings (whatever they were). And this
is not the only occasion on which it has done so, for its
reserves of all kinds are now only £26,755, whereas five
years ago they stood at £29,003. The decline isnt
large, hut the muddled state of the balance-sheet is
aggravating. . * ,

A dividend at the rate of 16 per cent, per annum absorbs

the profit balance is decreased by £ll7—from £12,113 to

depreciation fund to cover the half-year’s losses. Even
this, however, is at the rate of over 32 per cent, per an-
num on capital, so no one can reasonably complain.
The Swan Brewery has £76,087 10s. paid-up capital (of
which £31,703 is capitalised profits, so that the share-
holders are receiving over 27 per cent, on the money they
actually put into the business); £25,000 reserve-fund ;
£14,485 depreciation reserve ; and 611,996 profit balance ;
therefore the humble £44,384 which the shareholders
actually subscribed has grown to £127,569. Also the co.
has a bank balance of £5255, whereas 18 months ago
it had £18,667 overdraft. Yet with all this pro-
sperity the boom isn’t what it was. The profits for the
thirsty half of 1900 are nearly £2OOO less than those for
the corresponding half of 1899 ; over £BOOO less than for
’9B, and over £6OOO less than for ’97. And the returns for
September last showed a great fall as compared with the
two previous Septembers.

r * * * *
Like an invading horde, the gold-dredging
boomster, pegger, speckcr, and tipster have swooped
down upon Greymouth (M.L.). For the past six months
it has been the Mecca for those who suddenly remembered
the golden glories of the (Wild) West Coast of Maoriland
when the long-handled shovel, never able to delve deeper
than 13ft., turned out more gold per man than most
N.S.W. dredges are now winning per week. These were the
days of Darkies’ Terrace, when the poorest claim panned
out lloz. per load. It’s a passing mystery why ML.
should have been so long in calling all this to mind ; now
she is making up for lost time with a vengeance. Already
hundreds of miles of sea-beach and river-bank are pegged
and floated into limited liability cos., representing
£16,000,000 sterling, and they say the fun has only just
begun. „ ,
* * *
For the first quarter of the year, Maoriland re-
turned £371,838 in gold, an increase of £11,789 over same
period previous year. # #
The Woodlark Island mystery game goes merrily
on The show’s Sydney officials and perch have been
changed. Let us hope that some more of the good old
original 17cwt.-20oz. stuff will gladden the hearts and
pockets of the hopeful souls who waded in at 40s. or
thereabouts, and still hang on.

“Mainshaft”: Garnet Freehold is finding the
south end of its mine to be the best. The new make of
ore has been driven on over 100 ft., and is now within 80ft.
of the southerly limit of* the Freehold. Little under-
ground work going on as enough ore has been blocked to
keep the smelters going for three years, but a small army
of navvies is removing the overburden and making the
benches. Smelters will be in blast by August, but direc-
tors should not delay appointing a general
already the friction between the sub-managers is
remarked locally.

Croydon tin-field has been named Stanhope.
Now apparent that country is stanniferous over an area of
nearly 20 miles long by 10 wide, and this area is being
extended by prospecting. Some splendid-looking tin
bodies recently opened in typical tin-granite country.
Many leases and claims taken up, also mill-sites. Croy-
don’s future now looms bright, and much money is
expected to be forthcoming when the “ shows ” have been
fully opened up.
* * * •
Announced that the Mt. Deddiclc Railway
agreement has been duly signed by Vic. Govt., but not
yet announced that the agent for the English Syndicate
has put up a £lO,OOO deposit to bind the bargain. People
who have known the Deddiclc district for 20 years or
more are still dubious as to the sanity of that dark Syn-
dicate. The copper “ lode ” talked of by its agent in
Melbourne is no new discovery. Copper was always evi-
dent to anyone who looked for it on the other side of the
mountain from the Deddick lead mines. The alleged
expert who came from England and kept his movements
shrouded in mystery may have discovered the length,
depth and thickness of the copper lode, but where are the
proofs of his having gone any further into the question
than his scores of predecessors?
* * * *
South German Reef Co., Maldon, has received
another proposal from J. Bull, viz., £6 per share cash and
96,030 fully-paid shares in a co. of 400,000, but subject to
one month’s inspection. Expert has gone up to the mine.
Will it stand this ordeal? When reef was cut off by the
floor, it was awfully close to the adjoining property, and
the winze has been sunk to the 1700 ft. level without finding
it. The adjoining people think reef makes again in their
show, and Vic. Mines Minister has agreed that, in the in-
terests of mining, they should be allowed to drive 25ft.
into their ground from the lower level of the South
German Reef Co. The latter is very savage at this, but
cannot put it off for long. In any case, 400,000 shares
would be a nice load to carry for a mine that has already
distributed £S per share on 28,000 shares, and has now
lost its shoot of gold. The report of the expert now at
the mine is anxiously awaited.

Big reefing find reported from Yodda Valley,

trick or two.

For its half-year to March 31st, B.H. Block 14
made a profit of £12,323. This is the poorest for years ;
yet the output of ore was the co.’s heaviest. Figures for
some time back run like this :
Carbonate Sulphide

March, ’9OO .. .. 12,323 2934 58,147
Now, this looks worse than it probably is. If it means
that this co. is not now picking its ore (all Broken Hill
oompanies have been doing more or less of that), well and
good—it simply means giving the mine several more years
to live. Also there appears to have been a rather heavy
loss through the purchase of refractory gold ore. Little
can be got out of this co’s statements, however,
because the management never lets even the
shareholders into its confidence. How much the
ore has fallen off in metal value, what the new develop-
ments are worth, and what has been made out of the re-
treatment of the tailings, are things which one might
reasonably expect shareholders would like information
about. But they don’t ; they had their meeting, and
opened not their mouths. In their own vague way, the
directors announce that the half-year’s development
work has exposed better ore at 500 ft. than at 300 ft. and
400 ft., which is good ; also that, though the bad gold-ore
deal prevented the payment of one of the usual quarterly
divs. last half, there is a likelihood of three divs. this half
to make up for it. Whether this alleged likelihood is
well-founded or not cannot be said, because there isn’t
anything to show what the loss was on the gold-ore deal
or how much profit the co. would have made if it had
stuck to silver-lead mining. If the shareholders get the
extra div., they’ll get it. If they don’t, they don’t. And
that’s all about it.

SOCIETY
Such appointments as those of the sons
of Premier Lyne, Colonial Secretary See,
and Darley, C.J., to commissions in N.S.w!
Lancers, constitute an abuse which should be
once and for all squelched by the Legisla-
ture. These first commissions should in every
case be given to the sergeants of the corps,
aS .] possible in the order of seniority,
and the sooner politicians are deprived by statute
of the power of doing otherwise, the better it will
be for he service. “ Promotion from the ranks ”
must be the watchword of the Australian army,
b ortunately, we have as yet no alleged aristocracy
here to grovel to. The man who can rise from the
ranks is the best man. “I made my marshals
out of mud,” said Napoleon.
In the Imperial service, the “ranker” has a
good enough time while he remains single. When
he marries, his life is a “ hell-upon-earth,” for
“ the other cats ” point at his wife and say, “No
class—she married a ranker.” We must get ahead
of this kind of savagery in Australia by 4 seeing that
every man rises from the ranks.
Captain Phillip was the only N.S.W. Gover-
nor untroubled with clerical precedence questions.
He allowed Rev. Richard Johnston to dodder
along as he pleased with a weak sort of Moravian
Church of Englandism, and flogged anyone who
attempted lo teach any other creed. But when he
left, the seels lifted up their heads. First, various
religions w ■ e tolerated, next the heads of these
religions claimed social recognition, next som<
claimed equal recognition and others primary re
cognition, and so oil, until the other day the
''•esbyterians and Wesleyans fell foul of Gov.
-champ because their heads did not receivo
the same cards as the E.C. and R.C. heads.

Point Piper Road is to have its name
changed at the whim of one of Sydney’s un-
speakably small-minded and toadying suburban
councils to “Jersey Road. Its present name
certainly isn t pretty, but it has some historic
interest; and, if a change is necessary, surely
some better substitute could be found than the
name of a bird of passage who dropped in easu-
ally to •govern” N.S.W. until he got tired of
Sydney has about ten Jersey streets, roads
and terraces already.
In other cities, when the military fall
out with the citizens and beat the latter with
then' belts, the soldiers are refused leave and are
confined to barracks. Were this rule followed
in Sydney the street-war would soon end.
* • • *
It had been hoped that the plague in
Sydney was dying out; but six new cases on 2nd
June from a wide metropolitan area, show
that the need of precaution is as urgent as ever.
1 here is a great and costly scare at Sydney’s
sea ode suburb, Manly, where one of the big hotels
has been quarantined. The plague looks likely to
be the most ruinous misfortune which has eve’
struck Australia ; and if it is not stamped out th
ruin will affect every soul in the community soono
or later. Pity that all the war-wasted cash air
‘ ithusiasm was not spent in fighting plague.
Despite the fact that the plague still con-
tinues in Sydney, very few citizens consent to
inoculation. On some days those who present
themselves don’t number a dozen. One reason
may lie that the operation takes place at the
I own Hall, and people fear that the dirt, and
“language” of the place may got into the'
systems.

A Bendigo shopkeeper
has lost all his romantic faith
in the block arts. A middlC-
aged gipsy lady (one of the
tribe of nuisances camped at
Eaglehawk) sauntered into
this guileless citizen’s place of
business with a proposal to
tell his fortune. Me didn’t
want to know his fortune, but
he was willing for the glib
female to show him her me-
thod of making water boil in
a tumbler. 1 laving procured
a tumbler of water, he anx-
iously watched the brown
sorceress. She put a cloth
over the top of the tumbler,
and asked for sundry pieces of
silver to “cross” the magic
cloth. The shopkeeper tod
the cloth with 235. in all, but
the water didn’t boil. Then
the lady gathered the cash
into her vast pocket and
wished her client good-bye
and good-luck. Also, she told
him he was a Good Man.
Remarkable fact that the dupe
dared to publish his foolish-
ness by giving the woman into
custody.

“ G. 8.” : Recently met
a man I hadn’t seen for 30
years. On crutches, badly-
scarred face, nose broken.
Told me his story. Got mar-
ried ; and after two years,
wife bolted with another fel-
low, leaving a child behind.
My friend tried to cut his
throat, but a blunt razor and
impertinent interference frus-
trated the effort. Resolved
to live for child’s sake. Child
died of typhoid. Took to
drink, ana went to the dogs.
Pulled together and made a
fresh start, but got broken up
in a railway smash, and was
months in hospital. Got £5OO
damages. Bought a farm and
went in for dairying, but pleuro soon finished that
up. Cleared out with £lOO, and went up country.
Got on the burst at Hay, and tried to throw him-
self into a river, but fell into a boat and broke his
leg and three ribs. Hospital once more. Dis-
charged, came down to Melbourne. Fell foul of a
Carlton “push”; got half-murdered, and had
just come out of hospital when I met him. And
some people say there’s no such thing as hard
luck !

James Murphy, the Grafton youth whom
Justice G. B, Simpson sentenced to death at
Central Criminal Court, Sydney, last week for
the murder of another youth, Cyrus M'Farlane,
is but 17 years of age—which caused the Judge
to shod pitying remarks. But the death-aenteno-
mg of youths has been an early and frequent
practice in N.S.W. The first execution in
Botany Bay, 112 years ago, was that of James
Barrett, a boy of 17, and the roll of juveniles
executed runs down to the Mount Rennie holo-
caust. Murphy was recommended to mercy on
account of his youth, and his life will probably
be spared.

memb. committee.

“A NIGHTMARE ON THE PLAGUE-GERMS QUESTION.”
“At the Indian Famine Meeting, in Sydney Town Hall, a suggestion was
made that ladies should make attacks upon the population with collection-
boxes, and the Mayor intimated that the suggestion had in it the germ or ex-
cellence.” —Daily paper.
The end-of-the-week prowpect of the Boer
war petering out started queries as to what will
be done with the surplus (financial authorities say
there is a substantial surplus on present face-
showings) thousands contributed to N.S.W. war-
fund. Some contributors of large sums urge a
pro-rata return to the donors, while others favor
endowments of one kind or another. The Soudan
“Patriotic” Fund, which came to about £27,000,
spent only 2s. in the £, and made a refund of 18s.
in the £ to the donors. Jimmy Tyson got back
£lBOO, and others were proportionally recouped.
Many, however, paid the returned money over to
charities or objects of military enthusiasm. Tyson
handed his £lBOO to the Naval Home at Church
Hill, Sydney.
* * • •
At Singleton (N.S.W.), the other day, a
solicitor, at the Small Debts Court, made applica-
tion for a non-suit at the proper time —after plain-
tiff’s case had closed. The Bench retired to the
magistrates’-room to consider, and smin. after-
wards returned and dumbfounded the court by
announcing a verdict for plaintiff. And amongst
the beaks was a P.M. ! Defendant swallowed the
pill without a murmur, in that the verdict was for
but a fourth of the amount which plaintiff had
sworn to! The most disappointed man in the
court was plaintiff, although defendant had had
an order made against him without having a
chance to be heard !
* * * »
To gratify the vanity of the pompous
H. V. Venn, the W.A. Premier appointed
him president of a Paris Exhibition Com-
mission, and gave him carte blanche. Up to
date over £30,000 has been spent, or rather
thrown away, as the work of collecting exhibits
was entrusted to officials, not one of whom had
ever seen a bigger exhibition than the awful Cool-
gardie show. Result is that the W.A. court is in
an inextricable mess, as even the Govt, paper has
had to admit. Old Jules Joubert, who was
ignored by the Forrest Govt, throughout, smiles
with grim satisfaction. By the way, the veteran
showman is now living in Fremantle, and carries
his age remarkably well —he will be 76 this July.

An ideal place for bank-clerks is Mudgee
(N.S.W.). April 25 was a public holiday there on
account of the local races; May 2 was ditto for
the Bligh races ; May 9 for the Rylestone races.
Rylestone is not quite 40 miles away. Then there
was May 23 for trie relief of Mafeking, and May
24 was Queen’s Birthday.

The feature of butcher “ Bill ” Buttel’s
funeral was the ponies and carriage which
Mr. Buttel always used following next
the hearse, in charge of an employe of
28 years ’ standing. The ponies, a pair formerly
driven by Lady Carington, are understood to
have been a gift from Lord Carington when
leaving N.S.W. as a mark of appreciation of the
manner in which “Butcher Bill” attended to
the appetites of Government House folk.
ROBBED OF THEIR TRUMPETS; OR,
MELBOURNE ON MAFEKING DAY.
A recent Bulletin par. anent red, white
and blue has raised something like consternation
in Melb. Jingo circles, and has led to much hunt-
ing-up of authorities. Result seems to be that
red, white and blue are utterly unknown as
British colors. A reference to the diagrams in
Webster’s Dictionary and in the Encyc. Brit.
fails to reveal them, whereas the former plainly
gives red, white and blue arranged horizontally
as the Dutch ensign, and arranged vertically as
the French (tricolor). The popularity of songs
about the red, white and blue arose, it would
seem, at the time of the Crimean war, where, of
course, the French and the British were fighting
side by side against the Russians. The songs got
so popular that an erroneous impression worked
its wav into the average English brain that
R.W. and B. were the British colors. It really
seems too amusing to be true —that the howling
Jingoes of the last few months have been, wildly
saturnalious over either the French or the Dutch
flags. A possible explanation might be found in
the fact that, before ’Ol, the flag-oifieers (admirals)
of the navy were divided into three classes—
the red, blue and white. But, in ’64, this dis-
tinction was abolished, and, since then, the white
ensign has been the one in ordinary use, the red
and the blue being reserved for extraordinary
occasions. On the whole, therefore, the Jingoes
have been following the wrong funeral.

A riot at a wedding is un-
usual in N.S.W. A settlement
known as Mt. Vincent, near
Maitland, managed to work up
a successful one, t’other day. A
large table heavily laden with
presents, such as pounds of tea,
rice, tins of jam, &c., got its
accumulation augmented by an
unmentionable gift at the hands
of a new arrival on the scene.
He called several friends to
enjoy the joke, when up came
a relative of the bride’s. The
latter negatived all idea of the
action being funny in his eyes by
smashing the article to smither-
eens on the floor, and immediately
afterwards hitting on the jaw
the man who had brought it.
Then the row began, and when
it ceased the casualty list was
large. At the police-court two
accused were called upon to pay
fines, costs, and doctor’s fees
amounting to £26.
Maoriland Govt, proposes to
keep one telegraph-office in each chief
town open day and night. A reform much needed
in Australia.
Noteworthy that, of the N.S.W. soldiers
recently returned invalided from S’Africa, only
three —leaving out a case or two of broken bones
—were wounded. Enteric fever, rheumatism,
dysentery, &c.', accounted for all the rest. Glory 1
• * * *
When the confusion was at its height; in
Bourke-street, Melbourne, on the night officially
set apart for the celebration of the relief of
Mafeking, the crowd rushed an unhappy Chow
just in front of Cole’s. John, suspecting that
lie was to be offered as a sacrifice before the
great god Jingo, howled, whilst the push stripped
him of his trousers, and turned him loose, semi-
nude and utterly ashamed. Then the poor Pagan
was hustled up and down and gu.yed mercilessly,
and for a minute or two civilised Melbourne
revelled in the spectacle of a trouserless Chow
racing madly in front of a howling crowd. John
dashed along Elizabeth-street, and found cover in
Little Bourke, thanking all his gods for his
miserable life, Not a few of the “fair” sex took
a hand in this delectable outrage.

“ S.S.” : In Adelaide the crowd wrecked
the shop of a grocer who kept open on Mafeking
celebration day; in Melbourne' suburbs open
shops of various kinds provoked expressions of
hostility in the shape of bricks ; but in both cities
all the pubs were awake and doing a roaring trade
and it never occurred to anyone to heave a rock
through a bar window. An innocent paragraphist
wants to know why this was thus ?
* * * *
The N.S.W. civil servants think of pre-
senting an illuminated address to President
Kruger, in acknowledgment of his kindness in
procuring so many holidays for them. In addi-
tion to those they have already had, they ex-
pect at least half-a-dozen more, including
holidays on the cessation of hostilities, on the
conclusion of peace, and on the re-naming of
the two newly-acquired provinces ; not to mention
the departure of further “Contingents,” should
matters henceforth not go quite as smoothly as
expected.

Out of the 12 new appointees to N.S.W.
Leg. Council but three—Messrs. Reginald Black,
Langwell and Slattery—have had previous politi-
cal experience. Mr. Black, as a keen man of
figures and with the knack of popularising them,
will be a distinct accession to the ability of the
House. By-the-way, the organised Laborists
assert that Mr. Fred. Flowers is the only recog-
nised Laborist of the three alleged workmen’s
representatives in the just-appointed batch.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Jane 5, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
“Pinafore”—and Brownlow, and Kenningham,
and all the rest of the popular C.O. Co. drew an
immense house to Her Majesty’s on Saturday;
the comfortable sort of house a manager likes,
with half the people standing. Quite a rejuvenated
d.c. In the V ,R. box a party of pretty
girls included Evelyn Martheze in black, with a
blue fillet in her hair, and Miss Wade Brown, and
Miss Winifred Austin, also in black. Mrs. Rennie
was in pink ; Mrs. J. C. Williamson in high-
necked and low-sleeved white. Every woman’s
head flowered till it was roses, roses all the way.
But do you like a long drab face—what Dickens
ealls a face in a tablespoon —to top its grey hair
with roses ? To a sensitive mind tbe sight recalls
that soulful canvas, that we all don’t buy, depict-
ing a skull with roses growing out of it.
Carrie Moore’s white satin frock is the one bold
bit of dressing. I less than admired her first
costume, a shortish cream frock, and big turned-
up hat, which seemed to be spelling Josephine
A-r-r-i-e-t. The white satin atoned for much. It
was Empire, long and flowing, with chiffon sashes
floating from the chest, and a novel slashing-up,
and letting-in of frilly chiffon over the left ankle.
Hair dressed high, with a shaky white aigrette
that had to be clung to in the dance. By the way,
to danoe in this flowing robe wasn’t easy.
Carrie gathered the tail up carefully, and let it
drop again several times before things were right.
Onee properly fixed, and the tucked (not frilled,
that s the latest) white petticoat showing, she can-
canned with Laurie and Brownlow so dashingly
that five recalls didn’t seem to satisfy the house.
I do hope Carrie Moore will not smash her health
by over-work. There are some terrible examples
of other clever little Australiennes to ponder over.
Dorothy Vane, as Little Buttercup, looks dark
and lost in a green poke-bonnet and black curls ;
so lost that you find yourself forgetting all about
her. Brownlow, as Captain Corcoran, took his
hot welcome with the same old damaging smile,
but showed a patch or two of cotton between the
velvet of his voice. Bathurst suffered from over-
done-up eyes. Little Stella Tracey’s Midshipmite
m blue and brass I wanted for my mantelpiece,
only that her genius for gesture would have upset
£ Ul ' ea - When the little voice squeaks
and flags as it does sadly sometimes —study the
play of that olever child’s hands. Like the lady
at the palmist’s, there is a future in her paw.
“Pinafore,” being short, we watched Biograph
pictures for half-an-hour. They were nearly all
warlike, wonderfully lifelike, and the only thing
wanting was a little audible swearing, and a little
wet “bluggy.” “A Skirmish with the Boers”
was almost good enough to gratify all the poor
little men whose wives wouldn’t let them go gor-
We also saw the Queen in Dublin. Was it
the Biograph whizzing? Or does Her Majesty
really bob, and bob, and bob without even stop-
ping long enough to let you see she is going to bob
again ?
The pictures remind me that a Sydney baby has
just been baptised “ Mafeking ’’—with all its life
before it.
Moreover the A.J.C. races on Saturday trotted
out a horse called Baden-Powell, but there wasn’t
patriotism enough among the other gee-gees to let
him win in a walk. Can you tell me where lies
the compliment in sharing names with a horse
that doesn’t win ?
Mrs Brough and Emma Temple watched
Saturday’s sport in twin black suits with white
vests and sailor hats. Mrs. Lovell—the Mel-
bourne girl who married the Leading Juvenile
last year’—was in a dark-blue, tailor-made, blue
toque, and white feather boa. Facially, Mrs.
Lovell might easily be mistaken for Grace Noble.
H you looked long enough you found the
whole of the Brough Co. taking a day out before
their departure for Brisbane. Which leaves Sydney
mourning, but otherwise the lawn was lonely and
unremarkable.
Edith Berry, after playing a succession of slaveys,
has got into an upper role—private secretary to
Nance O’Neil. People expecting an autograph
letter from Nance’s own little fist (noticeably
tiny for her huge proportions), have been crushed
with a note signed large and square by Nance’s
retainer.
In the green-room, the other day, I had a chat
with Miss O’Neil. She was doing Magda, and
came in holding her handkerchief to her cheek.
In the storm; and stress of her great emotions she
clenched her teeth so hard that a side-tooth broke,
and Magda added molar agony to her other mis-
fortunes., The coloured maid ran in and out with
remedies. Did you ever read “ Barbara Dering ? ”
It is a stoiw about a big, lovely American heroine,
wuose hothnilk baths and vivid hair were made
such a poem of by Amelie Rives. Nance and
her coloured Jenny bring Barbara and her black
TOud vividly before one. The actress’ skin is a
white gleam, and her pale hair shines delicately.
She wore the silk neglige of turquoise blue over
Chantilly lace, and made a slight change by
having violets in her hair. * Her hands were full
of violets, and, as they say in Bow Bells, looked
perfectly lovely. Conversationally, she doesn’t
shine. Conscious of this, perhaps, she never goes
anywhere, shuns society, and hasn’t been at a
single function—private or otherwise—since she
advented into Sydney.
A mild sensation emanates from our Premier’s
wife, who quietly refuses to be “ my-ladied.” All
her friends are requested to call her Mrs. Lyne.
Also, with marvellous courage, she alludes to
William John as plain “Mr.” A unique case of
declined, and by the woman.
A very different class of sensation agitates our
Advanoed Women. An Advanced Lady took an
Advanced Petition round an Advanced Woman’s
meeting some time ago—in Maoriland—to be
signed. Afterwards she tore off the names of
people that looked “ common ” —Mrs. Jones, Mrs.
Brown, Mrs. Smith. And now— not unnaturally,
you must confess —Mrs. Jones, Mrs. Brown and
Mrs. Smith have decided to stop Advancing—
down that sort of road, anyway.
By-the-way, I heard a woman remark the other
day, “ When I can get my dresses from Paris I
shall go in for Suffrage.” There’s a lot in that.
I’m waiting for that myself.
Miss Spence, from Adelaide, helped at a
Suffrage Meeting in Sydney last Monday. The
platform was decorated with Miss Rose Scott and
various Labor Ministers, and no better argument
could be found for the Cause than the speechifying
of the men v. the women at that meeting. Chairman
Began spoke; members O’Sullivan and McGowen
meandered, and sometimes shouted, and some-
times roared ; and member Thomas boggled into
an extraordinary bye-way about the father of the
Premier of N.S. W.
The women were a distinct improvement. Miss
Spence, a white-haired old lady, wearing a quiet
black bonnet, black silk cape and skirt, brightened
up her remarks with a twinkling eye and a little
bit of Scotch in her voice. To my mind, she
appeared neither as an orator nor an advanced
thinker, but then perhaps it was not the time for
anything of that sort, and considering the audience
simple sentiment was the most appropriate,
Simple sentiment, therefore, Miss Spence gave
them. Sweet-faced Rose Scott caused the man in
the gallery to titter over her careful enunciation
of Sir William Lyne, K-C-M-G, but the earnest-
ness and charm of this speaker are certainly
N.S.W.’s trump cards in the game of Suffrage.
“Justice” was often referred to at that meet-
ipg- Let me be' just. Walt Whitman says,
“ Each man for himself, and every woman for
herself is the byeword of immortality.” So let
mo put my own case, and be just to me. I went
to that meeting wanting to be convinced. If Suf-
frage is going to help this unfortunate old world
along, I am eager for Suffrage to be accomplished.
lam simply looking, for it. I was longing to be
convinced that the elite of women were equal to
the elite of men, as rulers and law-makers. But
the elite of men stayed away, so there was no
chance of comparison. I heard—from Mr. Alfred
Allen—that Miss Rose Scott was equal to, or
better than, the larrikin leaning against the post
outside. Do you think I wanted to be told that ?
That Queen victoria proved what women could
do as law-makers. How much Lex est rex, rex non
est lex was naturally forced upon my considera-
tion. That —(from Mrs. Young of 8. A. —in green
tailor-made) —one’s husband can think one a very
good woman even if one does vote. That—(from
Miss Golding)—the mind of man is unknowable
to man, and how much more is the mind of
woman unknowable ! Putting it all together, a
beginner, like myself, was just puzzled, and came
away as much beginner as ever.
Once a thin, quiet, weird-eyed old man near the
front, rose, and held up his arm patiently. I did
so want to know what that man wanted, but the
speeches went on, and at last he sat down
patiently and stood up no more. Perhaps he
wanted to know, you know ; like myself. Another
dash of color was lent by a lady-speaker who
paused. In the pause I distinctly heard a well-
known M.P. breathe half a sentence, not from
notes, but looking straight in front of himself as
he did so. The lady-speaker jumped at the sen-
tence, and went straight on to the end of her
speech. Curious, was it not ?
Invitations to the Evening Party at Govt.
House came out last week, and I am wondering if
a certain pathetic query in a Sunday paper is
apropos. “Ruby” writes that her arms are “a
bright red color, and covered with long black
hairs ! ” She wants to wear low sleeves. What
is she to do ? Poor Ruby ! I should think one
could only recommend a cage.
Mafeking finally relieved Sydney at the Ass.
Ground last Wednesday, when I don’t know how
many thousand school-children promenaded and
masqueraded, and marched and sang and shouted,
and wore themselves and their teachers out with
excitement. The affair was a huge success,
smiled on by the Governor, Ladies Mary Lygon
and Susan Gilmour —a bright-faced likeness of
Lady Mary, Mayoress Harris and 30,000 others.
The only cross on the celebration was not intended
for a subtle sympathetic link with Mafeking,
although it might have been taken that way—by
people who had dined. Hunger beset teachers
and children, and the preparations for feeding
them were not as comprehensive as they might
have been, and mixing with the crowds of little
ones returning at dusk one might have heard
many pitiful complaints, fully endorsed by weary
teachers and mothers taking home the celebrators.
Elsie Hall’s concert last week was chiefly
remarkable for the fact that the pianiste wore red,
and entertained twenty wounded sojers with
Chopin’s “ March Funebre. ” It didn’t occur to
Elsie till ten minutes before “Funebre” was due
that she might be opening old wounds, as it were.
She went through, but her nervousness crept into
her playing not a little.
Another concert was Miss Alice Hollander’s,
her second, given for folks who couldn’t get in to
the first. No. 2 was uncrowded. The young
contralto owns a telling organ, but it is much
harder to be a young contralto than a young
soprano, don’t you think ? A soprano can tra-la-la
things over, but contralto songs demand such a
flow of soul to give meaning to the bellow, that a
girl of seventeen is always just a little tame.
It wasn’t at this concert, but another crowded
one near it, that a dog sneaked up to the front
row and got violently trodden on. He set up a
howl, and the people at the back mistook the
sound for the fiddling, and clapped enthusiasti-
cally. Rather hard on musical instincts, that sort
of thing !
The Dog Show proper was opened by His Ex.
and aide Harris last Thursday. The opening was
brief, and after a hurried rush alongthe canines, the
vice-regal party disappeared. Mayoress Harris
and a few others took tea in the tea-room. The
party included a society bride who was contacted to
quarantine lately, and who described the tea-table
down there so vividly that we could all see the
pound of butter on a plate, jam in tins, and
broken whisky bottle full of dead flowers. It i$
not malapropos to mention plague and dogs toge-
ther, as tne Melbourne dogs all stayed away this
year on account of Sydney bubonics. Conse-
quently the show was smaller than usual, but there
were plenty of dogs to lose your heart to —if you
prefer the genuine article.
Here is an S.M. Herald advt. :
A LADY of extensive means is eager to Adopt a
Child of either sex. The baby must be born
between June and the end of August next, the mother
must be tall, dark, and of Irish extraction. Open one
week. —Mother, Herald office.
The booking of the little one to arrive, and the
conditions as to mother’s height, complexion, and
nationality, seem to imply a desired resemblance
to someone else, and give the impression that the
advt. is the foundation of a London Journal
romance in real life.

) “Jap. crows sang a war-song at the Exhibition
t’other night. Exactly like a corroboree chant, it
brightened things a bit. Before that the audience
spent its time investigating outside rows, just as
at a recent Town Hall sing-song. To the Exhi-
bition concert the Hi-Yei’s professional funny
man came early and drew weird music from a
horn. He put a Melb. newspaper (upside-down)
on the floor, and affocted to read music from its
pages as he played. Then he fixed it on the top-
mast, so to speak, and strained his eyes aloft. He
was a pretty little person, and he and his mates
trot round town hand-in-hand so guilelessly that
one wants to buy them chocolates, and say ‘ God
bless the innocents ! ’
“ Western District eligibles—some of them are
very eligible indeed—are one by one submitting
their necks to the yoke. The latest engagement
is that of the wealthy Mr. Manifold, whose
beautiful home lies a few miles out of Camper-
down. The tastes of the bride prospective are the
opposite of horsey, a fact which suggests that the
affections of the up-to-date sporting man are not
always captured by the up-to-date young sports-
woman.
“ The London policeman, ’tis said, is the guide,
philosopher and friend of mankind. So far, our
own boys of the baton-brigade, though right
enough, are not too sweet to live. T’other day,
when those wedding carriages (one of the hand-
some Bayles, of Toorak, had married a pretty
daughter of Dr. Gray, of Collins-st.) couldn’t get
past, the beauteous best man with his halo of 'fair
curls, lovely grey suit, tricolor in button-hole and
palpably tight boots, dismounted and begged a
constable’s advice. Despite this vision, plus
glimpses of palpitating bride, blushing bridegroom
and maids in misty white-and-azure, there was
nothing of the ‘By my word the Bonnie Bird in
danger shall not tarry r spirit in Blue Coat’s cold
reply.
The new Mrs. Bayles is one of the charming
Gray sisters, another of whom married a nephew of
the late Salmon Wilson, while a third is the wife
of a local barrister. Some time back the bride of
to-day, her sisters and cousins launched into busi-
ness on their own as society milliners, and their
firm still goes strong.
“Pretty nurses and other flirtatious types of
femininity enjoyed themselves while the Mafeking
riots raged. Ministering angels in crisp caps and
pinnies were in fascinating force on conspicuous
balconies and received ovations, notably from
“ proceshing ” medical students, who further took
innocent glee in flaunting human bones and squirt-
ing onlookers in the eye. 1 How many have you
got in the tank at present, Slasher?’ shouted a
bystander. ‘ Upwards of seventy ! ’ yelled young
Sawbones in reply. What worse could befall a
poor human husk, once the tenement of love,
grief and self-sacrifice, than to wobble in a pickle-
tub at the mercy of these callous Sangrados ?
“ A Geisha picnic happened out Heidelberg
way on Queen’s Birthday, and went off joyously
till night came, the dew darkened the earth, and
sitting out in the moonlight was rendered a rheu-
matic affair. At length, when last train had
streamed away to Melbourne, a great dread seized
the drivers of the picnic-drags, and they refused
very respectfully to take their tender young
charges home through the impenetrable fog.
There were female shrieks, arguments, inquiries,
exhortations, but at length the party resigned
itself to a night of ghost-stories around the big log
fires. At seven a.m. the drivers harnessed up,
and took the picnickers home to their distracted
relatives. Probably some of the more youthful
Geishas are still doing penance for the misad-
venture, which gave a chill to many maternal
souls.
“ Prof. Marshall-Hall capitulated so far to the
f races on the occasion of the students’ concert last
'uesday night as to sport a dainty button-hole,
probably presented by one of his adoring flock.
During the influx of a mob of swallow-tails and
chiffons after the first number, the swarthy genius
beat his familiar tattoo on the pulpit-rail. Catch-
ing sight of a few fair fiddlers giggling, he smiled
blackly himself, and changed the movement to tne
alleyro appassionato till the last shining shirt-front
and bit of chiffon had shuffled hastily into place.
The students of the Conservatorium are a brow-
beaten, hungry-eyed, earnestly-endeavoring col-
lection, plain of face and figure, indifferent to
dress, shutting their eyes to all but their art and
their master. Miss Ethel Bergin was the one
snappy frocker and soulful singer of the crowd.
She has dimples and a soul—a valuable combina-
tion.

but the violet-eyed wife of the Vic. baronet is ex-

“ Mrs. Kate Fraser, the lady who shot her hus-
band by accident, as a jury put it, made applica-
tion last week for a Melbourne hotel license. The
police offered a feeble resistance to her claim, on
the strength of an allegation that she had once
brought a revolver into play in N.S.W. or there-
abouts, but Mrs. F. emphatically denied connec-
tion with any firearm, save the one that sent a
bullet, by accident, into Dr. Fraser’s brain.
Police having nothing further to say on this
matter, there was no legal reason why Mrs.
Fraser should be prohibited from serving her
country with drinks. Lawyer Gillott incidentally
stated that his fair client had obtained a ‘ protec-
tion order ’ under the Marriage Act, so there was
no fear of her much-damaged husband coming to
live on the licensed premises.
“ At the Royal the ‘ Ladder of Life ’ still keeps
its end up, thanks to its fund of humor in connec-
tion with Highlanders’ legs. Eugenie Duggan,
as a hunted Nihilist, sports an artistic blend of
blue and purple. This sounds conspicuous in the
wearer’s risky position, but of course even a con-
spirator on trial for her life understands that to
look nice is her wisest course—always providing
that her judges are mere men. When woman
gets her rights and mounts the Bonch, she-culprits
will smart every time unless they’re dowdy, flat-
chested and frumpish. Miss Duggan’s voice
tones-down wonderfully. Nowadays it doesn’t
drive hearers to repent of their sins by calling-up
ideas of the “ Awful Trump of Doom.’’

by the way, rejoice in a pair of Scawtch bachelor

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Grand Master A. J. Peacock (Vic.) will be
39 and Ex-Premier Braddon (Tas.) will be 71 on
Monday June 11.
Sporting men Wallace and Wilson raced
each other into eternity, and the ex-owner of
champion Carbine won by half-a-day.
Premier Forrest’s sudden climb-down over
Federation was, it is said, prompted by a burning
desire to become the next occupant of Govt.
House, Perth.
Cape Premier Schreiner is married to the
sister of F. W. Reitz, formerly President of
the Orange Free State, and now State Secretary
to the Transvaal.
Mark Lemon, son of the well-known Lon-
don Punch editor and friend of Charles Dickens,
is now a clerk in the office of Nesbit, Q. 0., m
Adelaide. He was once a guardsman.
Mrs. Kate Fraser, heroine of the St.
Hilda shooting sensation, has taken out a license
for a pub. in Queen-street, Melbourne. Ihe
wonder is that the lady did not take to the boards
instead of the bar. She should have happened m
melodrama.
An Australian parent, proud of her
relationship to the reliever of Ladysmith, named
her son (who arrived most opportunely) after the
tardily-victorious general. But she forgot the
significance of her married name, and the
youngster staggers under the name Redvers
Puller Shute.
Postmaster-General Crick is the only
member of the present New South Wales Minis-
try who has not entered his name on Government
House visiting book. For 30 years of his political
life Parkes declined to enter his name there,
though while a Minister he visited Government
House on official business.
William Bibby, who so successfully
developed the splendid property of the Australian
Raub gold-mining co., Singapore, died recently,
aged 63. He had for over 40 years been a miner
and came to Australia when 21. Before taking
over the Raub management he was at Croydon,
Q. As a mining-organiser Bibby was a man in a
thousand.
THE LATE W. R. WILSON.
From a speech by W.A. Premier Forrest
on 19th May :
In the recent election campaign some of the candidates
had taken special pains to deny him. W lien Peter was
asked if he knew the Saviour he replied, “ I know not the
Man.” So it was with some of the aspirants to Parlia-
mentary honors —they knew not the man.
In same speech the Coolgarrlie Water-Works were
described as a “ God-like scheme ! ”
