The ex-Queen of Hanover is a year older
than Queen Victoria. Monarchy seems to be the
healthiest occupation in the world for a woman.
The ex-Empress of the French had only 17 years
of it, but at the age of 74 she is still feeling the
good effects.
“F.”: Wrong to say that Bertram Mac-
kennal had no honor in his own country. Say,
rather, no honor from his own countrymen, who
had no considerable market for his art. Wasn’t it
in Melbourne that Sarah Bernhardt raved about
the young man’s genius ?
Hon. A. J. Gould, M.L.C., is entitled to
three N.S.W. State railway-passes. One is for
life for long service in the Assembly, one as a
member of the Upper House, and one as a mem-
ber of Great Cobar Copper syndicate, which pays
the railways £30,000 a year in freights.
A prominent Cornstalk, returned from a
S’Africa trip, says he has an unbounded admira-
tion for Tommy Atkins and the British Artillery,
which seems to be absolute perfection. The
officers, however (again excepting the artillery
and engineers), are, for the most part, a set of
vapid asses, and utterly devoid of consideration
for their men.
Should J. P. Abbott resign the Speaker-
ship of N.S.W. Assembly—he says he doesn’t
mean to—the coveted chair will go—judging by a
careful counting of noses during last week—to F. B.
Suttor. Mr. Suttor held Ministerial office 23
years ago, and has had a quarter of a century’s
Parliamentary experience. He is a N.S. VV. native,
and 01 years of age.
The cabled intimation that Fowell-Buxton,
the unutterably dreary bore who figureheaded S. A.
for three years, has denounced Kingston for his
attack on Way, C.J., will surprise no one.
During the brewer-evangelist’s last year of office
he and Kingston (then Premier) were hardly on
speaking terms. The reason of the estrangement
is another story—a story of petticoats.
Editor Bulletin. —I have myself on
several occasions seen the Gladesville patient,
Cress well, when before the Royal Commission,
and I know that he is most anxious to be set at
liberty. He will not, he says, answer certain
questions while in the Asylum. He is not a
lunatic although to a certain extent “ soft,” and
some of the doctors think he would improve out of
detention. —I. B. C. H.
Australia’s old friend, Pepper, the “ghost”
showman of yore, was responsible for Matthew
Arnold’s repudiation of the title of Professor,
when he took the Chair of Literature at Oxford.
It was the time when “Professor” Pepper’s
Ghost flourished as an entertainment for children
(and parents) at London Polytechnic, and
the scholar said—“ Professor Pepper—Professor
Arnold ! No, thank you ! ”
Lieut. J. C. M‘Kenna, in a letter dated
Mafeking, March 26, published in a Melb. paper,
says :
Still beleaguered and happy. When we are to be re-
lieved goodness knows. I don’t want to be relieved ; I
never had a jollier time in my life.
Which reads curiously, in view of what we have
read about the dreadful distress of the garrisons
n the three besieged S’African towns.
The interest taken by half-a-dozen mem-
bers in J. P. Abbott’s prospective resignation of
N.S.W. Speakership is more than pathetic. Need-
less to say over a score of members are prepared
to accept the office, but half-a-dozen stand ready
to demand it. One of these latter indirectly
eulogises his fitness by impressing that the Speaker
should be a man of large independent means and
with capacity to revive the traditional hospitalities
of the position.
Nathan Young, alleged nephewof themany-
wifed Brigham, has turned up in the Old Men’s
Home, at Parramatta, after nearly 50 years’ gold-
fossicking in various parts of Australia. Nathan
was present when the mob murdered prophet Joe
Smith in Carthage. He afterwards shared in the
war between North and South and, without know-
ing it, entitled himself to a pension of £3 a month.
This pension he now, at the age of 71, goes to the
States to recover.
Every now and then the cable-liar tells us
that another brother of ex-President Steyn has
been captured, or shot, or something, until the
size of the Steyn family becomes as big a puzzle as
the identity of the particular Botha who falls into
the hands of the British at irregular intervals.
But both names are as common among the Boers
as are Brown and Robinson in England ; a list of
the first draft of prisoners to St. Helena contained
20 Steyns and 18 Bothas.
This war may yet, even in the hour of
triumph, prove the downfall of Cecil Rhodes. It
has inspired a national desire for the man’s past
history, and his enemies are rising to the occasion
by publishing pamphlets containing extracts from
his speeches in the days when he prated glibly
about “eliminating the Imperial factor from South
African politics.” That was when the great Cecil
wanted a free hand to enslave and apprentice and
compound the natives of the North.
Editor Howard Willoughby, of the Akgus,
thus replies to critics who have said to him that
the tone of the paper has not improved since the
days of “used to was“l don’t edit
the paper to please the Melbourne
Club, but to suit the suburban grocer.
The approval of superior persons is
gratifying, but it is the penny of
the average man that pays'. The
of my policy is its success.”
Hence the scareheads, the booming of
popular movements, the nursing of
the prejudices of the householder with
the cast-iron ornamentation on his
verandah, the flattering of the small
farmer, and—the increase of the
Argus circulation by thousands and
the feverish vexation of David Svme.
’Tis said that Willoughby has erected
over his bed the portrait of a sleek grocer,
aproned and side - whiskered, witli a bronze
aureole (the edge of a penny) round his head,
serving a mall girl at a counter with half-a-
pound of brown sugar. He says his prayers
to his apotheosised grocers.
The expression, “Journeyman Judge,’’
to effectively used by Kingston, Q.C., lately
in England as to have apparently caused the
Times .to reconsider its approval of the tem-
porary tenure for members of the pro-
posed Imperial Council of Appeal, ap-
pears to have been first used by
Purves, Q.C., at an indignation
meeting of the Vic. bar called to
protest against the appointment of
Wrenfordsley (since C.J. of Westralia) as
acting-Judge. Wrenfordsley was then carpet-
bagging it in a judicial sense about all the pro-
vinces. He took the phrase in very good part;
in fact, at a Trades Hall Council affair some time
after lie justified his claim to speak by the fact
that he was a duly-qualified “ journeyman Judge.”
Melbourne Akg us, mentioning the fact
that the late Donald Wallace was a member of
two separate Australian Parliaments—those of
Queensland and Vic. —claims it as “a unique dis-
tinction.” No such thing. Dr. Nicholson was
Speaker in N.S.W. Assembly and afterwards
Speaker in Q. Assembly. John Douglas, now at
Thursday Island, was M.L.A. of N.S.W., and
afterwards M.L.A. and Premier in Q. Arthur
Hodgson sat in the Parliaments of N.S.W. and
Q. ; John Hurley held a seat in the Parliaments
of both these provinces, and many others might
be cited to similar effect.
“F.” : Knew Ben Bridge, the horse-
stealer, in the Gulf before ho broke gaol at Burke-
town. He was the most daring horseman I ever
saw. he was one of the kindest men to
dumb animals. Of course he was up North under
an assumed name ; and when the police identified
him, he made a desperate attempt to escape by
swimming the Gregory, but was eventually
caught. He said afterwards that he had given up
horse-stealing, and that if the police had only left
him alone he would have been all right. He had
been on the station six months and was living very
quietly when arrested. He was taken to Burke-
town, and once more escaped by firing the lock-
up. Most people were rather sorry to hear of his
re-capture.
John Meagher, one of the recently ap-
pointed Cornstalk Ms. L.C., once opposed Frank
Suttor for Bathurst, and was beaten. He was (and
is) popular, but-is not a platform orator. In his open-
ing speech Suttor anticipated that he would be at a
disadvantage with an orator of Mr. Meagher’s
calibre. When his turn came Meagher disavowed
oratory, and to illustrate his meaning related an
anecdote. A priest happened to meet a boy, and
enauired if he said his prayers. The boy promptly
replied that “ he didnT, but that his brother was
a divil at them.” “ And,” continued the candi-
date, “it’s my brother that’s the divil at oratory.”
The late A. H. Jacob, Chairman of Com-
mittees of N.S.W. Leg. Council, was on one occa-
sion reported to have been killed by a fall from his
horse. As the Council was about to adjourn out
of respect for the dead legislator’s memory, Mr.
Jacob stalked in to his accustomed seat. His
biography had meanwhile been extensively pub-
lished. The late W. H. Suttor, of Bathurst, also
had the honor of an ante-mortem biography, his
family receiving many letters and wires of con-
dolence. Another prominent man on the Hunter
had a S.M. Herald column given him in the
nature of a biography while he was hale and
hearty. He demanded and obtained £lO from the
Herald people for expenses involved in answering
wires ana generally contradicting the obit.
“ Tangata-mata ” : The future Premier of
Maoriland is not hard to pick. Big Mackenzie,
therugged, blundering, but able Minister of Lands,
has been lying at his home in the South Island for
weeks past, and it is feared he will never again
take office. Seddon has also been burning the
candle at both ends. The old strength that used
to enable him to strap himself to the box-seat of
a coach and sleep in that position for hours as he
bumped over a rough road, and yet be ready for
midnight deputations (whilst his cursing, sleepy
secretaries could hardly stand), is gone, tie &nd
Mackenzie promise to be listed with the rest of
M.L. political martyrs that have succumbed in
harness—Atkinson, Ballance and many others.
Meanwhile the whole burden and heat is falling
upon Joseph Ward, the resuscitated one, and he
holds up the whole log well. He is just now
Colonial Secretary, Postmaster-General, acting-
Premier, Minister of Railways, and a host of other
things besides. He has become immensely popular
with all classes, and Seddon affectionately alludes
to him as ‘ My boy, the coming Premier.” Ward
is undoubtedly the strong man of the Seddon
party, and six months hence will see him on top.
Premier Seddon’s illness is the natural
result of overdoing it. The working machine that
was wont to rouse up his private secretary at 2.30
a.m. to start work, and leave him worn out and
go off cheerily on a long railway journey with two
fresh secretaries to cope with the rush, has got
weak at the bearings. For years his life has been
nothing but a whirling succession of tremendous
bursts of graft and banquets. He roared and
raged through Parliament, carrying all before him
like a mighty cyclone ; while awav in the recesses
of the big departmental building he did the work
of two absent Ministers as well as bis own, and
then fought a big election and pulverised more
thoroughly than ever the plutocratic Tones who
reckoned that the Reaction must be due. Then
came the plague-scare, and King Dick hurried
north to superintend the precautionary measures
that were being slowly wrangled over. When he
arrived the thing began to move, and the people
who objected to being put to trouble and expense
to cope with a disease which had not appeared
were suddenly squelched and trampled underfoot.
And now the giant has had three paralytic attacks
that have been repulsed, but show distinctly that
he is worn out, and that in a little while Pre-
mier Seddon will be added to the number of strong
men that Maoriland has ruthlessly used up in
shaping her destiny as the leading democratic
community of the world.
Adye Douglas was 85 on May 31. Sat
down with Henry Parkes in ’6l to “ dream ’’about
federal Australia. “ Practical ” politicians called
it a dream for over 20 years after. Douglas has
a big chance to see it a reality.
Strange that some of the obituary notices
of Donald Wallace forgot to mention the Mel-
bourne Cup victory which caused him to become
the owner of Australia’s immortal horse. It
was in ’BB that Mentor won the Cup for genial
Donald, beating the big up-country horse Tradi-
tion. At the same meeting Carbine, t en fresh
from Maoriland, had been jockeyed out of the
Derby by a short head, and when Carbine and Tra-
dition were presently offered for sale Donald
Wallace and his jockey, Mick O’Brien, were of
different minds about the business prospects of
the two cracks. Mick, having a great opinion of
Mentor, reckoned that Tradition, who had run a
close race in the Cup, would be a good invest-
ment, so he formed a syndicate of three to buy
the big ’un. Tradition fetched 3050 guineas at
auction, arid proved to be no good whatever.
Donald Wallace paid 3000 guineas of his Mentor
winnings for Carbine, and won more than
10 times that amount in stakes alone with
the matchless galloping machine. He didn’t lay
himself out for big handicap coups, he simply let
the horse go for big stakes, handicap or otherwise,
and the horse rarely failed to beat the field,
whereas a tricky owner would probably have con-
verted the same generous goer into a rogue by
running him “ stiff.” It was a good day for pun-
ters when Mentor put the Melb. Cup stake to the
credit of the man who subsequently bought Car-
bine. Mentor was the last to leave the saddling*
paddock. Donald Wallace and bis beautiful
young wife laughed at Mick O’Brien as he told
them to consider the race all over, but the san-
guine jockey said again, “ This is the happiest day
of your lives.” And he was a true prophet. Mrs.
Wallace died before next Cup-day came round,
and when Carbine won the £lO,OOO stakes in 00,
with more than lOst. on his back, poor Mick
O’Brien was too far gone in consumption to be
there.
THE LATE DONALD WALLACE.
What has become of the great Mr. Winston
Churchill? But a few short months ago we had
him every morning for breakfast in the cable —his
exploits, his marvellous escapes, his daring, his
opinions, his idea of what the Generals ought to
do, &c. And now he seems to have been suddenly
turned off at the meter in the full flush of his
management of the S’African war. AVhy is this ?
Can it be jealousy on Roberts’ part ? or was he
cut off from this end—by a combination of Aus-
tralian editors who had grown weary of the
youth’s exasperating monopoly of the military
limelight ?

Aboriginalities.
“Lar. They handle strychnine care*
lessly in West Queensland. Have often seen the
“dog-stiffeners” start out on their rounds with
baits for the dingoes—in a leather wallet which
also held the man’s tucker for the day (wrapped in
paper if the hut-cook had time, if not, rolled in a
handkerchief and pitched in alongside the baits).
Given a hot day, melting grease and a soluble
poison in same bag, and things are liable to get
“a bit thick.” One old “stiffener” was once
found by station hands lying on the ground and
twitching like a dog with a bait inside nim. They
gave him a mouthful of tobacco-water, tied his
feet together with a saddle-strap and hoisted him
up to the limb of a brigalow ; then they twisted
his body round and round till he vomited ; and in
a couple of days the patient was little the worse.
A couple of months later, however, same man was
found stiff and stark at the edge of a billabong to
which he had crawled for a drink ; he had tom the
ground up all round him in his agony. They gave
what poor Paddy had left of his last lunch to a
dog they wanted to kill—and i'„ was dead in
15 min.

“A. 0.”: For the information of “R.W.S.”
(B. 12/5/’00): Take a solid junk of deal, or any soft,
dry wood, and shape a groove on one side of it
about 7in. long. Place junk in a firm position,
and, with a stick of hard, dry wood, endeavor to
deepen that groove in the quickest time on record.
Writer has obtained fire by this method in under
smin. By rubbing two sticks together you would
probably get results when you would be too old to
appreciate them.

which he knows will fail. All L have seen chose
two pieces of hardwood —one partially rotted and
the other sound—and in a few minutes, by rapidly
twirling the sound stick in a hole in the decayed
one, heat and smoke were produced in the fine

“K.S. :Be animal chivalry—or, rather,
affection. What about the brush turkey, which,
having laid her eggs in the mound and covered
them up, troubles no more about them and wouldn’t
know her own chicks if she met them ? Fortun-
ately for the youngsters, they can get their own
living as soon as hatched, and can run like the
wind from danger. I have found as many as 100
eggs in one nest, and, as one bird can’t have laid
all these, there must have been an arrangement
between the hens to lay in a common nest.

Dear Bulletin, —“ Synd’s ” assertion (8.,
26/5/’00) that “the N.S. Wales cockie sends
his youngsters to school to do the 70 —or
even 140—days for the year straight off,”
is pure bunkum. If the cockie were to send
his youngsters for the 140 €ays “straight
off ” and then keep them at home, he would still
be liable for the second half-year ; and, as for the
70 days assertion—in writers school (all farmers’
youngsters) the enrolment for quarter ending
March was 24, average attendance 16'3. Present
quarter, enrolment 27, average will be about 18.
According to “Synd,” average for March would
have been 24, and for current quarter 27. Yet
every pupil attended at least two days in every
week. —Dominie.

“ Tell ” : A big iguana used daily to
steal eggs from my hens’ nest. Killed it at last
and on opening it found inside two unbroken eggs.
Another time killed a big carpet-snake near
nest of brooding hen and found in him three eggs
which had been purloined from the nest. These
were put back and in due time hatched out with
the rest. Those three chicks have early entered
on a “ career of adventure ” !

“Cecil F.” : Re the hatching of reptilian
eggs (B. 5/5/’OO and 26/5/’00). From Borneo I once
sent to London 120 eggs from the Bahalla bird ■
nest caves, supposed to be the produce of the
edible-nest-building swallow. But upon arrival
the packet was found to contain about 50 young
lizards, some of which were still alive. As em-
bryo swallows were found in some of the unbroken
eggs, I could only conclude that through laziness
the “ boy” who had gathered the eggs for me had
made up the required number with lizards’ eggs.
Turtle and crocodile eggs, by the way, in tropical
countries need no artificial heat to cause vivifiea-
tion ; in fact, croc.’s eggs taken from the ovaries
of the parent have been successfully hatched by
me. ,

“ Vril.” : Re teetotal swaggies. I
met one once near Bogantungan (C.Q.),
but I grant he was a freak (of nature.
Scandinavian by nationality, Wander-
ing Jew by nature —for 27 years he had
“padded.” His hair, about 18in. long,
was carefully tied up with a piece of
blue ribbon, and his grey beard, reach-
ing almost to waist, was cleanly kept.
Foot-gear : cypress-pine sandals, se-
cured by greenhide latchet-strings.
The Cats-kill mountains silhouetted on
my brain. “Are you Rip Van Win-
kle?’ I said. “Xo, I vas Henrik
Bjomsen, and vas you able to gif me
a leedle bit of tea ? ”

young 7ft. alligator had grabbed a sow on the

rolled with him into the water, emerging
promptly therefrom, however, in safety. A few
days afterwards the’gator was shot, and found to
have a gash bin. long beneath the forearm.

• Myall ” : In answer to “ Xerxes ”
(8., 12/5/’00): Gilgai is tho name given t°
a hole, or collection of holes (found in the
brigalow scrubs more especially). They are
filled with water in a wet season, and will
hold for a considerable time. They are not
caused by any animal, and are often sur-
rounded by a thick ring of brigalows. They
are sometimes called “ melon-holes.” Also,
to “ Wil.” (8., 12/5/’00). 1 have seen black
cockatoos, and loss frequently white ones,
“barking” the limbs of box and white-
gum. Again, to “Stalky” (8., 12/5/’00).
The “porcup ne” lays eggs, which it hatches
in a temporary pouch. It has no teats, but
an area of the breast becomes glandular and
serves the same purpose. The pouches
only exist in the breeding-season.

“ Yangan ” : Re ability of turtles to
return themselves (“Lyndhurst,” B. 5/5/’00).
Given time and suitable ground and any
turtle will do so, not by “rocking” but by
flicking away the sand as it spins round
on its back, thus gradually forming a gutter
all round, and into this the turtle will tip at
such an angle that it will be able to lever
itself right-way-up again. The operation
requires many hours to complete, but I have
seen it accomplished more than once when
hunting for turtle-eggs on islands off Borneo.
“Xerxes”: / e fish swallowing fish
(B. 12/5/’00), I once set a small hook baited
with worm in Murrumbidgee, and next
morning had a 121 b. cod on. Found that a
small bream had taken the worm and had
been swallowed head-first by the cod,
which the back-spines of bream had then
hooked. I can swear to this, but, of course,
every old “whaler’’has caught the “nest
of fishes”—varying in number from three to
ten. according to the appearance of the
auditor—one inside the other, and ending
with a final surrounding of about lHOlb.
of cod. All of which is possible.
“ Alpar ” : While we were
drafting sheep “Jaeky” (darkie)
picked up a £1 note in one of the
yards, and somebody spotted him
and told the boss. Boss fearing
“Jaeky” would be off to town,
and not wanting to lose his services*
called the black up “Jaeky,”
lie said, “you bin pickem up one
pound. That pellar belonga me.”
“Was him ole man (dirty) pound,
or young man (clean) pound ? ”
asked Jaeky, and the boss, naturally
supposing the note would be soiled,
said : “ Ole man, Jaeky !” “ Vella
(very)man?’ asked Jaeky. “Yes,”
said the 8., “vella.” Then Jaeky
put his hand into his moles, and
pulled out a brand-new note ! There
was a very drunk aboriginal in
town next day.
“YVilgar”: A gipsy gang re-
cently visited this (Vic.) town and
camped in the drain of the local reser-
voir. Lovely ! Used to go round ask-
ing to have a glass of water brought, and while the
housewife would be getting it would pocket any-
thing portable. Then they would want a couple
of shillings placed on the table each side of the
glass. More running away for purses, and more
snapping-up of trifles. When the purse would be
forthcoming, they would jabber some more, and
eventually depart with a goodly spoil. One local
bank-manager said that a £l7 bag of silver had
been handed in by some of these beauties.

If certain statements contained in reports
in the Vic. Police Department anent the Kelly
' gang are to be believed, that crowd narrowly
escaped making a political landmark in Aus-
tralian history. These reports indicated the
existence of such a widespread state of disaffection
in N.E. Victoria owing to what was called the
“ remand-ring” as applied to persons “guilty ”of
being Kelly sympathisers, that the Kellys had
determined to take advantage of it for their own
purposes. They had resolved, it was said, after
having upset the special train containing the
police from Melbourne, to make a cut across
country from Gienrowan to Bonalla, destroying
bridges and telegraph lines en route, and there to
have proclaimed N.E. Vic. a republic with
Benalla as capital. This move was stopped by
the failure of the effort to destroy the train, owing
to a miscalculation of the time at which it was to
arrive. But for this hitch, it is asserted,
nothing could have averted the railway catastro-
phe as a prelude to the Presidency of Edward
Kelly, Esq., supported by nine men out of every
ten in the disaffected district.

“ Malle ” : He T.C.F. (Bulletin 19/5/’00).
The ganggung is not brown but grey; in other
respects it answers description given. Has about
tlie most raucous note of any of the parrot tribe.
They are common in Southern coastal ranges and
are reputed to be splendid talkers. Have shot
many on Upper Murray for specimens.

pub. when the township went off its head over the

HENRY LAWSON; photo, of a quick sketch in oils by John Longstaff.
The famous old Maori priest, Rangitahau,
who conducted the ceremonial over the recent
opening of the carved house at Rotorua (M.L.j,
has since died, and the day after his decease his
old comrade, Te Hiinopo, also died, after but a
few hours’ illness. This, as predicted 50 years
ago (according to the natives), completes the
seven victims doomed on account of some law of
tapu. said to cover the carved house.

Dear Bulletin.
The sententious state-
ment made by “ Puy-
yaka” in 8., 5/6/’OO,
that “ the death-ad-
der is ... . the only
viviparous Australian
snake, all others lay-
ing eggs,” is untrue.
Several venomous
Australian snakes
bring forth their young alive ; and if readers of anti-
quated cyelopindias don’t know this all snake authorities
(to. Probably every country school-boy in Australia
knows that during the breeding season living young, Sin.
or 9in. long, can always be found in the egg-vessels of red-
bellied black snakes, tiger snakes, and several black
s lakes of the genus Iloploccphalus, to which the tiger
snake belongs. If the snakes just mentioned had never

At the annual convention of tne Victorian Auxiliary of The London Missionry Society, Mrs. Halley referred in a mournful fashion to the fact that the lady missionaries who went to China had
developed a great tendency towards marriage. “With one honorable exception.” Mrs. Halley remarked, "the Ladies don’t seem to be able to resist the marrying tendency. In eleven years fivo
ladies have been sent out from this auxiliary, and of these four hare married. Some have married the missionaries of other societies. Others have married merchants. Even the fifth is now about
to be married.”

An old Melbourne identity died the other
day, when Mr. Hamil Kerr went over. He and
his brother Robert arrived in Victoria in ’46, and
started business as grocers in Collins-street,
between Swanston and Elizabeth-streets, in which
street at the same time (keeping a greengrocer’s
shop) was William Nicholson,_ “ father of the
ballot ” and Premier of Victoria in 1559. Fashion-
able Gollins-street would now turn up its nose at
cabbages and cauliflowers being sold upon the
Block.

Delegate Dickson is on the horns of a
hideous dilemma. If that bauble doesn’t soon
materialise, his candid friends will murmur things,
and if it does, what about his candid enemies !
Said that John See will be nominated for
the Federal Parliament, Brother Sam for the
State Parliament seat of Grafton, and Brother
Dave for the State Parliament seat of the Clarence l
Ihe British Independent Labor-party’s
strenuous opposition to the Transvaal war is in
striking contrast to the truckling and time-serving
attitude of the Australian Labor-parties. It’s the
difference between backbone and belly.
1 he rush of IST.SAY. Labor members and
Radical members generally into the profession of
Law is a striking commentary on the sustained
antagonism of both these parties to allowing law-
yers to become numerically strong in Parliament.
M llwraith s transcontinental railway
scheme is small beer to the present contemplated
niching per private-railway land-grants, of the
best of the glorious mineral lands of the Northern
province. Ihe Philp Govt, now throws off the
mask with a vengeance.
“A Member ” : How military enthusiasm
is manufactured. I have just asked the only mes-
senger whom I found at one of the Sydney
Govt. Departments where the other messengers
were. “ Oh, they have all been sent out to give
the troops off the Moravian a “ oheer ! ”
Q. Premier Philp's assertion that “ it had
been abundantly proved that private enterprise
oould work mining railways hotter than the
Government,” was made in the land of the Char-
ters lowers and Mt. Morgan railways—-two of the
best-paying mining State railways on the face of
the globe.
“ Ancient ” . - lie N.S. VV. Minister Crick’s
alleged desire for the portfolio of Lands. Always
understood he could have had the post from the
start, but, as he commands the best Lands practice
m the profession, to hold the position would com-
Eel him to refuse all cases connected with the
iand Courts.-
The secretary of the London branch of the
W.A. Goldfields league prophesies that blood will
yet be spilt in Westralia if Bigjohn Forrest
persists in refusing the gold-mining towns their
fair share of representation. And more im-
probable things than that have been spilt on even
smaller provocation.
HIS OPINION.
“ Now, honestly! Do
you think a picture
like that should have
been hung ?”
“I do not. I think it should have been shot!”
An extended advt. signed “J. W. Holli-
man” notifies that N.S.W. Public Service Board
will in July next hold a competitive examination of
candidates (male) for employment. Pay : £39 per
annum for the first year. Then says the advt. :
“Persons already employed in the general division
of the Public Service will be admitted to the
examinations, provided their age does not exceed
30 years.” Thirty years—£39 per annum ! Where
is O’Sullivan’s minimum wage ?
Writes There may be only 300,
or even only 30, members of N.S.W. Licensed
V ictuallers’ Association, but then the brewers and
W. and S. merchants vote for their bond-slaves,
the tied houses. The number of members in the
L.\ .A. looks as if there were only that many free
houses in the province.
\ abien Solomon, who was Premier of
S.A. for a week, last Dec., has again been made
leader of the Assembly Opposition. Parliament
meets about the middle of this month, and, in all
probability, there will be a no-confidence motion
fired at the Holder Govt, within a week or two of
the beginning of business.
Perth Morning Herald, of which Alick
Forrest is chief shareholder and chairman, yells
for a reduction of food-duties and abolition of the
stock-tax, which in Groperland runs up to 30s. for
every beast imported. As Brer Alick is boss of
the meat-ring, which has sent the price of beef to
Is. per lb., the attitude of the Herald is effron-
tery personified.
N.S.W. Labor-party should avoid the
Upper House’ till the members thereof are
paid for their services. How can a carpenter or
plasterer make an honest living and hold a useful
place in that Chamber ? Or else the people who
support the Labor Party should subscribe £3OO
a-year to save the Labor M.L.C. from the tempta-
tion of selling himself soul and body.
According to the recent decision of
N.S.W. Supreme Court in the long-contested
-National Guard case, the volunteer corps, which
are armed, clothed, and drilled at the public
expense, belong nevertheless to their amateur
commanding officers—such people as George
Republican Dibbs and J. C. Neild —as absolutely
as if said C.O.’s paid and maintained them.
The Secretary of Sydney Slock Exchange
calls upon the united Australian Govts, to cable
Chamberlain and have Pretoria re-named “ Vic-
toria,. Thinks it would be “happy and signifi-
cant,” also that it would “ please the Empire.” It
would also be most brilliantly original. But why
not call it New Jerusalem, or Cohenstadt, or
Wernherburg, or Nabothvillo? The truth is
mighty.
Ihe final results of S.A. Leg. Cpuucil
elections show that, taking the four districts, the
total Tory majority was about 2800 votes in a poll
of about 25,000. In the Southern, Central, and
Northern districts 1601 more votes would have
put in six supporters of Household Suffrage
instead of three, and so would have carried the
Bill. In the Central and Northern districts 480
votes more would have returned four supporters
instead of two.
One probable opponent of consistently-
Radical Jack Murray at next Vie. elections is a
personage noted for the ease with which lie turns
on the tap of earnestness in any available cause or
for any profitable adventure. A pro-Boer himself
before the Murray treason, hid indignation at the
latter’s “disloyalty” swung him gracefully to
ardent Imperialism at the very moment when a
hopeful vacancy yawned invitingly at Warrnam-
bool. His election speeches should be models of
apologetics, as they will no doubt be admirable
exercises in the mathematics of earnestness.
There was a deal of soreness in
official circles over the annexation of
the Fijis, which did not reach the public
ear at tho time. Commodore Good-
enough and Mr. Layard were appointed
by the Gladstone Govt., in ’73,-to pro-
ceed to Fiji as Commissioners to inquire
into and report on the state bf affairs
there. They were anxious to secure
the annexation of the country, and both
of them looked for the Governorship of
the new province. Goodenough worked
hard for it, and Layard, not the most
prudent of men, said openly that he was
promised the position before he left
England. Owing to the action of J. B.
Thurston, Chief-Secretary in King
Thakombau’s Govt.—who was persist-
ently ignored by Goodenough the
Commissioners were only able to obtain
an offer of cession which was so
hampered by conditions that the
British Govt, was certain to refuse.
When the report reached England,
Beaconsfield had superseded Gladstone;
and Hercules Robinson was deputed to
go to Fiji and fix up matters. The
two Commissioners were greatly
incensed. Goodenough wrote a strong
remonstrance to the Imperial author-
ities, and Layard threatened, like an
ordinary English paterfamilias, to send
a letter to the Times. Robinson
squared Thurston in one act by meet-
ing his views as to employment, and
obtained an unconditional surrender of
the group. Thurston was made Colonial-
Secretary of the Crown colony, and as
he was the only member of Thak.’s
Govt, who did not lose by the change
of sovereignty, he was sent to Coventry
by his former colleagues. He was an
able man, and an untiring worker, with a thorough
grasp of the political and financial possibilities of
the country. But for his assistance the first Gover-
nor, Sir Arthur Gordon, would have been lost.
In course of time the old animosities died out, and
Thurston became a highly popular Governor.
When Kingston put the question of S.A.
Council amendment to the people at large by
referendum, his reform scheme was approved by
49,000 voters (10,000 majority). But when the
Leg. Councillors, at their own little election, asked
their own little constituency of property-voters if
it was quite satisfied with the arrangement,
whereby it had all the Council voting power to
itself, 16,000 (about 3000 majority) voters
rose up and said the present arrange-
ment suited them very well. The majority
for reform was as large as the whole anti-reform
vote ! And the local Tory Critic calls this “the
clear and emphatic verdict of the PEOPLE”
against reform. Ye gods !
A SPION-KOP-PER.
Perhaps the two most unexpected of the
nominees to N.B.W. Leg. Council are Messrs.
Alex. Ross and W. M. Robson. Both owe their
elevation to services rendered to Premier Lyne
and Attorney-General Wise in their respective
constituencies of the Hume and Ashfield. Espe-
pecially the latter, for both of Wise’s narrow vic-
tories over his opponent, Bavister, were in no
small measure due to the energy and influence of
his chairman, Mr. Robson.
N.S.W. Chief Secretary See, at the in-
stance of the Labor - party, contemplates the
appointment of a young and efficient Inspector of
Scaffolding. Yet it was a Ministry comprising
both Lyne and See that appointed the last In-
spector of Scaffolding, and the person selected
was Mr. Luscombe, the then secretary of
the Protection Union. Luscombe was an aged
(about 70) and infirm ex-M.L.A., and his appoint-
ment was received with such derision that it was
soon cancelled.
The Lyne Govt.’s appointment of 12 new
men—mostly nobodies—to N.S.W. Leg. Council
brings that Chamber up to 79 members. But
the country is on the eve, if old promises pre-
vail, of having both Upper and Lower House
numerically cut down, on account of so many
important political matters passing over to
the Federation. Few, except party politicians,
felt a crying need for any accessions at the present
time. There will only be so many the more to kill
a month or two hence.
David Forrest’s new electorate of Ashbur-
ton (W.A.) nominally contains 42 electors, but the
number of resident electors is about four or five.
The rest are absentees with propertv-votes. The
plural property-vote is a great factor in W.A.—
Alick Forrest is a voter in 34 different electorates.
David didn’t go up to his constituency to be
returned because there was nobody there. He
just stayed in Perth and : was elected unopposed.
No one has ever been opposed in Ashburton since
it was a constituency.
“ JFor the lirst time in our history,” cries
a frantic Sydney Telegraph Jingo
correspondent, “ the blood of
Australians has been let in, we
believe, a noble cause. ’’ Which is
a lie, and a slander on the dead.
Australian blood was spilt in the
noble cause of Freedom at Eureka
Stockade at Ballarat many years
ago. There were neither gaudy
uniforms nor pay nor titles nor
promotion to be gained there—
the men of Eureka Stockade
fought for Freedom only, The
latter-day crawler may do his best
to ignore that episode, and the
latter-day Jingo liar may try to
drop it out of history, but Aus-
tralia’s first attempt at war was
a far grander event than anything
done by the contingents that went to
, aid an army of 220,000 in downing an
irregular and ill-trained force of 35,000.
Vic. P.M.G. Watt, when tackled on
the statement that telegraph operators, at
£2OO a year, who went to Africa in khaki, have
been replaced by cheaper men, could only say
that these temporary substitutes at £7B a year
will soon give way to minimum-wage operators at
£oo a year. The recent cry of “ sweating ”in the
Govt, telegraph service would be all the louder if
the public knew that “ messengers ” at 12s. a
week do the work of operators in small coun-
try offices. A messenger enters the
Civil Service as a boy of 16, say,
and increases in wisdom and
stature for years without getting
anywhere near a pound a
week. Some of the “ mes-
senger” telegraphists in
the Vic. Postal Dept,
are weary, grey -
haired boys with a
wife and family
apiece to help them
dissipate their
woekly thirty - five
“ bob ”

and found
wanton”:

In one of Q. Agri-
cultural - Minister Chata-
way’s recent letters to his
paper, Mackay Mkkcury, his
own name crops up something
like 101 times in connection with
groat movements for the public good.
And yet in Brisbane not one man in
5000 knows Chataway by sight, and not one in
a hundred ever even heard of him except in connec-
tion with his —er —administration of the Repur-
chase of Lands Act.
David Storey, the soft-goods member for
Randwick, delivered a denunciation of the Lyne
Ministry one day last week, during which he
received the moral support of Henry Clarke, the
venerable member for Bega. The latter, who
has been a supporter of the Protectionist party
for years, was keenly disappointed at being
passed over when Premier Lyne formed his
Ministry. It is to be hoped that he has a
better reason than this for becoming a subscriber
to the Storey brand of politics.
The M'Lean-Shiels Govt, hopes to get out
of its difficulties after Parliament has re-assembled
by asking for a dissolution, to enable it to appeal
to the country on the question of the immediate
introduction of an old-age pension scheme. It is
admitted that the money is to come from the
general or customs revenue, and before the plan
can be put into operation the customs revenue
will be transferred to the Federal Govt. It is
clear, therefore, that the proposal is a mere sham,
to bolster up a tottering administration.
As a measure of the inadequacy ol the
news received by Australians per cable, not ono
word has been wired out about the army-contrac-
scandals, the dismissal of certain contractors for
dishonesty (through which poor Tommy Atkins
has had to suffer), their reappearance under fresh
names, and the futile efforts of British Ms.P. to
secure their prosecution by the War-office. As a
specimen of the incompetency of the War-office,
take Under-Secretary Powell Williams’ ex-
planation of how they could not trace one “croak"
contractor who had got back under another name.
“ As to Ross,” he explained, in effect, “the diffi-
culty in tra ing him arose in this way. We were
after him for coal, as a matter of fact he was in
under forage, where we never expected to find
him.” Even the gentlemen who supplied whole
regiments with none but left boots remain un-
punished.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Metal—Maid of Kent nag Duke of Kent, winner
of Exeter Stakes (6 fur.) at A.J.C. June meeting (Rand-
wick) on Saturday, ran fourth to his stable-mate, Dandy,
in recent Royal Stakes, and as more than one “trier”
rarely comes from same quarter, observant punters
singled out the Duke as a “ good thing.” Books and
public made him a hot favorite when betting opened, but
as the proper people didn’t appear to be coming with
their “ bit,” he receded to an outside figure. By-and-by
after being nibbled at quietly, Duke of Kent was rushed,
and he left the paddock—branded the best of good things
- —at threes. So it turned out, for, despite interference
that seemed to render his efforts hopeless, he shot forth
near the finish and won handsomely from Yellow and
Black (owned by those who had Merlo'olas) and the crack
country performer, Myosotis, as named. Owner of Duke
of Kent—" A. Stanley,” a big punter whose ill-luck on the
turf is frequently commented on—got him for 200 gs.
quite recently, and netted a good deal more than that
sum on Saturday.
» * * •
In Saturday’s Steeple at Randwick, the winner
(Brokerage—by the Broker—Hermitage), with list, up,
won all the way from such good cattle as Minister for
Lands Hassall’s Crusado (11.0) and H. L. Mackellar’s
Taipo (10.10). Brokerage took his jumps like a true
hunter, never making the semblance of a mistake at any
time, and stamped himself as far and away the best
’chaser Sydney has seen for many a day. The new course
at Randwick is catching on in great style—particularly
the hill at the back, which, lends a true cross-country
appearance to the whole thing.
» • * *
Backers of the Deemster (Hurdles) and Cre-
mona (Ellesmere Stakes) at Randwick, Saturday, were
lucky in having to collect. Donald—property of Lands
Minister Ilassall—should have won former event. He
often jumped slovenly, lost several lengths at the mile-
post through mulling, blundered again at the 6-fur., and
was then only a head behind when passing the judge. In
Ellesmere Stakes, Dandy’s rider kept in the rear until the
last half-mile, and then it was a big struggle through the
crowd. Kelso’s top-notcher—humping top weight (9.5)
showed himself equal to the occasion until he struck
Cremona, when a neck-and-neck battle resulted in the
latter’s favor by a long head. Dandy’s effort evoked
admiration from every man jack on the course.
» » * •
“Guido ” : Glaring instances of “flesh of one,
fowl of another,” were shown at two recent meetings at
Randwick. Tatt.’s Club stewards challenged the running
of Fairy Prince in Winter Stakes—and failed to notice the
indifferent exhibition of Oarberra, who finished nowhere
in same race, after easily winning Rosehill May Handicap
previous Saturday. S.T.C. stewards questioned Royal
King’s running in Novice Handicap, but overlooked the
§oor display in T.O. Handicap of Alice Mostyn, who five
ays previously won Tatt.’s Welter Handicap; and the
erratic running of Crosslake, who finished last in Tatt.’s
Welter, ran nowhere in T.O. Handicap, and two days
later was second in Birthday Cup. As a further illustra-
tion of how clubs bestow their favors, Alix’s running may
be cited. She easily won Rosehill Stewards’ Mile, and
ran nowhere a week later in Tatt.’s Mile Welter, but only
five days afterwards easily won T.O. Handicap. In ques-
tioning Royal King’s running, which writer believes was
fair and square, another is added to the many instances
of an owner, who is. not in the inner circle, being made
the victim of a pretentious effort to check suspicious
things.

Not generally known that Cast Iron’s first win-
ning bracket was gained in a hack-race at Quirindi,
N.S.W. He is only a 14’2, and full brother to Plain
Bill 11., who has won numerous races in northern N.S.W.,
and half-brother to the great galloway sprinter, Luther,
now racing in Vic. Another item of interest is that Cast
Iron is trained by Jim Smith, who won A.J.O. Metrop.
and Summer Cup on Secundus and The Gem respectively.
• • * *
“Punter”; Gush in various papers re A.J.C.’s
action with regard to tote and double shops driving
leviathan bookie Oxenham out of the country sickens one.
If A.J.O. succeeded in wiping these parasites out alto-
gether that body would deserve a monument as high as
Kosciusko. Backers have no hope with them—they have
so many “ stiff ’uns ”in the bag. One Sydney city shop
seems to have got the grip of three or four big stables and
rarely dabbles in “ scrouchers.” Writer has seen long
odds offered against at the shop referred to when another
Ikey, not in the know, would cramp the prices or ask for
odds himself. It’s always the thing to call at this place
before going out to the course, as one gets a good line by
the figures on the board. Other shops operate more or
less similarly, but not so extensively.

Paul Pry’s victory in Adelaide Birthday Cup
stamps him as a great horse—he carried 10.5 home in a
mud-heap. It is a great pity such a fine animal is a
gelding. Although the books would not lay more than
10’s, he paid £l4 14s. on the tote.

Evidently the Adelaide people were altogether
wrong in their estimate of Gungadin’s chance for last
V.R.O. Derby. T’other day, in the Birthday Cup, when
the colt was sandwiched between Paul Pry and La Cara-
bine, they were giving him 351 b. and 181 b. respectively.
Had the race been 100yds. longer La Carabine would have
beaten the other two handsomelv.

A.R.C. Cup meeting ended at Victoria Park
(Adelaide) Saturday. Attendance was big and £13,516 15s.
(£1305 15s. less than same day last year) went through
machine. Cup (If miles) produced a most exciting
finish. La Carabine (9.7 up) displayed marvellous
stamina. Catching Gunga-Din (who looked like winning
easily) near the post, as if shot from a catapult, the Car-
bine—Oratava mare snatched the race out of the fire.
Time (3.6£) evidences that the pace was sound from end
to end. La Carabine was made a hot pot. Tote showed
£2549 invested ; dividend, £ll6s.

S.A. Birthday meeting furnished another big
tote div. of £6O odd. Against the winner, Trapper, the

* * * *
The trotting rule re declaration of overweight
appears to have fallen into desuetude in M.L., but a
couple of recent happenings will tend to make those con-
cerned more careful. It has been customary for jocks—
some of them jumbos—to get on the scale, show that they
weigh over (say) 10st., and then hop off without declaring
how much they are over the required weight. Thus, a
14st. man could ride or drive a nag that “ wasn’t having
any ” although apparently doing its best; while later on
with a 10 stoner as pilot he could make a race of it.
Four stone makes a mighty difference. Verily, there is
no end to horse-rogueries.
* * * *
The death of ,W. R. Wilson is in one sense not
such a great loss to the turf as some horse-
reporters would make out. Of late years
punters got but little encouragement to lay
odds on hot favorites from the St. Albans stable,
nor did any of Wilson’s best horses win big races, year
after year, like the old-time cracks. Strathmore, Zalinski,
Havoc, Carnage, Wallace, Aurum, Bobadil—not one of
them became a popular identity. They had bad tempers,
bad habits, bad feet or bad luck, or something, and their
reigns Were short. Wallace is remembered as a brilliant
and always trustworthy 3-year-old, but he didn’t train-
on. In the matter of running good horses when they
were not fit or couldn’t stay the distance, Mr. Wilson com-
pared unfavorably with such owners as the late James
White. All the same, he was a sport who desired to
maintain a fair average respect for himself, and as a
breeder in particular he deserved better fortune than he
got.

The ruling passion of Horsetralia. As the

* • * *
“Cornstalk”: lie whilom Melb. Cup winner
The Grafter’s success in recent English City amt Suburban
Handicap, for which another Australian nag (Sverla), from
same stable, and owned by “ Oxy,” was made favorite.
Files by mail indicate that there were “ructions” over
the matter —at least, that is the word used by London
Sportsman, which professes not to understand the affair,
and states that the backing of Syerla was past its com-
prehension, for, “if the clever section thought Syerla
superior. to The Grafter at the weights, it could only be a
reason for backing neither, as on no form that he has ever
shown could Syerla be made out a winner.” The more
information we have the more puzzling does the whole
thing become. Those immediately connected assert that
The Grafter was devoid of condition, and carried little or
no stable-money ; yet he started second favorite in one of
the biggest English events, and erstwhile cash-bookmaker
“ Bob " Sutton netted £20,000, while Joe Thompson alone
laid £lO,OOO against. Oxenham’s money went on Syerla—
a fact which is causing Australian sports to anticipate
some interesting developments when the leviathan pen-
ciller has time to look round him “ down under.”
* * # *
Donald Wallace had a Carbine story of his own.
He was riding down to the course one day in a railway
carriage full of young fellows who looked like suburban
clerks. There had been much talk of Carbine’s “sore
toe,” and one of the youths was saying : “ He ain’t fit.
He can’t do it —Wallace himself ain’t got a cent on him.”
Others doubted. They thought “ Old Jack ” might come
out all right. “ Look here,” said the first, “ I lay any-
one 10 to Ihe don’t do it to-day. There you are —10 to
1.” Wallace, who joked rarely and with difficulty, said :
“ I’ll take that in pounds.” “No fear,” said the plunger,
“in shillin’s.” The bet was booked with great formality,
Wallace posing as “ Mr. Smith,” and when the race was
run and Carbine had won, Donald found himself con-
fronted by the youth of the railway carriage, who slipped
a half sovereign into his hand, saying : “ Here’s yer half
jim, Mr. Wallace. I put my last shirt button on Carbine
when I see you had confidence in him.” Donald grinned
meekly, but fobbed the half sov.

“Jab.”: Sydney fight-followers were again,
last week, treated to the spectacle of a pug. refusing to
go on with his contract because the “ gate ” didn’t reach
“ swelled head ” anticipations, and hundreds had to go
home disgusted. Similar happening occurred at same
place recently. Wonder how long those who keep the
game going will stand this ? Principals in latest instance
were Dawson and “ The Poet.” Latter was eager, - but
Dawson didn’t take to it. Cribb undertook to fill the gap
with an eight-rounds spar, but he can’t spar—fighting is
his game—so the show was a sorry one.
* * * •
“Nulla”: Aboriginal pugs, are rare birds.
“ Warrigal,” now fighting in Sydney novice-tourneys, is
as game as they make ’em, but not big enough. A good
’un in his day (Mungia Hippi) died suddenly at Wirra-
billa (N.S.W.) recently. He was born at Gundablown
Station (N.S.W.) nearly 50 years ago, and spread his life
over the Barwon, Collarendabri, Mogil-Mogil, Mungindi,
and Moree, droving and on sheep-stations. Mungia was
a tip-top sample—Oft. high and powerfully built, ap ideal
athlete to look at. None of his race and very few whites
ever licked him with the “ mauleys.” Mungia’s record
and experiences would form capital reading if they could
be got hold of.
• » • *
Writer saw Peter Jackson in town t’other day,
and had a chat with him anent pugs, and other things
Yankee. Peter isn’t gone on the country, and says few
of its first-flight fighters would have been considered top-
notchers here in the early part of last decade. Most
matches are “ arranged,” and unless a man will let himself
be “ used” ’Murkan managers don’t want him. The
“oulddart” element finds most favor with followers of
THE DUNLOP TYRE CO.'S TURN-OUT AT MELBOURNE ON tSrd MAY.
bruising struggles, and the more “ Irish " a pug. has in
his blood the more chances he gets—as instance Peter
Maher, who has been licked a dozen times, perhaps, and
is still allowed to hang on to the front rank, A colored
man has a hard row to hoe, no matter how circumspect
or inclined to knuckle under he may be. Talking of
that peculiar development known to the cognoscenti as
the “ cauliflower-car,” and brought about by frequent
bashes in the shape of swings (right and left) on the aural
organ, writer mentioned that it was strange the big black
escaped unscathed in this way. Peter smiled broadly
am! said somehow or other they couldn’t land on his head
anywhere, and only once did his face stop one, and that
on the forehead. “ The bingey was my weak point, they
said,” continued Peter, “ and most of them met disaster
trying to reach there.”

Pigeon-shooting match at Botany (Sydney) last
week between O. Massey (Yass), champion of N.S.W.,
and A. \\. Eales (Duekentield) for £5O a side, created a
lot of interest. Conditions, 100 birds each) 30yds. rise.
Kales won with 92 to 89. Eales was very deadly with the
first barrel throughout, and, though only a yenmgster
(comparatively), toed the mark like a veteran. All the
birds were good. Eales- killed 32 straight off without a
miss. Winner’s achievement is full of merit; he never
engaged in a big match before, hut has always been a
consistently brilliant performer from 32£vds. ' He went
into the contest brimful of confidence, anil didn’t weaken
onee. Previously Massey had won the N.S.W. supremacy
from a Dig field; beat “Ohud” Gorrick at Yass, then
settled Neeld, of Wyalong (“ Lockslev ”), grassing 96 out
of 100, and afterwards easily accounted for W. Best in a
“ double-rise” match with first-class birds. The styles of
Eales and Massey were widely different. Eales would go
to the mark, get a sight, sweep round the traps, and
cry “pull” firmly. Atasse.v held the barrel up above his
head, and, bringing it down to sight, would give the
signal without further move.
* • * .
In Sydney, last week, champion billiard-player
Fred Weiss gave S. Woods 900 in 1500 and had things all
his own way. He knocked up breaks of 223, 82, 74, 157,
and 55, besides several thirties and forties ; his average
(at close of play) for each lift-up of the cue was 36.73.
Woods’ performance fell far short of his reputation ; his
best was 34. At close of first night’s play scores stood :
Weiss (with an unfinished 74), 750 ; Woods, 1015. Second
evening Weiss again shaped brilliantly, compiling runs of
102, 82, 76, 63, 60, and 59. Average was 33. Weiss won
with 245 to spare. Woods wasn’t in it at any stage.

Thirteenth inter-’varsity eight-oar event on the
Parramatta (Sydney), Saturday, proved a soft snap for
Sydney, which won by 20 lengths irr hollow fashion. Only
other crew racing was Adelaide. Plague scared Melb. off
for the first time in the history of the race. In first
100yds. Adelaides met with a mishap which lost them a
few lengths, hut made no difference in the result. Form
offthe winners—though they had the game to themselves
all the way—was nothing to boast about. Of 13 races
rowed Sydney ’Varsity has 7 to its credit, Melb. 4, and
Adelaide 2. Sydney and Melb. have each been repre-
sented 11 times, and Adelaide 9.

The Sydney daily papers are fighting hard the re-
action that is setting in in favor of free speech and
in favor of the able and candid W. A. Holman as
a concomitant, and carefully suppress all favorable
mention of that politician. As an instance, the
incident at Miss Spence’s Effective-A oting meet-
ing of Wednesday, last week. The ballot-papers,
with the 13 names, were given out to the audience,
when a fat, important-looking personage arose and
protested against Holman’s name being allowed
to remain on the list, in view of his “disgraceful
anti-war utterances. The fat gentleman was,^ how-
ever, promptly put down with cries of “He s
worth 10 of you ! ” &c., and, when the poll was
taken, Holman was found to be at the top, beating
Barton, Reid, and the rest of the bunch, and
was, moreover, the only man elected on the “first
choice” with a “quota.” S.M. Herald reported
the meeting at length, simply mentioning that
Holman was one of the eleoted, and not mention-
ing Fatman. The Telegraph gave even more
space to the meeting, but altogether ignored the
election rather than report that Holman headed
the poll. The meeting, by the way, took place on
the evening of the day when the papers reported
the no-confidence vote at S. Wyalong against
Holman. At that meeting there were barely 180
present, of whom but 70 or 80 voted ; but nobody
cognisant of who represents the Telegraph and
E. News at Wyalong was surprised at the sort of
report which appearod in those papers. Another
gross instance of journalistic suppression was the
manner in which the S.M. Herald (whose reporter
seems to have been the only pressman present)
burked Gov. Beauchamp’s remarks concerning
The Bulletin at last meeting of Sydney Univer-
sity Debating Society.

SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not he
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears Soap in the foim of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it with him .on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
“ matchless for the complexion.”

Samuel Beecher, cousin of Henry Ward
Beecher, has had three wives in the past year,
His first wife died inside that period, after 50
years of matrimony. He married another, who
died in a few weeks. Being “unable to endure
the loneliness,” he has taken a third.
Blunders at peace-manoeuvres are over-
looked in the British army, and carelessness in
the performance of field duties treated as a venial
offence, whereas a German officer, who, owing to
a social entertainment, was a little late at
autumn manoeuvres, was at once removed from his
command, and afterwards placed on the retired
list.

February, says London Times. After that, who
can wonder the Boers were—-beaten.

When Roberts began his move the French
colonel Villebois Mareuil, (since killed) sent word
to Cronje that he was in danger of being
surrounded, and the reply of the Boer commander
was, “Tell the foreigner that whilst he was still

Sydney D.T.’s Melbourne correspondent:
Chairman Fitzgibbon, and the majority of those con-
nected with the Melb. Metrop. Board of Works, are loyal
to the verge of ridiculousness, as witness the chairman s
cables of congratulation in the name of Victoria. Strange
to say, no mention has been made of any replies. Perhaps
the Queen has mislaid hers, if it ever reached her.
John King Lethbridge, who died at St.
Mary’s (N.S.W.) recently, was a son of the
youngest daughter of Capt. Philip Gidley Ling,
second Governor of N.S.W. He was born in
Nepean district in ’32, and held the positions of
coroner and returning officer in the Nepean for
almost 40 years. Amongst his coronary duties
were the inquests in connection with Butler s
victims.
Writes Mr. Marshall Lyle :
I see it again printed that I agitated for the release of
the notorious negro convict, W. King, now awaiting trial.
I never did anything of the sort for reasons I cannot state
now, but maybe able to explain later on. I have, how-
ever, agitated a good deal tor the detention of dangerous
criminals, which is a very different thing to releasing
them When I read these oft-repeated misstatements I
feel something like Herbert Spencer when people refer to
him as “ a distinguished Socialist.’
N.S.W. Govt, has cabled Chamberlain
asking him to expedite the Pacific cable. "I he
sooner the better. Two cables are better than
one. _ an d perhaps when the news comes
through America 6ur cables will no longer
be composed exclusively by a duke and a
footman. Even a cowboy’s cable would be an
agreeable change. Between the dukes yarn and
the cowboy’s yarn, Australia would have a chance
of getting at the truth occasionally without wait-
ing for the mail.
“Banjo ” Paterson and Frank Wilkinson
are the only first-rank Australian war correspon-
dents who bid fair to last out the campaign.
Lambie and Spooner are dead, and MacDonald
and Reay have been invalided home. In the case
of Lambie, no insurance was effected before he
left, and the Age, Telegraph and Advertiser
had to make up £lOOO out of their own pockets.
The fund raised by his comrades to perpetuate his
memory amounts to £2lO, most of the sum having
been subscribed in Melbourne.

loses”one of its straightest sportsmen. There was
no “ waiting for a slice of the sweep about him.
A few years ago, an employe at tjie Australia
Hotel drew one of his horses in a big consulta-
tion,” and approached Mr. Wilson with an oner of
so much to nothing. “ No,” said the horse-owner,
“I don’t do that sort of thing. 11l try to win,

That rev. Jingo, the somewhat chamele-
onic George Walters, got a surprise the other night
in his own church when he had Miss Spence, of
effective-voting fame, to preach for him in the big
Oddfellows’ Temple to a crowded audience. though
Mr Walters had denounced War—war m the
&bsU<*ot-u. the past, he has failed to denounce
tins particular war, his judgment in the matter
having possibly been disturbed by the overwhelm-
ing Jingo howl around him. .Miss Spence had
been so busy with her own particular fad that no
one had managed to gather whether she knew
there was a war in progress or not; and anyhow
her subject, “ Soul and Body,” was a pretty safe-
looking and far-away kind of theme. But the
gallant old lady—having somehow worked round
to the power of mere words for evil—instanced
the recently much bandied-about Suzerainty.
“For that word,” she said, “our nation has
dipped its hands in blood; is embarked on a
costly and cruel war over matters that might
easily and satisfactorily have been settled by arbi-
tration ! ” Amidst the storm of applause which
this observation evoked Miss Spence smiled
blandlv all round while the flabbergasted Walters
remonstrated feebly with, “ Hush !—hush .—this
is a church /” It was mighty convenient, just
then, to remember that the hall m which dances
are held to rake in the shekels is also by way of
being a “ sacred edifice.”

’ The war sentiment of S’Africa is every-
where in an inextricable tangle. Previous to the
Duke of Norfolk’s arrival as an Imperial Yeoman
at Capetown, a meeting of local Catholics was held
to arrange a “ hearty welcome ” to him as head of
the English R.C.’s. The Boers, by their desecra-
tion of the R.C. altars at Dundee, had done their
best to incline the hearts of the Duke’s co-religion-
ists towards him, nevertheless Dr. Colgan, a
leading priest, made a strong pro-Boer protest
against any honoring of the noble amateur soldier,
and finally he (Colgan) left the hall with twenty
or more sympathisers.
That good Democrat, Sangster, Vic.
M.L.A. for Port Melbourne, met with a brutish
reception t’other night from the Jingoes. Al-
though Sangster always opposed the sending of
Australian contingents.to S’Africa, he neverthe-
less wanted, at the meeting, to express his glad-
ness that Mafeking had been relieved. He was
howled down, however, and it was proposed that
he be fired out and jumped upon as a pro-Boer.
But the audience eagerly swallowed Purves
fustian, and there was apparently nobody present
to remind that orator that it isn’t so long since he
went in for “ cutting the painter ’ that holds our
cockle-shell to the old barge on the other side of
the globe 1

“Gunner” on the Mafeking business
All this vaporing about Mafeking and Baden-Powell
makes me tired. I don’t dispute B.P. s merit; but his
exploit isn’t for a moment to be compared with many
other defences of isolated posts by small scratch-garnsons.
Take the case of the Lucknow Residency in 57. the
garrison consisted only of a few miscellaneous
civil and military, with some detachments of faithful
sepovs, and a little contingent of European invalids. Ihe
besiegers, who were able to approach within a furlong,
under cover of buildings and walled gardens, had plenty
of trained gunners, besides sappers and miners,
who pushed forward the attack underground as well.
The first relieving force under Havelock and Outram,
after fighting its way through the pity of Lucknow, was
shut up with the original garrison m the Residency, and
would have perished therewith if it had not been in turn
relieved by Sir Colin Campbell (with whom was Roberts,
who in “ 41 Years in India,” has described the many days
of tremendous fighting necessary to force a way to the
Residency). It would be interesting, by-the-way, to know
what “ Bobs’ ” really thinks of the Mafeking business and
Baden-Powell’s somewhat patronising comparison between
the march of the relieving force and that of Roberts to
Kandahar.

The self-contained Kitchener lately eulo-
gised the deceased war-correspondent, G. W,
Steevens, in most flattering terms. Probably a
return for the Kitchener laudations in Steevens’
account of the Khartoum campaign—-wherein the
Sirdar is exalted almost to the heavens. Kitchener
won’t eulogise Winston Churchill overmuch should
the latter cut his stick to some celestial kopje.
The great ambition of a certain kind of
(mostly unmarried) woman is to be tied up of
otherwise handled and pawed by a man, and ir
that can’t be arranged the next best thing is to
say she was so handled or pawed or tied up. As
an instance, a Maoriland family came home re-
cently in the early morning to find the girl who
had been left in charge with a handkerchief fas-
tened over her mouth, and herself secured to the
bed-post with her hands behind her. She alleged
that a man had done it, but on cross-examination
she admitted that by vast ingenuity she had con-
trived a way of apparently fastening her own
hands behind her back, and had got up the little
show herself. How long she had been there it was
impossible to say, but as she didn’t know exactly
when the family would return it seems quite pro-
bable that she had been standing wearily on
her two feet for hours, with a gag in her
mouth and her shoulders aching, and her
body moored to a cold bedstead, waiting to be
discovered in that romantic position. Marriage is
a desirable state because it consists in being
owned by a man. And if the romantic maiden
can’t be permanently owned by a man, it is at
least something to go on with if she can so far
attract the attention of one as to lead him to tie
her to a bedstead. It shows that she isn’t utterly
forgotten and overlooked by the other sex. It
suggests that, though he was in too great a hurry
to propose marriage in the usual way, yet he
wanted to know where to find her again when he
came back with more leisure on his hands and
honorable intentions in his eye. It
jealousy ; he fastened her to the bedstead to pre-
vent her going out and showing her charms to
other admirers. Also, it seems to include a grati-
fying sense of proprietorship or wish for proprie-
torship, and a desire to gaze on her face
at his leisure and drink in her loveli-
ness. At all events, whatever fee'ing it
expresses, it does not express indifference. No
man ever broke into a strange house and tied a
piece of brown paper to the bedstead, for the suffi-
cient reason that man is quite indifferent to brown
paper. He might wander past it every day for
years and never once know it was there. And
that is probably the whole reason and explanation
why a healthy young woman goes to great pains
to rope herself to a cold iron bed on a cold night,
and gets cramps and frozen feet waiting to be
found there with her undigested handkerchief in
her mouth.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian eems.*
