At Poverty Point.
“ The Ladder of Life ” introduces three Cockney Hee-
landers at the storming of Dargai Heights. Seemingly
there are cockneys in kilts. A soger from South Africa,
interviewed by a Melb. Argus man last week, said, “ I
ain’t a mister ; I’m a blooming Tommy. Corporal Fuller,
of the 2nd Battalion Gordon Highlanders, -that’s who I
am. After fighting both at Chitral and Dargai, I sailed
from India with the first- battalion of the regiment, and
got up to the front in time for Elands Laagte.” The
braw Fuller incidentally mentioned “ a blessed little
bugler ” who was with the Australians at Enslin. and who
used to sing a certain song, whereupon a bystander
remarked that the blessed bugler must have been his
son. He identified bis offspring by the ditty in question.
“ I dessay,” said Corporal F. “ Well, next time you see
your son tell him to learn a new song. I got sick of
'earing it every night.”
The Dailv Mail report of Melba’s divorce supplies
sundry anecdotes and fictions which speak well for the
business ability of the ex-Melbournienne. She was only
19 when she first sang in public, and at 21 (! 1!) she left
Australia —she says, Her first appearance in grand opera
' happened when she was 23, ye gods ! One day when she
had been practising her part at her hotel in New York
she was approached by a strange lady, who said, “ Ma-
dame, I think you would be touched to hear what my
little boy said just how. He is lying in bed, getting over
an illness, and when you began to sing he lifted his tiny
forefinger and whispered, “ Hist, mummy ! Birdie ! ’’
Melba tells a very truthful-looking yarn of her interview
with the King and Queen of Sweden at the palace in
Stockholm The King had a decoration for her, and
wanted a pin to fasten the ribbon to her dress. Melba
promptly offered a pin, but his Majesty hesitated to take
it lest it might “ mar their friendship.” Then a happy
thought occurred to his royal mind. He took the pin,
saying, “ I will break the evil spell with a kiss, and we
will be friends always.” What chances these kings do
get 1
“M.E.”: Went to “Oliver Twist,” and returned a
sadder man. I want to be made wiser, and therefore I
ask, why, in the name of all that is hideous and beastly,
produce such a horror ? Why pander to the public’s love
of blood—the public, whose blood-lust has of late been so
glutted? How a woman like Nance O'Neil, who aspires
to the classic roles, can appear, and apparently revel in,
such a nightmare passes my understanding. The play is
one blot of blood. We all know what it is to buy a pot of
Aspinall’s enamel—how we simply must enamel every
available subject in the house. Nance O’Neil and co. have
evidently been investing in a ton or so of red paint, and
once they begin they paint the town red. All the people in
“ Oliver ” had a daub of red about them—and as for
Nancy 1 Talk of realism—this was realism with a venge-
ance. And if you have such filthy realism on the stage,
why not be realistic right through ? Bill Sikes blows out
the candle, and kicks Nancy’s face out of all human
resemblance, in the dark. Then why, in the name of
blood, after Nancy has dragged herself round the room in
the half-light, and thoroughly thrilled the horror-loving
public, why should the lights suddenly flare and reveal
the face, one inass of blood, or red paint, the eyes staring,
and that one bloody hand sticking out from the sheet?
Why couldn’t Nancy die decently in the next room ? ’Tie
a pity Nance doesn’t more often wear dark hair. It suits
her, and she was very suggestive of Maud Jeffries. And,
what a shame that that voice of hers should be used for
London-slum vernacular 1
“ Romeo ” Bellew is not aspiring to become a mining
expert. It is said that he is to manage for Musgrove ana
Co., at Melb. Princess.
A barber, in Auckland (M.L.), said to G. P. Oarey,
“ I’m going to see a famous new play, the ‘ Second Mrs.
Langtry.’ ”
“ Skull ” : The night after their arrival in Melb., a few
of the Australians invalided home from S’Africa appeared
at the Bijou, crowded in alongside the orchestra, in full
view of the whole house, and they competed for the
admiration of the audience, even more eagerly than the
people on the stage. The management drew attention to
them, although they had already paraded their sore toes
with immense effect ; the various performers drew
attention to them; after which they drew attention to
themselves, a civilian in their company rising up between
the turns with a speech about “ Heroes from the war,
ladies and gentlemen,” and “ Brave fellows who have bled
for their country,” but the storm of applause cut him off.
It was a very pretty and modest exhibition. The returned-
hero theatre-party is a most tasteful show.
“ Malle ” : Alleged facts lately given of dramatic trial of
Walter Montgomery, on a charge of murdering one W.
Shakespeare, are somewhat warped. The trial was in
Melbourne, not Sydney, and Dailey was Judge, not coun-
sel, and his mimicry of the mannerisms of a prominent
Yic. jurist convulsed the party. When Montgomery was
found guilty, and was asked if he had anything to say why
sentence should not be passed, he replied, “Nothing, ex-
cept that you are a set of hideous wretches.” Then
Dailey assumed great solemnity, and, before passing
sentence, said that no fulsome flattery on the part of the
prisoner should weigh with him.
Beauteous Mrs. Maesmore Morris is playing a lady Jour-
nalist in “ The Man of Forty,” at George Alexander’s
theatre. Her experience in Melbourne ( and Sydney)
must have givpn the much-paragraphed blonde a fair in-
sight into penwoman methods, anyhow. One notice
says : “ Mrs. Maesmore Morris looked smart and self-
reliant as the journalist, and gave her lines with point
and clearness.”
A pleasing incident happened at the opening of the
re-chriStened Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, on Saturday
night, the 19th, the night on which Melbourne went mad
about Mafeking. There was an atmosphere of used
whisky in the house, and the audience was rabid in its
patriotism. It signalised the occasion by singing many
Jingo songs and much National Anthem. When Pro-
vidence was being incited to save the Queen, a gaudy
soldier at the back of the balustrade, seizing his oppor-
tunity, leaned forward, and banged a stranger heavily on
the head. “ Why don’ you shtan’ up when we shing
‘ Gor sha Queen ’?” he demanded. “I am standing, you
idiot 1 ” answered the victim. He was a dwarf, with a
man’s body and infantile legs.
Re Daudet’s “ Sapho,” for producing which, on N.Y.
stage, our bold buxom little friend Olga Nethersole has
got into hot water, the author’s son, Leon Daudet,
quaintly says “Among literary people it is looked upon
as milk and water, the Sandford and Merton of French
novels. There is not a bourgeois in France who would
object to his wife or his son reading it; I don’t say his
daughter.” Milk and water that is confessed unfit for the
daughter of a house must be queer stuff. Leon must
mean rum-and-milk.
The statement that Mrs. Patrick Campbell was married
at the age of 17 reminds one that every fashionable actress
who admits to having been married for some years was a
girl of 17 when she tripped lightly to the altar.
Hermann Vezin, who married the old-time Australian
actress, Mrs. Charles Young, celebrated his stage jubilee
t’other day with a big benefit in London. Vezin at his
prime was a great, but always a cold actor, noted for
incisive elocution and dramatic intelligence rather than
force, and a couple of inches too short for heroic parts.
“ Hermes ” : “ Magda ” may not be a great play, but it
is certainly a remarkable one. And the most remarkable
bits are not the ones which have been most spoken of.
F'rinstance, the much-quoted passage where Nance de-
claims that we must sin to rise superior to our sins is very
vague and general after $ll, but the attention of John
Norton, as public censor of morals, is drawn to that win-
some, definite incitement to impropriety in the passage
where Nance asks her charming and conventional sister
if she never took her lover out into the glorious solitude,
and lay upon h'; breast, and forget everythin?. Now, an
engaged Miss is not supposed to lie upon her fiance’s
chest. She may pillow her golden head upon it, or let
her tears trickle over it, or hide her face in it, or use it
occasionally as a lean-to, but to subside upon it entirely
is taking an undue liberty.
Edna May, the original Belle of Xew York, was, at
latest dates, seeking a divorce from her husband, Frederick
Titus. One witness for petitioner testified that Titus and
the co-respondent (Miss Ward) were wont to hold one
another’s hands “ in a pointed manner,” and to “ sleep
with their heads on each other’s shoulders in the cars.”
Mrs. Bernard Beere was lately married again to a Mr.
Olivier, but the event “ will not interfere with her
acceptance of professional engagements,” she says.
J.S.: Re my former remarks about the dreary length
of present-day dramatic presentations : In Act I. of
“The Tyranny of Tears,” 35 minutes are spent merely
in explaining that Parbury is a scribbler with a female
secretary, and a wife who cries for her own way ; and the
other three acts in proportion. “T. ofT.” would be a
good 25 or 30min. curtain-raiser if a lawn-mower were
run over it, the 15min. waits cut out, and some 2.40-gait
actors engaged under a penalty of £1 per minute over-
time. The play is in itself good enough. So is Walker’s
whisky ; but if you put a bottle of said whisky into a cask
of water you'd have some -trouble in getting mellow on
the mixture. Look on the thing as a story, and take,
say, one of Dorrington’s short yarns and spread it over
four pp. of Bulletin'. Even then it wouldn’t take more
than a couple of hours to read, and wouldn’t cost two or
three shillings either for the perusal.
Argus critique of “ Pirates of Penzance ” Melbourne
revival—“ Mr. Gilbert is a passable-enough librettist.”
Why this jealousy ? Has one of the Argus young men
written an opera?
“ Pirates of Penzance ” humor is little understood now-
adays, seemingly. Men who call themselves intelligent
can see no joke in the idea of a triumphant lawless crew
collapsing suddenly when called upon in the Queen’s
name to lay down their arms. Also, the famous poster-
posturing of the Pirate King is regarded by the average
awful person as being “ absurdly overdone.” Perhaps it
has already lost its original meaning (“ P. of P." is 20 years
old) for the younger playgoers in London, where onetime
every small-boy’s ambition was to apply paint to a picture
—printed, price one penny—of the Pirate King, with one
foot on a barrel and a black flag over one shoulder,
exactly as represented in Gilbert’s opera. Anyway, those
absurd old “ penny plain, twopence colored ’’ prints have
not been seen in Australian shop-windows since Lord-
knows-when.
The Acetylene Miners’ Lamps, introduced by
the Acetylene Gas Co. of Australia, Ltd., have proved a
great success and are now largely used in the mines
throughout the colony. The company has lately been
rushed with orders for lighting up gold-mining dredges
owing to the many advantages offered by the light for
that purpose. It would be well for mine and dredge
owners to ascertain full information as to cost, etc., from
the Head Office of the Company, 30 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
The N.D.L. Atlantic liner Kaiser Wilhelm
der Grosse has topped the world’s marine speed-
record with an average, for 22 runs across the
Atlantic, of a shade under 22 knots (25| miles) per
hour. The fastest run of the 22 was (outwards)
6d. 17h. 27m.; and the biggest day’s run of the 22
trips was 569 miles.
What should be done in the vexed matter
of Sydney tram-fares. Same fare—cash or tickets;
but don’t worry the unhappy conductors to give
change for cash fares. If a man wants change,
make him buy tickets.
A letter received in N.S.W from Bloem-
fontein states that there are nearly 3000 cases of
enteric fever in the town, and that so many are
dying that they have to be buried in trenches,
instead of ordinary graves. The “ penny-plain ”
side of war is as deadly as the “twopenny-
coloured.”
Holman’s latest meeting at Wyalong, at
which the Jingoes prevailed in a way, means little
locally, in that the result was partly brought
about by the most outrageous terrorism, of a kind
that if used at an election would put a man in
gaol—and will; if used. Men may vote publicly
to suit their employers, but the ballot-box is
another thing.

To that large class which shaves itself,
and usually does the work badly, the Abbot Razor
is a heaven-sent implement. No Razor ever in-
vented will save some people from cutting them-
selves in 16 places, but a weapon made of the
finest steel, with an edge that is as sharp as any-
thing in this world can be, at least reduces this
danger to a minimum. And the Abbot Razor,
sold by Messrs. Mammatt and Sons of 93 York-
street, Sydney, is the weapon in question.*
N. Z. Loan and M. A. Go. Ltd. report for
week ended June 2nd that the catalogue of marsupial
skins was a record one. The co. topped the market with
42id. for grey kangaroos, 66d. for reds, 33£d. for
wallaroos, and 22d. for extra heavy scrub wallabies.
’Possum skins are not up to standard this winter, but the
co. got there with 12s. 3d. per dozen for greys, and 11s.
3d. for reds. As indicated in last week’s reporl, hides
continue to slump—again from Jd. to id. per lb. London
reports rather large decline, which will probably cause a
further decline here. Some quotations : Q’land factory
hides, heavy 6Jd. to s£d., medium sd. to 6Jd, light sd.
to 5Jd. Choice butcher, heavy 4id. to 4Jd, medium 4Jd.
to 4|d., light 4d. to4£d; sloppy and indifferently cured
Jd. below these values. Tallow still gravitates down-
wards. At last week’s London sale only 10 casks sold out
of 600 offered. Prospects indifferent. Owing to certain
restrictions imposed on Australian produce in some
foreign ports, buyers of horns, bones, &c., are not
enthusiastic. Glue-pieces and sinews; though not much
sought after, have not slid, down —still the tendency is
not upward. Wheat is far from uproarious ; but Friday's
demand for sheepskins was keen. Bright, sound-pelted,
half-growths showed a slight jump, but as a whole
the market was only just firm. Skins: Full-wool
merinos, 7Jd. to 7Jd., and occasionally Bd. ; half-growths
s|d. to 6f. ; shorts and pelts, 4d. to 4|d. ; fine cross-breds,
fnll-wool, 4Jd. to sld. ; coarse cross-breds, 4d. to 4}d.
Heavy, earthy, or otherwise inferior skins, from id. to Jd.
per lb. less.
The disinfecting brigade went fishing lately
in Sydney’s Beautiful Harbor to see if it wanted
cleaning as badly as some other ihe
harvest of the sea was nine pigs, 168 dogs, 27 pups,
98 goats, 87 fowls, 640 rats, one monkey, and 27
pieces of meat, all very extra-dead. And yet,
among all the beautiful poems about the treasures
of the sea, and the sheen of the coral caves, and
the pearls that are down below, there isn t one
single allusion to the drowned goat or the 168
dogs. Poetry is a beautiful but inaccurate su b -
stance cut up into short lengths that try to sound
alike at the far end.
Robson, M.L.A. for Geraldton, W.A.,
being new to Parliamentary life, had the nerve to
tell a big meeting the other day that the greatest
blot on W.A. was its corrupt Govt. Premier
Forrest threatens vengeance, but, so far, Robson
has neither withdrawn nor apologised. Briefly,
Robson drew attention to the presence in the
Assembly of several men, all Forrest supporters,
who had gone through the court and who had no
visible means of support, either before or since.
As for specific charges, Robson avers that he has
a shipload of them, and will fire them into the
Govt, on an early date.

Women and Marriage.
HINTS FOR CHOOSING A WIFE.
It is not always the prettiest women that
are the most popular with the sterner sex.
You will often find that one of the plainest
girls, if she be good-natured, light-hearted,
and witty, will be most sought after by tha
men. When it comes to marriage, a careful
man takes pains that the woman of his
choice is healthy and robust; he considers
it a duty he owes to himself and posterity,
that his companion in life should be as
nearly perfect, from a physical standpoint,
as possible. Many a man’s life has been
ruined by marriage with a frail and delicate
woman. It is these sort of matches that
find marriage a failure. Not that the
woman is so much to blame, as in many
cases she does not know of a remedy for her
ills. A woman who is run-down, nervous or
irritable, is not a helpmeet to a man in any
way. If she suffers in this way, sooner or
later she will be taken with fainting-spells,
dizziness, and backaches, aebompanied by a
heavy, crowded feeling in the abdomen.
There is no need to suffer with any of these
ailments when Bile Beans are close at hand.
They act gently (as all medicine for women
should), and are, therefore, to be recom-
mended. Bile Beans put the liver in a per-
fect condition and prevent the ducts con-
necting the various digestive organs from
becoming stopped up. In this way they
prevent biliousness. When a cure, however,
is required, they cause the liver to secrete
increased quantities of this fluid, and thus
flush the whole digestive system and carry
off all waste matter from the system, clear-
ing off at the same time all surplus bile.
Once tried (they are only per large
box), they will be found beneficial, and their
use continued the system will be toned
up, the appetite return, the circulation
improve, and general health restored. A
healthy woman is always interesting; she
is happy, and her spirits are contagious.
Good health and good nature are insepar-
able ; secure the first by using Bile Beans
for Biliousness and the latter will take care
of itself.
The Shepherd.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Adam Muir was an unfortunate. Life had mis-
used him—after he had misused life ; but he did
not dwell on that point whereas he never ceased
to blaspheme Life for the ironical manner in which
she had returned evil for evil. Poverty, hard-
living, and coarse food were bad enough, but tie
was inured to these, and yet he smouldered with a
sullen hate against the vast fates that had played
such scurvy tricks with Adam Muir, Gentleman.
Muir was born to riches and reared in luxury.
He could never get away from the conviction that
wealth and pleasure were his natural rights, as
toil, and hunger, and suffering were the ordained
inheritance and belongings of the children of the
poor. He had the contempt of a rich man s son
for arithmetic, and never counted the cost. Adam
awoke one morning to find himself a bankrupt; it
was curieus, but not very disturbing—he was a
gentleman, lie had always been rich, money came
to him by some natural process well understood by
the good God. There was a temporary stoppage
or a trifling miscarriage. All would come right.
Presently Adam Muir was friendless, homeless,
and hungry ,; then he bestirred himself, and ad-
dressed Providence somewhat after this fashion :
“ Come, I say, this is carrying the thing too
far, don’t you know ! ”
Expostulations were useless, and here we have
Adam Muir, at 45, a “hatter” in a bark-hut in
desolate Australia. Many of his boon companions
had gone to the dogs meanwhile Adam went to
the sneep. A shepherd’s calling is a most suitable
one for a man with a grievance; it affords such
spacious opportunities of revolving the situation.
A shepherd can be on his back by the hour, with
all his choice sorrows gathered about him, revelling
luxuriously in his distresses. The sheep, too, is a
very fit companion for such a man ; it is a fatuous
brute with a morbid outlook, and jet with a cer-
tain power of impressing its individuality on its
keeper. The old shepherd is always something of
a sheep. Lambs have a healthier influence, but
there came a time when Adam Muir positively re-
fused to have anything to do with lambs. They
disturbed his ideas with their imbecile frisking
and false, frivolous views of life.
“Lambs are so damn unsympathetic,” said
Adam. He would rather go hungry than shep-
herd them.
The few people with whom he came in contact
at Waddy had always considered the shepherd a
bit queer; his antipathy to lambs was gratefully
accepted as another development of an amusing
eccentricity. Muir shepherded flocks on the
great sweep of common land to the north and
west of the township, sometimes tending the
sheep from one sale-day to another only—some-
times keeping a mob on the grass for several
weeks. His hut was just within the bush-line,
and his nearest neighbor was German Jim, a
veteran fossickor living a quarter of a mile deeper
in the bush and near the creek, where he was
always busy over his “tom” and cradle. But
Adam made no friends ; he maintained an aris-
tocratic reserve, arguing that it would not be well
to be mixed up with the tag-rag and bob-tail when
Fate wearied of the sport and restored him to the
privileges that were his by some process of Divine
right.
After 17 years of poverty the shepherd clung to
the belief that his present condition in a manner
represented a reversal of a natural law. He felt
like a man barred from his lawful heritage by
fraud, and schemed, and planned, and plotted to
get back to halls of light and luxurious living.
His plots were all pantomimes, gorgeous hut
impossible.
It was German Jim who suggested murder as
an easy accessible means to the end.
The fossicker had a great reputation as a miser
in Waddy. It is hard to escape such a reputation
in a bush township if you are poor enough and
industrious enough. One day Adam came
suddenly and unexpectedly on the German as the
latter was panning off the sand from his slides in
the “tom,” which was full of water. The fossicker
swore a guttural oath or two, and threw a shovel-
ful of tailings into the dish. The action was
secret, suspicious—it hinted that he was working
rich dirt and dreaded discovery. Adam’s
imagination took fire at once. He was quite
ignorant of mining, and preposterous visions of
hoarded gold flashed upon him ; but he was cun-
ning, and pretended to have noticed nothing.
“ Doing pretty well, Jimmy ? ” he asked.
“ Go to der teffel ? ” answered Jimmy sullenly.
For a week Adam Muir lay on his back, dream-
ing dreams, and arranging the details of a capital
crime, and every hour the golden store of the
miserable old man in the creek grew and spread
till it rivalled the mines of Solomon in its glory.
He prowled about the German’s hut after dark, and
one night, peering through a crack in the wall,
saw Jimmy hurriedly and anxiously burying some-
thing in a hole at the foot of his bunk. Adam
stole away and next day, in his dreams, he spent
the fossicker’s savings in Sybaritish living.
At noon, impelled by curiosity, he stole to
German Jim’s cradle whilst Jim was at crib, and
was pawing about in the ripples when the fossicker
descended upon him in a fury, and almost beat the
life out of him with a pick-handle. Muir went
back to his flock, and comforted himself with
reverie. He did not complain of his bruises ; the
pain he endured did not even make him angry ; it
gave a new zest to tfib devilishly clever murder he
was perfecting. That was all. The thought of
killing old Jimmy had never given him any com-
punction whatever: he relished it now. What
other object could there be in showering riches on
a man like German Jim? Why else should he
have gathered and hoarded them? Why else
should he have been created at all ?
The shepherd feared nothing, ho thought of the
law as something hazy and a-far off, but his mur-
der was to be a masterpiece of crime. He dwelt
lovingly on the details of it, and, looking up,
found the large, incurious eyes of an old ewe fixed
upon him. A revulsion of feeling swept over him,
he trembled and stared into its dull eyes; then
sprang to his feet, and chased her away with stone
and blasphemy. . . .
“The cursed, prying, peering, inquisitive
sheep ! ” he said
After that he was careful not to excite the sus-
picion of the brutes.
One pleasant moonlight night, German Joe,
answering an imperious knock at his door, found
Adam Muir standing there with his old single-
barrelled gun under his arm.
“I’ve called to see if you’ll oome rabbitting
with me, Jim,” said Adam.
There was no light in the hut saving that thrown
by a handful of fire in the big chimney, but it was
strong enough to show Muir that the fossicker’s
face was drawn and grey, and his eyes distended
with' a horrible fear.
“ What! ”he said, surprisedly—“ you expected
it ? ”
He raised the gun instantly,and fired. Jim
remained for a moment erect in the attitude of a
man thinking, then fell suddenly, heavily, upon
his face.
Muir closed the door, placed his gun in a comer,
and went carefully to work to unearth the trea-
sure. He searched assiduously for two hours;
dug up the floor, burrowed everywhere in the
walls, the roof, the chimney, the bunk, and the
pillows. Not an inch of space was left unsearched,
and at the end of the two hours he had gained
German Jim’s treasure for his pains—a chamois
leather bag containing a pinch of very fine gold,
less than an ounce, also a greasy pound note, a
half-crown, and seven coppers—found in the hay
mattress.
The shepherd returned to his own hut, quite
cool, and with a clear mind. Evidently the fos-
sicker had hidden his wealth somewhere outside ;
he would wait till the body was removed and
curiosity was abated, and then he would search
again, and would find the treasure as a matter of
course. *
He went out with his sheep in the morning as
usual, but when he returned to his hut at twelve
to boil his billy he was followed by a policeman
and two miners from Waddy. The policeman
snipped handcuffs upon his wrists, and said :
“ I arrest you for the murder of Herman Seigel,
alias German Jim.”
Adam Muir was amazed. “It must be the
sheep,” he murmured. He was thinking of the
old ewe that spied upon him.
The policeman took a small piece of paper from
his pocket, and smoothed it out carefully on the
table. Then he seized Adam’s hat, turned down
the leather lining, and pulled out the long strip
of folded paper Muir had inserted to make the
hat fit. The policeman straightened this beside
the other, and the prisoner recognised the small
greasy piece burnt about the edges as the scrap
e used to wad his gun. There was no other
paper handy at the time, and he tore this from
the padding in his hat.
The officer compared the two. “It fits, you
see,” he said.
EDWARD DYSON
An Australian Woman Journalist in
America.
Dear Bulletin. —Miss Darchy, from the staff of Sydney
Daily Telegraph, has been lecturing in San Francisco on
matters pertaining to Australia and the Australians.
Unfortunately California is too absolutely self-centred to
take a marked interest in anything that does not redound
to its own glory. Especially are affairs pertaining to
Britain and its colonies at a discount just now, conse-
quently Miss Darchy’s excellent lectures, illustrated by
admirable views, only attracted those who had a personal
interest in Australia. But, on the other hand, the lec-
turess has been extensively and flatteringly interviewed
in the leading papers. The press of S.F. is always on the
look-out for novelties, more especially novelties of the
feminine gender ; and the opportunity of inspecting a
lady journalist fresh from another continent, and of
gathering her impressions of female journalism as under-
stood in America, was not to bo lost.
Poor Miss Darchy’s first impressions were unenviable.
She caine from a land where the female journalist is ex-
pected to be a lady, or, at any rate, to “ behave as sich.”
She arrived in a land where the female journalist is not
expected to be a woman ; where she is supposed to throw
aside refinement and delicacy ; to subordinate truth to
fakeism ; to tear aside the veil which shrouds privacy ; to
probe hearts, analyse motives, undermine reputations,
wring confessions from criminals, flirt with murderers,
and generally comport herself like a wolf in sheep’s cloth-
ing for the benefit of a morbid public. That she does not
invariably do these things is greatly to her credit and dis-
advantage, the successful journalist being she who casts
aside conscience. Small wonder if Miss Susie Darchy
gave a little shiver of dismay when the status of her pro-
fession in America first dawned upon her. And the shiver
was noted and commented on. But the lady is wise in
her generation ; she contented herself with pronouncing
her American sister “ wonderful,” which is an agreeable
adjective to be variously interpreted. So the American
sister accepted her graciously, patted her on the back,
and expressed a patronising surprise at the narrow groove
in which Australia’s women run. But she was kindly en-
couraging, and Miss Darchy and her views are now tour-
ing the State, aided by divers complimentary press-
notices.
To what lengths female journalism will go in this land
has just been exemplified in a manner which will set
scandal-mongers’ tongues wagging for many a month to
come. One of the fraternity has distinguished herself by
a malicious piece of literature of which even “Ouida”
and “Marie Corelli” might be proud, and which no man
would have dared to pen. The sketch appears in the
Smart Set, a N.Y. publication which aims at showing-up
social foibles ; the writer, who disdains nom de guerre, is
Miriam Michelson, a notable San Francisco presswoman ;
the subject, Alice Rix, formerly the clever, flashy “ star ”
writer on the Examiner, but now posing sedately in re-
putable Philadelphia. Now Alice Rix’s Californian
career held divers matrimonial incidents which do not
count for much in this state of multiform marriage, but
which certainly w'ould not contribute to her social success
in proper Philadelphia. To be sure, Miss Michelson does
not say she is writing down the Rix ; certainly not ; she
calls this “ new woman ” and her son, Malda and Percy,
with varying surnames ; but here her little fiction ends.
Every objectionable incident in the unfortunate victim’s
career is carefully annotated with suitable comments ;
every foible, every flaw, every evil motive is held up to
contempt; all the steps in her matrimonial path indi-
cated with unblushing veracity, every insult that an un-
bridled woman’s pen can command is heaped upon
mother and son. The portrait is a pitiless photograph,
carefully touched up so as to show all the wrinkles and
cosmetics ; the likeness is so brutally true that none can
mistake it; yet with fiendish ingenuity the painter has
omitted to give the lights and shades, the subtleties of
emotion, which would have lent it a saving grace. It is
at once the cruelest and the most disgraceful “ human
document” ever traced by a female pen, yet it is but the
logical outcome of the training which America gives its
women journalists.
If privacy is to be ignored, if the microscope and the
scalpel are to come into play, if it be a woman’s business
to discover other people’s business and display it to a
curious world, there is evidently no reason why she
should not vivisect and analyse such of her friends and
acquaintance as come under her ban. Only, the results
may be a little too far-reaching to be pleasant. S.F. is a
city of glass-houses where stone-throwing is a dangerous
occupation. Alice Rix is not the only female journalist
who has been down (picer-street; and if the public is to
be flooded by reminiscences from kindly friends under
guise of fiction we shall see the old S.F. days of the duel,
and the shooting that is no duel, revived. Only this time
the antagonists will all wear petticoats.
However, California’s women are not all soiling their
neighbors’ reputation. Some of them arc busy over good
works, for which future generations may arise to call them
blessed. The American woman does not limit her ener-
gies to philanthropy and charitable chatter; she keeps
er eye on the beauties of the land which has reared her,
and preserves them from the utilitarian touch of man.
The big Sequoia trees, the redwoods which are the pride
of California and the admiration of the tourist, had been
felled by the commercial axe save for the exertions of the
women of the California Club, who moved heaven and
earth, and Washington into the bargain, to preserve the
pride of the forest. One youthful member of the club
was peculiarly clear and succinct when appealing for her
verdant proteges. “Itis a great deal worse than killing
a man,” quoth she ; “ to fell one of those trees would be
worse than killing many men. For one of them takes
centuries to grow, and in that time one gets, oh, dear 1
so many men." (N.B. —The lady is not married.) That
same man-despising club is now turning its attention to
Telegraph Hill, the most characteristic and picturesque
site in the city, on which the contractor has his levelling
eye and hand. But the contractor will not win his way,
for the California clubbists have marked him and doomed
him, and in this land the women “ have it.”
San Francisco.

If the Bushiand be thy Mother!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If the Bushland be thy mother, brother mine,
She will often cast a shadow on thy shine;
She will salt thy blood with bitterness to please a
wanton whim,
She will dash thy cup of gladness when it bubbles
at the brim.
If the Bushland be thy mother, brother mine,
There shall lurk a laughing devil in thy wine ;
He shall stir the soul within thee when the West
is waxing dim,
He shall mock the world around thee with a cynic
humor grim.
If the Bushland be thy mother, brother mine,
She will bronze thee, chin to forehead, for a sign ;
She will tie thy tongue, my brother, so thy speech
be rendered slow,
And the words of love shall fail thee though thy
bosom be aglow.
If the Bushland be thy mother, brother mine,
1 hou wilt find, beneath her merriment malign,
How her heart is full of tenderness for children
that she bore ;
Thou wilt find beneath her railing she is sweetness
at the core.
If the Bushland be thy mother, brother mine,
She will lap thee in her solitude divine ;
She will call her wanderer homeward when the
ways are wild and bare,
She will hush thee in her bosom till thou find a
solace there !
Q.
P. LUFTIG
Her Boy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She had married her husband from pity, and
thereafter continued to pay a good round price for
indulging this expensive sentiment. Fortune does
not always favour the brave. In this oase, she
seemed to think that an unwarrantable liberty
had been taken, and accordingly showed no
quarter. The woman paid.
The man had a University degree which ill-
health rendered useless to him, but he was able to
soliloquize over his misfortunes in three languages
while his wife earned the bread by mere muscle.
People said it was a dreadful shame to see him
marry so much beneath him. She was young and
strong when she grafted this parasitic life on her
own, and hope came easy. It was not easy after-
wards. She had only her muscles, and muscles
count for so little. She washed, ironed, and
scrubbed from dawn till dark at 4/- a day for six
days in the week. On Sunday she turned out her
own house, and attended to the arrears of washing,
cooking, and mending that had accumulated in her
absence. «
Still she never lost heart. As for her husband,
the worst that could be said about him was that
he accepted his position with equanimity. In her
early anti-nuptial days she had dreamed of a
holiday—just one day away from tubs and blue,
box-irons and starch, but this vague and delightful
vision faded into obscurity after the birth of her
boy. Practical people would, of course, regard this
as her second great mistake, but her hardest day
would never wrest such an acknowledgment from
her.
It seemed that now she began to live indeed.
Her husband was still the tender care of her life
but her boy was life itself. When she recovered
her strength, she found that Life spared her none
of the penalty. She had worked well before. She
worked almost superhumanly now. Her clients
showed their appreciation of her by recommending
her to others—never by paying her better. Her
reward for working well was working more.
Sometimes dawn found her standing amidst a
ghostly array of shirts hung by their sleeves over
a clothes-horse. But this was poor policy, as it
frequently entailed a day in bed with neuralgia.
Presently Nature showed her sense of injury by
bringing along the neuralgia without this excuse,
and constantly recurring physical pain ranged
itself among the facts of life. But she always had
her boy. Even when his natural childish screams
of healthy rage tried her overtaxed nerves to the
last point of endurance, she never lost her sense of
pride and joy in him.
She called him Arnold Eugene. Sometimes in his
play he overturned a shirt or two on the floor that
had such a slender chance of being swept. She
then pretended to smack him, but covered him
with kisses instead, and did the shirts over again.
From these demonstrations Arnold Eugene
gathered that tipping shirts over was a game that
was as suitable for his mother as himself.
As he grew older his requirements became more
extensive. Unfortunately the household purse
was not proportionately elastic. At length she
was obliged to admit that her earnings were
sufficient only for rent and food. The clothes for
the household were supplied from the cast-off
garments of her more charitable employers, and
re-adjusted to the family requirements when
husband and son slept. Through all this she
hugged the consolation of how it would end
some day, some golden day when her boy would
b 3 a man , a real man with arms that could work
for her. Through the steam of the wash-tub she
conjured up visions of his pouring his earnings
into her lap, and kissing her poor toil-worn hands
i ito rest. Rest! What a rest she would have
then ! She smiled luxuriously at the thought, as
she rubbed away.
But Arnold seemed to have inherited ill-luck.
He left school at 14, and drifted in and out of
various meagre situations. At 18 he was earning
15/- a week in a boot shop, and was to his mother’s
eyes the ideal of manly beauty. As a matter of
fact he was sft. 7i,n. and weedy, with elaborately-
curled black fringe and a smear of incipient
moustache. On Sunday he wore a hat with a gay
cord and tassel, and high-heeled boots. His
fascinations filled his mother with as fearful a joy
as though he had been a daughter. He did not
place her in any easy chair and pour silver into
her lap, but in what time he could spare from his
fringe and evening “ stroles ” (as he spelt it in his
notes), he was tolerably kind to her. so she was
still proud and happy, till she found one of the
a fore-mentioned notes in the pocket of a waistcoat
she was washing.
Dear flow, come for astrole this evening.—Yours truely,
. mold.
S he was anxious, but comforted herself with the
thought that there could be nothing in it. He
was so young. It transpired, however, that there
was a good deal m it.
The fatal attractions of Arnold Eugene had been
his undoing. \\ hen he mumbled his confession to
Ins mother—she had heard it first from the neigh-
bours—with sullen averted face, she sat mute as a
stone, ihe silence was intolerable to the young
man. He lifted his head defiantly. Then she
broke out: Arnold—my Arnold! ” and burst into a
passion of weeping. The commonness of it, after
these years of waiting !
j W made an impatient movement, and she
dried her eyes.
_ , Omy boy ! ” she said “it is not all your fault,
its because you’re so ’andsome, and things is
made easy for you. We’ll make it all right yet.”
then she proceeded to make it “all right”
Her boy would never be her support. That dream
was relinquished, hut he was her boy still, and no
woman should have the right to reproach him.
lhe girl was about as helpless, childish, and
generally rudimentary a specimen of the working
as could well be found. She had very
little idea in life beyond twisting up her colourless
tlaxen hair with wisps of newspaper—except
taking the wisps out again and smiling under the
thatch at the wearer of the high-heeled boots.
\ V i.,, , was a PProached on the subject by
Arnold s mother, (her own was indifferent) she
blandly acquiesced in everything. So the burden
or it all fell upon the woman who cared. She
hurried home from work to interview the clergy-
man, and finally, personally escorted the youthful
couple to the altar. Sho then provided them with
a ticket each for Westralia, where she managed
to secure a place for the boy. She gave the girl
the best part of her own scanty wardrobe, packed
the luggage for both and went to the wharf to see
them off. When the final moment came, the
agony of separation, which activity had kept
in the background, overcame her. She flung her
arms round him and choked for breath.
Arnold Eugene tipped his hat back from his
forehead. He wondered whether he would be
very sick on board. “ Good-bye, mother,” hesaid.
j"- n d Oh, mother ! ” —She lifted her head eagerly.
”ho could say what her heart hoped in that
second ? His eyes were roving absently.
“Yes, my boy.”—
He looked down at her condescendingly.
... Y °u won’t forget to keep up my insurance,
will you ? ” *
CECIL. WARREN.
Murphy Reminiscent.
[FOIR THE BULLETIN.]
“Now, how old would you tako me to be ? ” said
Paddy, tho old fencer.
“Anything,” I said, “between fifty and
eighty.”
“Well, lam just a few years younger than
George Thornton— Hon. George ho is now ; and I
might have been the Hon. Patrick, or Sir Patrick,
had I had my head built that way, and looked
after myself a bit.”
“George and I used to go hunting for five-
corners out Surry Hills way, and many a time
we’ve been ‘stuck up’—not by bushrangers, but
by great hulking fellows who were too lazy to
hunt for themselves. They used to clean us out
every time.” “No! I never could fight, but I
was a grand runner .”
“ It was I who found the couple that was buried
alive one night at the ‘Sand Hills,’ but that was
long before your time. It was a little dog of hers
that led me to the spot. Tho pair were sitting
beneath a bank of sand when it gave way and their
skeletons were found still in close embrace. My
dad had a big butcher’s shop, and used to supply
pretty well all the ships. I got as good an educa-
tion as could be got then, but it wasn’t compulsory
or free either.”
“No ! I didn’t go to Cape’s school. Why did
you think that ?”
“Yes! I knew Daniel Deniehy, and Dan
O Connor too.”
<l Poor Deniehy was an absent-minded chap—
always had a book in his hand. We lived up
William-street then, and I often used to see
‘Danny,’ but never played with him. Nobody
ever did, because he didn’t play. He always had
a book in his hand—even when he was on a
message for his mother, and he always had his face
right up against it.- He always went right past
the particular shop he wanted, and sometimes had
to walk a mile back. We kids used to wait for
him with a rope across the track, and trip him up
It didn’t rile him a bit. He’d just scramble for
his book and run on again with his face up against
it —must have been short-sighted. Clever ! well,
I can’t say, but he always was reading. Do you
know, I’m sometimes sorry for tripping the poor
fellow ! Yes ! I remember Phil. McCarroll the
butcher too. I used to write all the poetry for his
advertisements, but I haven’t done much at the
game since ; although, mind you, I believe I’m a
born poet.
“How did I become a bushman? Well, I
believe I started up Parramatta way chopping
down Geebung trees and ‘Hellfires.’ I always
wanted to be chopping at something, and I’ve
since had more than I wanted many a time.
George Washington and • Gladstone were great
choppers too. All great men are ! How long
since I’ve been to Sydney! Well, yesterday
thirty-eight years age I left it for Campbelltown
and haven’t seen it since—only in a picture. It
don’t know me, and I don’t know it. Besides, I
only know its big men, and I don’t suppose they’d
care to have the likes o’ me poking around with
them—not after ‘em mind.”'
“ Yes ; mine’s rum ? ”
YARRUM.
On its bill-heads Sydney Star sets down
its circulation at 42,199 daily. One of the direc-
tors being N.S.W. Colonial-Secretary See, it
couldn’t be expected that the paper would tell a
lie for the sake of a single copy.
As pointed out by a Sydney journey-
man baker in the press, the masters’ objection to
the appl.cation of the 8-hour system to the baking
trade is that they have been too long accustomed
to see their men toil from 12 to 16 hours out of
the 24. It is just as possible to adjust the
baking trade to the 8-hour limit as it is any other.
The president of Melb. Eight Hours’
Association made some wise fatherly remarks at
the annual Pioneers’ Dinner the other night. He
“warned the workers against allowing the time to
be cut up. Men could not start the day on a
snack, work four hours, and then get a meal and
be healthy. Had heard it said on last Eight
Hours’ Day—“ These men don’t look as strong
and robust as on previous processions. It was
the outcome of cutting the time which did not
allow the men to do justice to themselves or to
their employers.” Good old President! Man is
what he eats, but he is also how he eats.
